\
Dorcas was rather startled by the unexpected question.\
\
\'93No, sir.\'94\
\
\'93Are you quite sure?\'94\
\
\'93Oh, yes, sir.\'94\
\
\'93Has anyone else in the house got a green dress?\'94\
\
Dorcas reflected.\
\
\'93Miss Cynthia has a green evening dress.\'94\
\
\'93Light or dark green?\'94\
\
\'93A light green, sir; a sort of chiffon, they call it.\'94\
\
\'93Ah, that is not what I want. And nobody else has anything green?\'94\
\
\'93No, sir\'97not that I know of.\'94\
\
Poirot\'92s face did not betray a trace of whether he was disappointed or\
otherwise. He merely remarked:\
\
\'93Good, we will leave that and pass on. Have you any reason to believe\
that your mistress was likely to take a sleeping powder last night?\'94\
\
\'93Not _last_ night, sir, I know she didn\'92t.\'94\
\
\'93Why do you know so positively?\'94\
\
\'93Because the box was empty. She took the last one two days ago, and she\
didn\'92t have any more made up.\'94\
\
\'93You are quite sure of that?\'94\
\
\'93Positive, sir.\'94\
\
\'93Then that is cleared up! By the way, your mistress didn\'92t ask you to\
sign any paper yesterday?\'94\
\
\'93To sign a paper? No, sir.\'94\
\
\'93When Mr. Hastings and Mr. Lawrence came in yesterday evening, they\
found your mistress busy writing letters. I suppose you can give me no\
idea to whom these letters were addressed?\'94\
\
\'93I\'92m afraid I couldn\'92t, sir. I was out in the evening. Perhaps Annie\
could tell you, though she\'92s a careless girl. Never cleared the\
coffee-cups away last night. That\'92s what happens when I\'92m not here to\
look after things.\'94\
\
Poirot lifted his hand.\
\
\'93Since they have been left, Dorcas, leave them a little longer, I pray\
you. I should like to examine them.\'94\
\
\'93Very well, sir.\'94\
\
\'93What time did you go out last evening?\'94\
\
\'93About six o\'92clock, sir.\'94\
\
\'93Thank you, Dorcas, that is all I have to ask you.\'94 He rose and\
strolled to the window. \'93I have been admiring these flower beds. How\
many gardeners are employed here, by the way?\'94\
\
\'93Only three now, sir. Five, we had, before the war, when it was kept as\
a gentleman\'92s place should be. I wish you could have seen it then, sir.\
A fair sight it was. But now there\'92s only old Manning, and young\
William, and a new-fashioned woman gardener in breeches and such-like.\

\
\'93The good times will come again, Dorcas. At least, we hope so. Now,\

\
\'93Yes, sir. Thank you, sir.\'94\
\
\'93How did you know that Mrs. Inglethorp took sleeping powders?\'94 I asked,\
in lively curiosity, as Dorcas left the room. \'93And about the lost key\
and the duplicate?\'94\
\
\'93One thing at a time. As to the sleeping powders, I knew by this.\'94 He\
suddenly produced a small cardboard box, such as chemists use for\

\
\'93Where did you find it?\'94\
\
\'93In the wash-stand drawer in Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s bedroom. It was Number\
Six of my catalogue.\'94\
\
\'93But I suppose, as the last powder was taken two days ago, it is not of\
much importance?\'94\
\
\'93Probably not, but do you notice anything that strikes you as peculiar\
about this box?\'94\
\
I examined it closely.\
\
\'93No, I can\'92t say that I do.\'94\
\
\'93Look at the label.\'94\
\
I read the label carefully: \'93\'91One powder to be taken at bedtime, if\
required. Mrs. Inglethorp.\'92 No, I see nothing unusual.\'94\
\
\'93Not the fact that there is no chemist\'92s name?\'94\
\
\'93Ah!\'94 I exclaimed. \'93To be sure, that is odd!\'94\
\
\'93Have you ever known a chemist to send out a box like that, without his\
printed name?\'94\
\
\'93No, I can\'92t say that I have.\'94\
\
I was becoming quite excited, but Poirot damped my ardour by remarking:\
\
\'93Yet the explanation is quite simple. So do not intrigue yourself, my\
friend.\'94\
\
An audible creaking proclaimed the approach of Annie, so I had no time\
to reply.\
\
Annie was a fine, strapping girl, and was evidently labouring under\
intense excitement, mingled with a certain ghoulish enjoyment of the\
tragedy.\
\
Poirot came to the point at once, with a business-like briskness.\
\
\'93I sent for you, Annie, because I thought you might be able to tell me\

were there? And can you tell me any of the names and addresses?\'94\
\
Annie considered.\
\
\'93There were four letters, sir. One was to Miss Howard, and one was to\
Mr. Wells, the lawyer, and the other two I don\'92t think I remember,\
sir\'97oh, yes, one was to Ross\'92s, the caterers in Tadminster. The other\
one, I don\'92t remember.\'94\
\
\'93Think,\'94 urged Poirot.\
\
Annie racked her brains in vain.\
\
\'93I\'92m sorry, sir, but it\'92s clean gone. I don\'92t think I can have noticed\
it.\'94\
\
\'93It does not matter,\'94 said Poirot, not betraying any sign of\
disappointment. \'93Now I want to ask you about something else. There is a\

\
\'93Yes, sir, it was put in her room every evening, and she warmed it up\
in the night\'97whenever she fancied it.\'94\
\
\'93What was it? Plain cocoa?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, sir, made with milk, with a teaspoonful of sugar, and two\
teaspoonfuls of rum in it.\'94\
\
\'93Who took it to her room?\'94\
\
\'93I did, sir.\'94\
\
\'93Always?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, sir.\'94\
\
\'93At what time?\'94\
\
\'93When I went to draw the curtains, as a rule, sir.\'94\
\
\'93Did you bring it straight up from the kitchen then?\'94\
\
\'93No, sir, you see there\'92s not much room on the gas stove, so cook used\
to make it early, before putting the vegetables on for supper. Then I\
used to bring it up, and put it on the table by the swing door, and\
take it into her room later.\'94\
\
\'93The swing door is in the left wing, is it not?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, sir.\'94\
\
\'93And the table, is it on this side of the door, or on the\
farther\'97servants\'92 side?\'94\
\
\'93It\'92s this side, sir.\'94\
\
\'93What time did you bring it up last night?\'94\
\
\'93About quarter-past seven, I should say, sir.\'94\
\
\'93And when did you take it into Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s room?\'94\
\
\'93When I went to shut up, sir. About eight o\'92clock. Mrs. Inglethorp came\

\
\'93Then, between seven-fifteen and eight o\'92clock, the cocoa was standing\

\
\'93Yes, sir.\'94 Annie had been growing redder and redder in the face, and\
now she blurted out unexpectedly:\
\
\'93And if there _was_ salt in it, sir, it wasn\'92t me. I never took the\
salt near it.\'94\
\
\'93What makes you think there was salt in it?\'94 asked Poirot.\
\
\'93Seeing it on the tray, sir.\'94\
\
\'93You saw some salt on the tray?\'94\
\
\'93Yes. Coarse kitchen salt, it looked. I never noticed it when I took\
the tray up, but when I came to take it into the mistress\'92s room I saw\
it at once, and I suppose I ought to have taken it down again, and\
asked cook to make some fresh. But I was in a hurry, because Dorcas was\

had only gone on the tray. So I dusted it off with my apron, and took\
it in.\'94\
\
I had the utmost difficulty in controlling my excitement. Unknown to\
herself, Annie had provided us with an important piece of evidence. How\
she would have gaped if she had realized that her \'93coarse kitchen salt\'94\
was strychnine, one of the most deadly poisons known to mankind. I\
marvelled at Poirot\'92s calm. His self-control was astonishing. I awaited\
his next question with impatience, but it disappointed me.\
\
\'93When you went into Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s room, was the door leading into\
Miss Cynthia\'92s room bolted?\'94\
\
\'93Oh! Yes, sir; it always was. It had never been opened.\'94\
\
\'93And the door into Mr. Inglethorp\'92s room? Did you notice if that was\
bolted too?\'94\
\
Annie hesitated.\
\
\'93I couldn\'92t rightly say, sir; it was shut but I couldn\'92t say whether it\
was bolted or not.\'94\
\
\'93When you finally left the room, did Mrs. Inglethorp bolt the door\
after you?\'94\
\
\'93No, sir, not then, but I expect she did later. She usually did lock it\
at night. The door into the passage, that is.\'94\
\
\'93Did you notice any candle grease on the floor when you did the room\
yesterday?\'94\
\
\'93Candle grease? Oh, no, sir. Mrs. Inglethorp didn\'92t have a candle, only\
a reading-lamp.\'94\
\
\'93Then, if there had been a large patch of candle grease on the floor,\
you think you would have been sure to have seen it?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, sir, and I would have taken it out with a piece of blotting-paper\
and a hot iron.\'94\
\
Then Poirot repeated the question he had put to Dorcas:\
\
\'93Did your mistress ever have a green dress?\'94\
\
\'93No, sir.\'94\
\
\'93Nor a mantle, nor a cape, nor a\'97how do you call it?\'97a sports coat?\'94\
\
\'93Not green, sir.\'94\
\
\'93Nor anyone else in the house?\'94\
\
Annie reflected.\
\
\'93No, sir.\'94\
\
\'93You are sure of that?\'94\
\
\'93Quite sure.\'94\
\
\'93_Bien!_ That is all I want to know. Thank you very much.\'94\
\
With a nervous giggle, Annie took herself creakingly out of the room.\
My pent-up excitement burst forth.\
\
\'93Poirot,\'94 I cried, \'93I congratulate you! This is a great discovery.\'94\
\
\'93What is a great discovery?\'94\
\
\'93Why, that it was the cocoa and not the coffee that was poisoned. That\
explains everything! Of course it did not take effect until the early\
morning, since the cocoa was only drunk in the middle of the night.\'94\
\
\'93So you think that the cocoa\'97mark well what I say, Hastings, the\
_cocoa_\'97contained strychnine?\'94\
\
\'93Of course! That salt on the tray, what else could it have been?\'94\
\
\'93It might have been salt,\'94 replied Poirot placidly.\
\
I shrugged my shoulders. If he was going to take the matter that way,\
it was no good arguing with him. The idea crossed my mind, not for the\
first time, that poor old Poirot was growing old. Privately I thought\
it lucky that he had associated with him someone of a more receptive\
type of mind.\
\
Poirot was surveying me with quietly twinkling eyes.\
\
\'93You are not pleased with me, _mon ami?_\'94\
\
\'93My dear Poirot,\'94 I said coldly, \'93it is not for me to dictate to you.\
You have a right to your own opinion, just as I have to mine.\'94\
\
\'93A most admirable sentiment,\'94 remarked Poirot, rising briskly to his\
feet. \'93Now I have finished with this room. By the way, whose is the\

\
\'93Mr. Inglethorp\'92s.\'94\
\
\'93Ah!\'94 He tried the roll top tentatively. \'93Locked. But perhaps one of\
Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s keys would open it.\'94 He tried several, twisting and\
turning them with a practiced hand, and finally uttering an ejaculation\

pinch.\'94 He slid back the roll top, and ran a rapid eye over the neatly\
filed papers. To my surprise, he did not examine them, merely remarking\

this Mr. Inglethorp!\'94\
\
A \'93man of method\'94 was, in Poirot\'92s estimation, the highest praise that\
could be bestowed on any individual.\
\
I felt that my friend was not what he had been as he rambled on\
disconnectedly:\
\
\'93There were no stamps in his desk, but there might have been, eh, _mon\
ami?_ There might have been? Yes\'94\'97his eyes wandered round the\
room\'97\'93this boudoir has nothing more to tell us. It did not yield much.\
Only this.\'94\
\
He pulled a crumpled envelope out of his pocket, and tossed it over to\
me. It was rather a curious document. A plain, dirty looking old\
envelope with a few words scrawled across it, apparently at random. The\
following is a facsimile of it.\
\
\
\
\
       04\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
CHAPTER V. \'93IT ISN\'92T STRYCHNINE, IS IT?\'94\
\
\'93Where did you find this?\'94 I asked Poirot, in lively curiosity.\
\
\'93In the waste-paper basket. You recognise the handwriting?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, it is Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s. But what does it mean?\'94\
\
Poirot shrugged his shoulders.\
\
\'93I cannot say\'97but it is suggestive.\'94\
\
A wild idea flashed across me. Was it possible that Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s\
mind was deranged? Had she some fantastic idea of demoniacal\
possession? And, if that were so, was it not also possible that she\
might have taken her own life?\
\
I was about to expound these theories to Poirot, when his own words\
distracted me.\
\
\'93Come,\'94 he said, \'93now to examine the coffee-cups!\'94\
\
\'93My dear Poirot! What on earth is the good of that, now that we know\
about the cocoa?\'94\
\
\'93Oh, _l\'e0 l\'e0!_ That miserable cocoa!\'94 cried Poirot flippantly.\
\
He laughed with apparent enjoyment, raising his arms to heaven in mock\
despair, in what I could not but consider the worst possible taste.\
\
\'93And, anyway,\'94 I said, with increasing coldness, \'93as Mrs. Inglethorp\
took her coffee upstairs with her, I do not see what you expect to\
find, unless you consider it likely that we shall discover a packet of\
strychnine on the coffee tray!\'94\
\
Poirot was sobered at once.\
\
\'93Come, come, my friend,\'94 he said, slipping his arms through mine. \'93_Ne\
vous f\'e2chez pas!_ Allow me to interest myself in my coffee-cups, and I\
will respect your cocoa. There! Is it a bargain?\'94\
\
He was so quaintly humorous that I was forced to laugh; and we went\
together to the drawing-room, where the coffee-cups and tray remained\
undisturbed as we had left them.\
\
Poirot made me recapitulate the scene of the night before, listening\
very carefully, and verifying the position of the various cups.\
\
\'93So Mrs. Cavendish stood by the tray\'97and poured out. Yes. Then she came\
across to the window where you sat with Mademoiselle Cynthia. Yes. Here\
are the three cups. And the cup on the mantelpiece, half drunk, that\
would be Mr. Lawrence Cavendish\'92s. And the one on the tray?\'94\
\
\'93John Cavendish\'92s. I saw him put it down there.\'94\
\
\'93Good. One, two, three, four, five\'97but where, then, is the cup of Mr.\
Inglethorp?\'94\
\
\'93He does not take coffee.\'94\
\
\'93Then all are accounted for. One moment, my friend.\'94\
\
With infinite care, he took a drop or two from the grounds in each cup,\
sealing them up in separate test tubes, tasting each in turn as he did\
so. His physiognomy underwent a curious change. An expression gathered\
there that I can only describe as half puzzled, and half relieved.\
\
\'93_Bien!_\'94 he said at last. \'93It is evident! I had an idea\'97but clearly I\
was mistaken. Yes, altogether I was mistaken. Yet it is strange. But no\
matter!\'94\
\
And, with a characteristic shrug, he dismissed whatever it was that was\
worrying him from his mind. I could have told him from the beginning\
that this obsession of his over the coffee was bound to end in a blind\
alley, but I restrained my tongue. After all, though he was old, Poirot\
had been a great man in his day.\
\
\'93Breakfast is ready,\'94 said John Cavendish, coming in from the hall.\
\'93You will breakfast with us, Monsieur Poirot?\'94\
\
Poirot acquiesced. I observed John. Already he was almost restored to\
his normal self. The shock of the events of the last night had upset\
him temporarily, but his equable poise soon swung back to the normal.\
He was a man of very little imagination, in sharp contrast with his\
brother, who had, perhaps, too much.\
\
Ever since the early hours of the morning, John had been hard at work,\
sending telegrams\'97one of the first had gone to Evelyn Howard\'97writing\
notices for the papers, and generally occupying himself with the\
melancholy duties that a death entails.\
\
\'93May I ask how things are proceeding?\'94 he said. \'93Do your investigations\
point to my mother having died a natural death\'97or\'97or must we prepare\
ourselves for the worst?\'94\
\
\'93I think, Mr. Cavendish,\'94 said Poirot gravely, \'93that you would do well\
not to buoy yourself up with any false hopes. Can you tell me the views\
of the other members of the family?\'94\
\
\'93My brother Lawrence is convinced that we are making a fuss over\
nothing. He says that everything points to its being a simple case of\
heart failure.\'94\
\
\'93He does, does he? That is very interesting\'97very interesting,\'94 murmured\
Poirot softly. \'93And Mrs. Cavendish?\'94\
\
A faint cloud passed over John\'92s face.\
\
\'93I have not the least idea what my wife\'92s views on the subject are.\'94\
\
The answer brought a momentary stiffness in its train. John broke the\
rather awkward silence by saying with a slight effort:\
\
\'93I told you, didn\'92t I, that Mr. Inglethorp has returned?\'94\
\
Poirot bent his head.\
\
\'93It\'92s an awkward position for all of us. Of course one has to treat him\
as usual\'97but, hang it all, one\'92s gorge does rise at sitting down to eat\
with a possible murderer!\'94\
\
Poirot nodded sympathetically.\
\
\'93I quite understand. It is a very difficult situation for you, Mr.\
Cavendish. I would like to ask you one question. Mr. Inglethorp\'92s\
reason for not returning last night was, I believe, that he had\
forgotten the latch-key. Is not that so?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93I suppose you are quite sure that the latch-key _was_ forgotten\'97that\
he did not take it after all?\'94\
\
\'93I have no idea. I never thought of looking. We always keep it in the\
hall drawer. I\'92ll go and see if it\'92s there now.\'94\
\
Poirot held up his hand with a faint smile.\
\
\'93No, no, Mr. Cavendish, it is too late now. I am certain that you would\
find it. If Mr. Inglethorp did take it, he has had ample time to\
replace it by now.\'94\
\
\'93But do you think\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93I think nothing. If anyone had chanced to look this morning before his\
return, and seen it there, it would have been a valuable point in his\
favour. That is all.\'94\
\
John looked perplexed.\
\
\'93Do not worry,\'94 said Poirot smoothly. \'93I assure you that you need not\
let it trouble you. Since you are so kind, let us go and have some\
breakfast.\'94\
\
Everyone was assembled in the dining-room. Under the circumstances, we\
were naturally not a cheerful party. The reaction after a shock is\
always trying, and I think we were all suffering from it. Decorum and\
good breeding naturally enjoined that our demeanour should be much as\
usual, yet I could not help wondering if this self-control were really\
a matter of great difficulty. There were no red eyes, no signs of\
secretly indulged grief. I felt that I was right in my opinion that\
Dorcas was the person most affected by the personal side of the\
tragedy.\
\
I pass over Alfred Inglethorp, who acted the bereaved widower in a\
manner that I felt to be disgusting in its hypocrisy. Did he know that\
we suspected him, I wondered. Surely he could not be unaware of the\
fact, conceal it as we would. Did he feel some secret stirring of fear,\
or was he confident that his crime would go unpunished? Surely the\
suspicion in the atmosphere must warn him that he was already a marked\
man.\
\
But did everyone suspect him? What about Mrs. Cavendish? I watched her\
as she sat at the head of the table, graceful, composed, enigmatic. In\
her soft grey frock, with white ruffles at the wrists falling over her\
slender hands, she looked very beautiful. When she chose, however, her\
face could be sphinx-like in its inscrutability. She was very silent,\
hardly opening her lips, and yet in some queer way I felt that the\
great strength of her personality was dominating us all.\
\
And little Cynthia? Did she suspect? She looked very tired and ill, I\
thought. The heaviness and languor of her manner were very marked. I\
asked her if she were feeling ill, and she answered frankly:\
\
\'93Yes, I\'92ve got the most beastly headache.\'94\
\
\'93Have another cup of coffee, mademoiselle?\'94 said Poirot solicitously.\
\'93It will revive you. It is unparalleled for the _mal de t\'eate_.\'94 He\
jumped up and took her cup.\
\
\'93No sugar,\'94 said Cynthia, watching him, as he picked up the\
sugar-tongs.\
\
\'93No sugar? You abandon it in the war-time, eh?\'94\
\
\'93No, I never take it in coffee.\'94\
\
\'93_Sacr\'e9!_\'94 murmured Poirot to himself, as he brought back the\
replenished cup.\
\
Only I heard him, and glancing up curiously at the little man I saw\
that his face was working with suppressed excitement, and his eyes were\
as green as a cat\'92s. He had heard or seen something that had affected\
him strongly\'97but what was it? I do not usually label myself as dense,\
but I must confess that nothing out of the ordinary had attracted _my_\
attention.\
\
In another moment, the door opened and Dorcas appeared.\
\
\'93Mr. Wells to see you, sir,\'94 she said to John.\
\
I remembered the name as being that of the lawyer to whom Mrs.\
Inglethorp had written the night before.\
\
John rose immediately.\
\
\'93Show him into my study.\'94 Then he turned to us. \'93My mother\'92s lawyer,\'94\
he explained. And in a lower voice: \'93He is also Coroner\'97you understand.\
Perhaps you would like to come with me?\'94\
\
We acquiesced and followed him out of the room. John strode on ahead\
and I took the opportunity of whispering to Poirot:\
\
\'93There will be an inquest then?\'94\
\
Poirot nodded absently. He seemed absorbed in thought; so much so that\
my curiosity was aroused.\
\
\'93What is it? You are not attending to what I say.\'94\
\
\'93It is true, my friend. I am much worried.\'94\
\
\'93Why?\'94\
\
\'93Because Mademoiselle Cynthia does not take sugar in her coffee.\'94\
\
\'93What? You cannot be serious?\'94\
\
\'93But I am most serious. Ah, there is something there that I do not\
understand. My instinct was right.\'94\
\
\'93What instinct?\'94\
\
\'93The instinct that led me to insist on examining those coffee-cups.\
_Chut!_ no more now!\'94\
\
We followed John into his study, and he closed the door behind us.\
\
Mr. Wells was a pleasant man of middle-age, with keen eyes, and the\
typical lawyer\'92s mouth. John introduced us both, and explained the\
reason of our presence.\
\
\'93You will understand, Wells,\'94 he added, \'93that this is all strictly\
private. We are still hoping that there will turn out to be no need for\
investigation of any kind.\'94\
\
\'93Quite so, quite so,\'94 said Mr. Wells soothingly. \'93I wish we could have\
spared you the pain and publicity of an inquest, but of course it\'92s\
quite unavoidable in the absence of a doctor\'92s certificate.\'94\

\'93Yes, I suppose so.\'94\
\
\'93Clever man, Bauerstein. Great authority on toxicology, I believe.\'94\
\
\'93Indeed,\'94 said John with a certain stiffness in his manner. Then he\
added rather hesitatingly: \'93Shall we have to appear as witnesses\'97all of\

\
\'93You, of course\'97and ah\'97er\'97Mr.\'97er\'97Inglethorp.\'94\
\
A slight pause ensued before the lawyer went on in his soothing manner:\
\
\'93Any other evidence will be simply confirmatory, a mere matter of\
form.\'94\
\
\'93I see.\'94\
\
A faint expression of relief swept over John\'92s face. It puzzled me, for\
I saw no occasion for it.\
\
\'93If you know of nothing to the contrary,\'94 pursued Mr. Wells, \'93I had\
thought of Friday. That will give us plenty of time for the doctor\'92s\
report. The post-mortem is to take place to-night, I believe?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93Then that arrangement will suit you?\'94\
\
\'93Perfectly.\'94\
\
\'93I need not tell you, my dear Cavendish, how distressed I am at this\
most tragic affair.\'94\
\
\'93Can you give us no help in solving it, monsieur?\'94 interposed Poirot,\
speaking for the first time since we had entered the room.\
\
\'93I?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, we heard that Mrs. Inglethorp wrote to you last night. You should\
have received the letter this morning.\'94\
\
\'93I did, but it contains no information. It is merely a note asking me\
to call upon her this morning, as she wanted my advice on a matter of\
great importance.\'94\
\
\'93She gave you no hint as to what that matter might be?\'94\
\
\'93Unfortunately, no.\'94\
\
\'93That is a pity,\'94 said John.\
\
\'93A great pity,\'94 agreed Poirot gravely.\
\
There was silence. Poirot remained lost in thought for a few minutes.\
Finally he turned to the lawyer again.\
\
\'93Mr. Wells, there is one thing I should like to ask you\'97that is, if it\
is not against professional etiquette. In the event of Mrs.\
Inglethorp\'92s death, who would inherit her money?\'94\
\
The lawyer hesitated a moment, and then replied:\
\
\'93The knowledge will be public property very soon, so if Mr. Cavendish\
does not object\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93Not at all,\'94 interpolated John.\
\
\'93I do not see any reason why I should not answer your question. By her\
last will, dated August of last year, after various unimportant\

Mr. John Cavendish.\'94\
\
\'93Was not that\'97pardon the question, Mr. Cavendish\'97rather unfair to her\
other stepson, Mr. Lawrence Cavendish?\'94\
\
\'93No, I do not think so. You see, under the terms of their father\'92s\

\
Poirot nodded thoughtfully.\
\
\'93I see. But I am right in saying, am I not, that by your English law\
that will was automatically revoked when Mrs. Inglethorp remarried?\'94\
\
Mr. Wells bowed his head.\
\
\'93As I was about to proceed, Monsieur Poirot, that document is now null\
and void.\'94\
\
\'93_Hein!_\'94 said Poirot. He reflected for a moment, and then asked: \'93Was\
Mrs. Inglethorp herself aware of that fact?\'94\
\
\'93I do not know. She may have been.\'94\
\
\'93She was,\'94 said John unexpectedly. \'93We were discussing the matter of\
wills being revoked by marriage only yesterday.\'94\
\
\'93Ah! One more question, Mr. Wells. You say \'91her last will.\'92 Had Mrs.\
Inglethorp, then, made several former wills?\'94\
\
\'93On an average, she made a new will at least once a year,\'94 said Mr.\
Wells imperturbably. \'93She was given to changing her mind as to her\
testamentary dispositions, now benefiting one, now another member of\
her family.\'94\
\
\'93Suppose,\'94 suggested Poirot, \'93that, unknown to you, she had made a new\
will in favour of someone who was not, in any sense of the word, a\

surprised?\'94\
\
\'93Not in the least.\'94\
\
\'93Ah!\'94 Poirot seemed to have exhausted his questions.\
\
I drew close to him, while John and the lawyer were debating the\
question of going through Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s papers.\
\
\'93Do you think Mrs. Inglethorp made a will leaving all her money to Miss\
Howard?\'94 I asked in a low voice, with some curiosity.\
\
Poirot smiled.\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
\'93Then why did you ask?\'94\
\
\'93Hush!\'94\
\
John Cavendish had turned to Poirot.\
\
\'93Will you come with us, Monsieur Poirot? We are going through my\
mother\'92s papers. Mr. Inglethorp is quite willing to leave it entirely\
to Mr. Wells and myself.\'94\
\
\'93Which simplifies matters very much,\'94 murmured the lawyer. \'93As\
technically, of course, he was entitled\'97\'97\'94 He did not finish the\
sentence.\
\
\'93We will look through the desk in the boudoir first,\'94 explained John,\
\'93and go up to her bedroom afterwards. She kept her most important\
papers in a purple despatch-case, which we must look through\
carefully.\'94\
\
\'93Yes,\'94 said the lawyer, \'93it is quite possible that there may be a later\
will than the one in my possession.\'94\
\
\'93There _is_ a later will.\'94 It was Poirot who spoke.\
\
\'93What?\'94 John and the lawyer looked at him startled.\
\
\'93Or, rather,\'94 pursued my friend imperturbably, \'93there _was_ one.\'94\
\
\'93What do you mean\'97there was one? Where is it now?\'94\
\
\'93Burnt!\'94\
\
\'93Burnt?\'94\
\
\'93Yes. See here.\'94 He took out the charred fragment we had found in the\
grate in Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s room, and handed it to the lawyer with a\
brief explanation of when and where he had found it.\
\
\'93But possibly this is an old will?\'94\
\
\'93I do not think so. In fact I am almost certain that it was made no\
earlier than yesterday afternoon.\'94\
\
\'93What?\'94 \'93Impossible!\'94 broke simultaneously from both men.\
\
Poirot turned to John.\
\
\'93If you will allow me to send for your gardener, I will prove it to\
you.\'94\
\
\'93Oh, of course\'97but I don\'92t see\'97\'97\'94\
\
Poirot raised his hand.\
\
\'93Do as I ask you. Afterwards you shall question as much as you please.\'94\
\
\'93Very well.\'94 He rang the bell.\
\
Dorcas answered it in due course.\
\
\'93Dorcas, will you tell Manning to come round and speak to me here.\'94\
\
\'93Yes, sir.\'94\
\
Dorcas withdrew.\
\
We waited in a tense silence. Poirot alone seemed perfectly at his\
ease, and dusted a forgotten corner of the bookcase.\
\
The clumping of hobnailed boots on the gravel outside proclaimed the\
approach of Manning. John looked questioningly at Poirot. The latter\
nodded.\
\
\'93Come inside, Manning,\'94 said John, \'93I want to speak to you.\'94\
\
Manning came slowly and hesitatingly through the French window, and\
stood as near it as he could. He held his cap in his hands, twisting it\
very carefully round and round. His back was much bent, though he was\
probably not as old as he looked, but his eyes were sharp and\
intelligent, and belied his slow and rather cautious speech.\
\
\'93Manning,\'94 said John, \'93this gentleman will put some questions to you\
which I want you to answer.\'94\
\
\'93Yessir,\'94 mumbled Manning.\
\
Poirot stepped forward briskly. Manning\'92s eye swept over him with a\
faint contempt.\
\
\'93You were planting a bed of begonias round by the south side of the\
house yesterday afternoon, were you not, Manning?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, sir, me and Willum.\'94\
\
\'93And Mrs. Inglethorp came to the window and called you, did she not?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, sir, she did.\'94\
\
\'93Tell me in your own words exactly what happened after that.\'94\
\
\'93Well, sir, nothing much. She just told Willum to go on his bicycle\
down to the village, and bring back a form of will, or such-like\'97I\
don\'92t know what exactly\'97she wrote it down for him.\'94\
\
\'93Well?\'94\
\
\'93Well, he did, sir.\'94\
\
\'93And what happened next?\'94\
\
\'93We went on with the begonias, sir.\'94\
\
\'93Did not Mrs. Inglethorp call you again?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, sir, both me and Willum, she called.\'94\
\
\'93And then?\'94\
\
\'93She made us come right in, and sign our names at the bottom of a long\
paper\'97under where she\'92d signed.\'94\
\
\'93Did you see anything of what was written above her signature?\'94 asked\
Poirot sharply.\
\
\'93No, sir, there was a bit of blotting paper over that part.\'94\
\
\'93And you signed where she told you?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, sir, first me and then Willum.\'94\
\
\'93What did she do with it afterwards?\'94\
\
\'93Well, sir, she slipped it into a long envelope, and put it inside a\
sort of purple box that was standing on the desk.\'94\
\
\'93What time was it when she first called you?\'94\
\
\'93About four, I should say, sir.\'94\
\
\'93Not earlier? Couldn\'92t it have been about half-past three?\'94\
\
\'93No, I shouldn\'92t say so, sir. It would be more likely to be a bit after\
four\'97not before it.\'94\
\
\'93Thank you, Manning, that will do,\'94 said Poirot pleasantly.\
\
The gardener glanced at his master, who nodded, whereupon Manning\
lifted a finger to his forehead with a low mumble, and backed\
cautiously out of the window.\
\
We all looked at each other.\
\
\'93Good heavens!\'94 murmured John. \'93What an extraordinary coincidence.\'94\
\
\'93How\'97a coincidence?\'94\
\
\'93That my mother should have made a will on the very day of her death!\'94\
\
Mr. Wells cleared his throat and remarked drily:\
\
\'93Are you so sure it is a coincidence, Cavendish?\'94\
\
\'93What do you mean?\'94\
\
\'93Your mother, you tell me, had a violent quarrel with\'97someone yesterday\
afternoon\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93What do you mean?\'94 cried John again. There was a tremor in his voice,\
and he had gone very pale.\
\
\'93In consequence of that quarrel, your mother very suddenly and\
hurriedly makes a new will. The contents of that will we shall never\
know. She told no one of its provisions. This morning, no doubt, she\
would have consulted me on the subject\'97but she had no chance. The will\
disappears, and she takes its secret with her to her grave. Cavendish,\
I much fear there is no coincidence there. Monsieur Poirot, I am sure\
you agree with me that the facts are very suggestive.\'94\
\
\'93Suggestive, or not,\'94 interrupted John, \'93we are most grateful to\
Monsieur Poirot for elucidating the matter. But for him, we should\
never have known of this will. I suppose, I may not ask you, monsieur,\
what first led you to suspect the fact?\'94\
\
Poirot smiled and answered:\
\
\'93A scribbled over old envelope, and a freshly planted bed of begonias.\'94\
\
John, I think, would have pressed his questions further, but at that\
moment the loud purr of a motor was audible, and we all turned to the\
window as it swept past.\
\
\'93Evie!\'94 cried John. \'93Excuse me, Wells.\'94 He went hurriedly out into the\
hall.\
\
Poirot looked inquiringly at me.\
\
\'93Miss Howard,\'94 I explained.\
\
\'93Ah, I am glad she has come. There is a woman with a head and a heart\
too, Hastings. Though the good God gave her no beauty!\'94\
\
I followed John\'92s example, and went out into the hall, where Miss\
Howard was endeavouring to extricate herself from the voluminous mass\
of veils that enveloped her head. As her eyes fell on me, a sudden pang\
of guilt shot through me. This was the woman who had warned me so\
earnestly, and to whose warning I had, alas, paid no heed! How soon,\
and how contemptuously, I had dismissed it from my mind. Now that she\
had been proved justified in so tragic a manner, I felt ashamed. She\
had known Alfred Inglethorp only too well. I wondered whether, if she\
had remained at Styles, the tragedy would have taken place, or would\
the man have feared her watchful eyes?\
\
I was relieved when she shook me by the hand, with her well remembered\
painful grip. The eyes that met mine were sad, but not reproachful;\
that she had been crying bitterly, I could tell by the redness of her\
eyelids, but her manner was unchanged from its old gruffness.\
\
\'93Started the moment I got the wire. Just come off night duty. Hired\
car. Quickest way to get here.\'94\
\
\'93Have you had anything to eat this morning, Evie?\'94 asked John.\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
\'93I thought not. Come along, breakfast\'92s not cleared away yet, and\
they\'92ll make you some fresh tea.\'94 He turned to me. \'93Look after her,\
Hastings, will you? Wells is waiting for me. Oh, here\'92s Monsieur\
Poirot. He\'92s helping us, you know, Evie.\'94\
\
Miss Howard shook hands with Poirot, but glanced suspiciously over her\
shoulder at John.\
\
\'93What do you mean\'97helping us?\'94\
\
\'93Helping us to investigate.\'94\
\
\'93Nothing to investigate. Have they taken him to prison yet?\'94\
\
\'93Taken who to prison?\'94\
\
\'93Who? Alfred Inglethorp, of course!\'94\
\
\'93My dear Evie, do be careful. Lawrence is of the opinion that my mother\
died from heart seizure.\'94\
\
\'93More fool, Lawrence!\'94 retorted Miss Howard. \'93Of course Alfred\
Inglethorp murdered poor Emily\'97as I always told you he would.\'94\
\
\'93My dear Evie, don\'92t shout so. Whatever we may think or suspect, it is\
better to say as little as possible for the present. The inquest isn\'92t\
until Friday.\'94\
\
\'93Not until fiddlesticks!\'94 The snort Miss Howard gave was truly\
magnificent. \'93You\'92re all off your heads. The man will be out of the\
country by then. If he\'92s any sense, he won\'92t stay here tamely and wait\
to be hanged.\'94\
\
John Cavendish looked at her helplessly.\
\
\'93I know what it is,\'94 she accused him, \'93you\'92ve been listening to the\
doctors. Never should. What do they know? Nothing at all\'97or just enough\
to make them dangerous. I ought to know\'97my own father was a doctor.\
That little Wilkins is about the greatest fool that even I have ever\
seen. Heart seizure! Sort of thing he would say. Anyone with any sense\
could see at once that her husband had poisoned her. I always said he\'92d\
murder her in her bed, poor soul. Now he\'92s done it. And all you can do\

Friday.\'92 You ought to be ashamed of yourself, John Cavendish.\'94\
\
\'93What do you want me to do?\'94 asked John, unable to help a faint smile.\
\'93Dash it all, Evie, I can\'92t haul him down to the local police station\
by the scruff of his neck.\'94\
\
\'93Well, you might do something. Find out how he did it. He\'92s a crafty\
beggar. Dare say he soaked fly papers. Ask cook if she\'92s missed any.\'94\
\
It occurred to me very forcibly at that moment that to harbour Miss\
Howard and Alfred Inglethorp under the same roof, and keep the peace\
between them, was likely to prove a Herculean task, and I did not envy\
John. I could see by the expression of his face that he fully\
appreciated the difficulty of the position. For the moment, he sought\
refuge in retreat, and left the room precipitately.\
\
Dorcas brought in fresh tea. As she left the room, Poirot came over\
from the window where he had been standing, and sat down facing Miss\
Howard.\
\
\'93Mademoiselle,\'94 he said gravely, \'93I want to ask you something.\'94\
\
\'93Ask away,\'94 said the lady, eyeing him with some disfavour.\
\
\'93I want to be able to count upon your help.\'94\
\
\'93I\'92ll help you to hang Alfred with pleasure,\'94 she replied gruffly.\
\'93Hanging\'92s too good for him. Ought to be drawn and quartered, like in\
good old times.\'94\
\
\'93We are at one then,\'94 said Poirot, \'93for I, too, want to hang the\
criminal.\'94\
\
\'93Alfred Inglethorp?\'94\
\
\'93Him, or another.\'94\
\
\'93No question of another. Poor Emily was never murdered until _he_ came\
along. I don\'92t say she wasn\'92t surrounded by sharks\'97she was. But it was\
only her purse they were after. Her life was safe enough. But along\
comes Mr. Alfred Inglethorp\'97and within two months\'97hey presto!\'94\
\
\'93Believe me, Miss Howard,\'94 said Poirot very earnestly, \'93if Mr.\
Inglethorp is the man, he shall not escape me. On my honour, I will\
hang him as high as Haman!\'94\
\
\'93That\'92s better,\'94 said Miss Howard more enthusiastically.\
\
\'93But I must ask you to trust me. Now your help may be very valuable to\
me. I will tell you why. Because, in all this house of mourning, yours\
are the only eyes that have wept.\'94\
\
Miss Howard blinked, and a new note crept into the gruffness of her\
voice.\
\
\'93If you mean that I was fond of her\'97yes, I was. You know, Emily was a\
selfish old woman in her way. She was very generous, but she always\
wanted a return. She never let people forget what she had done for\
them\'97and, that way she missed love. Don\'92t think she ever realized it,\
though, or felt the lack of it. Hope not, anyway. I was on a different\
footing. I took my stand from the first. \'91So many pounds a year I\'92m\
worth to you. Well and good. But not a penny piece besides\'97not a pair\
of gloves, nor a theatre ticket.\'92 She didn\'92t understand\'97was very\
offended sometimes. Said I was foolishly proud. It wasn\'92t that\'97but I\
couldn\'92t explain. Anyway, I kept my self-respect. And so, out of the\
whole bunch, I was the only one who could allow myself to be fond of\
her. I watched over her. I guarded her from the lot of them, and then a\
glib-tongued scoundrel comes along, and pooh! all my years of devotion\
go for nothing.\'94\
\
Poirot nodded sympathetically.\
\
\'93I understand, mademoiselle, I understand all you feel. It is most\
natural. You think that we are lukewarm\'97that we lack fire and\
energy\'97but trust me, it is not so.\'94\
\
John stuck his head in at this juncture, and invited us both to come up\
to Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s room, as he and Mr. Wells had finished looking\
through the desk in the boudoir.\
\
As we went up the stairs, John looked back to the dining-room door, and\
lowered his voice confidentially:\
\
\'93Look here, what\'92s going to happen when these two meet?\'94\
\
I shook my head helplessly.\
\
\'93I\'92ve told Mary to keep them apart if she can.\'94\
\
\'93Will she be able to do so?\'94\
\
\'93The Lord only knows. There\'92s one thing, Inglethorp himself won\'92t be\
too keen on meeting her.\'94\
\
\'93You\'92ve got the keys still, haven\'92t you, Poirot?\'94 I asked, as we\
reached the door of the locked room.\
\
Taking the keys from Poirot, John unlocked it, and we all passed in.\
The lawyer went straight to the desk, and John followed him.\
\
\'93My mother kept most of her important papers in this despatch-case, I\
believe,\'94 he said.\
\
Poirot drew out the small bunch of keys.\
\
\'93Permit me. I locked it, out of precaution, this morning.\'94\
\
\'93But it\'92s not locked now.\'94\
\
\'93Impossible!\'94\
\
\'93See.\'94 And John lifted the lid as he spoke.\
\
\'93_Milles tonnerres!_\'94 cried Poirot, dumbfounded. \'93And I\'97who have both\
the keys in my pocket!\'94 He flung himself upon the case. Suddenly he\
stiffened. \'93_Eh voil\'e0 une affaire!_ This lock has been forced.\'94\
\
\'93What?\'94\
\
Poirot laid down the case again.\
\
\'93But who forced it? Why should they? When? But the door was locked?\'94\
These exclamations burst from us disjointedly.\
\
Poirot answered them categorically\'97almost mechanically.\
\
\'93Who? That is the question. Why? Ah, if I only knew. When? Since I was\
here an hour ago. As to the door being locked, it is a very ordinary\
lock. Probably any other of the doorkeys in this passage would fit it.\'94\
\
We stared at one another blankly. Poirot had walked over to the\
mantelpiece. He was outwardly calm, but I noticed his hands, which from\
long force of habit were mechanically straightening the spill vases on\
the mantelpiece, were shaking violently.\
\
\'93See here, it was like this,\'94 he said at last. \'93There was something in\
that case\'97some piece of evidence, slight in itself perhaps, but still\
enough of a clue to connect the murderer with the crime. It was vital\
to him that it should be destroyed before it was discovered and its\
significance appreciated. Therefore, he took the risk, the great risk,\
of coming in here. Finding the case locked, he was obliged to force it,\
thus betraying his presence. For him to take that risk, it must have\
been something of great importance.\'94\
\
\'93But what was it?\'94\
\
\'93Ah!\'94 cried Poirot, with a gesture of anger. \'93That, I do not know! A\
document of some kind, without doubt, possibly the scrap of paper\
Dorcas saw in her hand yesterday afternoon. And I\'97\'94 his anger burst\
forth freely\'97\'93miserable animal that I am! I guessed nothing! I have\
behaved like an imbecile! I should never have left that case here. I\
should have carried it away with me. Ah, triple pig! And now it is\
gone. It is destroyed\'97but is it destroyed? Is there not yet a chance\'97we\
must leave no stone unturned\'97\'94\
\
He rushed like a madman from the room, and I followed him as soon as I\
had sufficiently recovered my wits. But, by the time I had reached the\
top of the stairs, he was out of sight.\
\
Mary Cavendish was standing where the staircase branched, staring down\
into the hall in the direction in which he had disappeared.\
\
\'93What has happened to your extraordinary little friend, Mr. Hastings?\
He has just rushed past me like a mad bull.\'94\
\
\'93He\'92s rather upset about something,\'94 I remarked feebly. I really did\
not know how much Poirot would wish me to disclose. As I saw a faint\
smile gather on Mrs. Cavendish\'92s expressive mouth, I endeavoured to try\
and turn the conversation by saying: \'93They haven\'92t met yet, have they?\'94\
\
\'93Who?\'94\
\
\'93Mr. Inglethorp and Miss Howard.\'94\
\
She looked at me in rather a disconcerting manner.\
\
\'93Do you think it would be such a disaster if they did meet?\'94\
\
\'93Well, don\'92t you?\'94 I said, rather taken aback.\
\
\'93No.\'94 She was smiling in her quiet way. \'93I should like to see a good\
flare up. It would clear the air. At present we are all thinking so\
much, and saying so little.\'94\
\
\'93John doesn\'92t think so,\'94 I remarked. \'93He\'92s anxious to keep them apart.\'94\
\
\'93Oh, John!\'94\
\
Something in her tone fired me, and I blurted out:\
\
\'93Old John\'92s an awfully good sort.\'94\
\
She studied me curiously for a minute or two, and then said, to my\
great surprise:\
\
\'93You are loyal to your friend. I like you for that.\'94\
\
\'93Aren\'92t you my friend too?\'94\
\
\'93I am a very bad friend.\'94\
\
\'93Why do you say that?\'94\
\
\'93Because it is true. I am charming to my friends one day, and forget\
all about them the next.\'94\
\
I don\'92t know what impelled me, but I was nettled, and I said foolishly\
and not in the best of taste:\
\
\'93Yet you seem to be invariably charming to Dr. Bauerstein!\'94\
\
Instantly I regretted my words. Her face stiffened. I had the\
impression of a steel curtain coming down and blotting out the real\
woman. Without a word, she turned and went swiftly up the stairs,\
whilst I stood like an idiot gaping after her.\
\
I was recalled to other matters by a frightful row going on below. I\
could hear Poirot shouting and expounding. I was vexed to think that my\
diplomacy had been in vain. The little man appeared to be taking the\
whole house into his confidence, a proceeding of which I, for one,\
doubted the wisdom. Once again I could not help regretting that my\
friend was so prone to lose his head in moments of excitement. I\
stepped briskly down the stairs. The sight of me calmed Poirot almost\
immediately. I drew him aside.\
\
\'93My dear fellow,\'94 I said, \'93is this wise? Surely you don\'92t want the\
whole house to know of this occurrence? You are actually playing into\
the criminal\'92s hands.\'94\
\
\'93You think so, Hastings?\'94\
\
\'93I am sure of it.\'94\
\
\'93Well, well, my friend, I will be guided by you.\'94\
\
\'93Good. Although, unfortunately, it is a little too late now.\'94\
\
\'93Sure.\'94\
\
He looked so crestfallen and abashed that I felt quite sorry, though I\
still thought my rebuke a just and wise one.\
\
\'93Well,\'94 he said at last, \'93let us go, _mon ami_.\'94\
\
\'93You have finished here?\'94\
\
\'93For the moment, yes. You will walk back with me to the village?\'94\
\
\'93Willingly.\'94\
\
He picked up his little suit-case, and we went out through the open\
window in the drawing-room. Cynthia Murdoch was just coming in, and\
Poirot stood aside to let her pass.\
\
\'93Excuse me, mademoiselle, one minute.\'94\
\
\'93Yes?\'94 she turned inquiringly.\
\
\'93Did you ever make up Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s medicines?\'94\
\
A slight flush rose in her face, as she answered rather constrainedly:\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
\'93Only her powders?\'94\
\
The flush deepened as Cynthia replied:\
\
\'93Oh, yes, I did make up some sleeping powders for her once.\'94\
\
\'93These?\'94\
\
Poirot produced the empty box which had contained powders.\
\
She nodded.\
\
\'93Can you tell me what they were? Sulphonal? Veronal?\'94\
\
\'93No, they were bromide powders.\'94\
\
\'93Ah! Thank you, mademoiselle; good morning.\'94\
\
As we walked briskly away from the house, I glanced at him more than\
once. I had often before noticed that, if anything excited him, his\
eyes turned green like a cat\'92s. They were shining like emeralds now.\
\
\'93My friend,\'94 he broke out at last, \'93I have a little idea, a very\
strange, and probably utterly impossible idea. And yet\'97it fits in.\'94\
\
I shrugged my shoulders. I privately thought that Poirot was rather too\
much given to these fantastic ideas. In this case, surely, the truth\
was only too plain and apparent.\
\
\'93So that is the explanation of the blank label on the box,\'94 I remarked.\
\'93Very simple, as you said. I really wonder that I did not think of it\
myself.\'94\
\
Poirot did not appear to be listening to me.\
\
\'93They have made one more discovery, _l\'e0-bas_,\'94 he observed, jerking his\
thumb over his shoulder in the direction of Styles. \'93Mr. Wells told me\
as we were going upstairs.\'94\
\
\'93What was it?\'94\
\
\'93Locked up in the desk in the boudoir, they found a will of Mrs.\
Inglethorp\'92s, dated before her marriage, leaving her fortune to Alfred\
Inglethorp. It must have been made just at the time they were engaged.\
It came quite as a surprise to Wells\'97and to John Cavendish also. It was\
written on one of those printed will forms, and witnessed by two of the\
servants\'97not Dorcas.\'94\
\
\'93Did Mr. Inglethorp know of it?\'94\
\
\'93He says not.\'94\
\
\'93One might take that with a grain of salt,\'94 I remarked sceptically.\
\'93All these wills are very confusing. Tell me, how did those scribbled\
words on the envelope help you to discover that a will was made\
yesterday afternoon?\'94\
\
Poirot smiled.\
\
\'93_Mon ami_, have you ever, when writing a letter, been arrested by the\
fact that you did not know how to spell a certain word?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, often. I suppose everyone has.\'94\
\
\'93Exactly. And have you not, in such a case, tried the word once or\
twice on the edge of the blotting-paper, or a spare scrap of paper, to\
see if it looked right? Well, that is what Mrs. Inglethorp did. You\
will notice that the word \'91possessed\'92 is spelt first with one \'91s\'92 and\
subsequently with two\'97correctly. To make sure, she had further tried it\
in a sentence, thus: \'91I am possessed.\'92 Now, what did that tell me? It\
told me that Mrs. Inglethorp had been writing the word \'91possessed\'92 that\
afternoon, and, having the fragment of paper found in the grate fresh\
in my mind, the possibility of a will\'97(a document almost certain to\
contain that word)\'97occurred to me at once. This possibility was\
confirmed by a further circumstance. In the general confusion, the\
boudoir had not been swept that morning, and near the desk were several\
traces of brown mould and earth. The weather had been perfectly fine\
for some days, and no ordinary boots would have left such a heavy\
deposit.\
\
\'93I strolled to the window, and saw at once that the begonia beds had\
been newly planted. The mould in the beds was exactly similar to that\
on the floor of the boudoir, and also I learnt from you that they _had_\
been planted yesterday afternoon. I was now sure that one, or possibly\
both of the gardeners\'97for there were two sets of footprints in the\
bed\'97had entered the boudoir, for if Mrs. Inglethorp had merely wished\
to speak to them she would in all probability have stood at the window,\
and they would not have come into the room at all. I was now quite\
convinced that she had made a fresh will, and had called the two\
gardeners in to witness her signature. Events proved that I was right\
in my supposition.\'94\
\
\'93That was very ingenious,\'94 I could not help admitting. \'93I must confess\
that the conclusions I drew from those few scribbled words were quite\
erroneous.\'94\
\
He smiled.\
\
\'93You gave too much rein to your imagination. Imagination is a good\
servant, and a bad master. The simplest explanation is always the most\
likely.\'94\
\
\'93Another point\'97how did you know that the key of the despatch-case had\
been lost?\'94\
\
\'93I did not know it. It was a guess that turned out to be correct. You\
observed that it had a piece of twisted wire through the handle. That\
suggested to me at once that it had possibly been wrenched off a flimsy\
key-ring. Now, if it had been lost and recovered, Mrs. Inglethorp would\
at once have replaced it on her bunch; but on her bunch I found what\
was obviously the duplicate key, very new and bright, which led me to\
the hypothesis that somebody else had inserted the original key in the\
lock of the despatch-case.\'94\
\
\'93Yes,\'94 I said, \'93Alfred Inglethorp, without doubt.\'94\
\
Poirot looked at me curiously.\
\
\'93You are very sure of his guilt?\'94\
\
\'93Well, naturally. Every fresh circumstance seems to establish it more\
clearly.\'94\
\
\'93On the contrary,\'94 said Poirot quietly, \'93there are several points in\
his favour.\'94\
\
\'93Oh, come now!\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93I see only one.\'94\
\
\'93And that?\'94\
\
\'93That he was not in the house last night.\'94\
\
\'93\'91Bad shot!\'92 as you English say! You have chosen the one point that to\
my mind tells against him.\'94\
\
\'93How is that?\'94\
\
\'93Because if Mr. Inglethorp knew that his wife would be poisoned last\
night, he would certainly have arranged to be away from the house. His\
excuse was an obviously trumped up one. That leaves us two\
possibilities: either he knew what was going to happen or he had a\
reason of his own for his absence.\'94\
\
\'93And that reason?\'94 I asked sceptically.\
\
Poirot shrugged his shoulders.\
\
\'93How should I know? Discreditable, without doubt. This Mr. Inglethorp,\
I should say, is somewhat of a scoundrel\'97but that does not of necessity\
make him a murderer.\'94\
\
I shook my head, unconvinced.\
\
\'93We do not agree, eh?\'94 said Poirot. \'93Well, let us leave it. Time will\
show which of us is right. Now let us turn to other aspects of the\
case. What do you make of the fact that all the doors of the bedroom\
were bolted on the inside?\'94\
\
\'93Well\'97\'97\'94 I considered. \'93One must look at it logically.\'94\
\
\'93True.\'94\
\
\'93I should put it this way. The doors _were_ bolted\'97our own eyes have\
told us that\'97yet the presence of the candle grease on the floor, and\
the destruction of the will, prove that during the night someone\
entered the room. You agree so far?\'94\
\
\'93Perfectly. Put with admirable clearness. Proceed.\'94\
\
\'93Well,\'94 I said, encouraged, \'93as the person who entered did not do so by\
the window, nor by miraculous means, it follows that the door must have\
been opened from inside by Mrs. Inglethorp herself. That strengthens\
the conviction that the person in question was her husband. She would\
naturally open the door to her own husband.\'94\
\
Poirot shook his head.\
\
\'93Why should she? She had bolted the door leading into his room\'97a most\
unusual proceeding on her part\'97she had had a most violent quarrel with\
him that very afternoon. No, he was the last person she would admit.\'94\
\
\'93But you agree with me that the door must have been opened by Mrs.\
Inglethorp herself?\'94\
\
\'93There is another possibility. She may have forgotten to bolt the door\
into the passage when she went to bed, and have got up later, towards\
morning, and bolted it then.\'94\
\
\'93Poirot, is that seriously your opinion?\'94\
\
\'93No, I do not say it is so, but it might be. Now, to turn to another\
feature, what do you make of the scrap of conversation you overheard\
between Mrs. Cavendish and her mother-in-law?\'94\
\
\'93I had forgotten that,\'94 I said thoughtfully. \'93That is as enigmatical as\
ever. It seems incredible that a woman like Mrs. Cavendish, proud and\
reticent to the last degree, should interfere so violently in what was\
certainly not her affair.\'94\
\
\'93Precisely. It was an astonishing thing for a woman of her breeding to\
do.\'94\
\
\'93It is certainly curious,\'94 I agreed. \'93Still, it is unimportant, and\
need not be taken into account.\'94\
\
A groan burst from Poirot.\
\
\'93What have I always told you? Everything must be taken into account. If\
the fact will not fit the theory\'97let the theory go.\'94\
\
\'93Well, we shall see,\'94 I said, nettled.\
\
\'93Yes, we shall see.\'94\
\
We had reached Leastways Cottage, and Poirot ushered me upstairs to his\
own room. He offered me one of the tiny Russian cigarettes he himself\
occasionally smoked. I was amused to notice that he stowed away the\
used matches most carefully in a little china pot. My momentary\
annoyance vanished.\
\
Poirot had placed our two chairs in front of the open window which\
commanded a view of the village street. The fresh air blew in warm and\
pleasant. It was going to be a hot day.\
\
Suddenly my attention was arrested by a weedy looking young man rushing\
down the street at a great pace. It was the expression on his face that\
was extraordinary\'97a curious mingling of terror and agitation.\
\
\'93Look, Poirot!\'94 I said.\
\
He leant forward.\
\
\'93_Tiens!_\'94 he said. \'93It is Mr. Mace, from the chemist\'92s shop. He is\
coming here.\'94\
\
The young man came to a halt before Leastways Cottage, and, after\
hesitating a moment, pounded vigorously at the door.\
\
\'93A little minute,\'94 cried Poirot from the window. \'93I come.\'94\
\
Motioning to me to follow him, he ran swiftly down the stairs and\
opened the door. Mr. Mace began at once.\
\
\'93Oh, Mr. Poirot, I\'92m sorry for the inconvenience, but I heard that\
you\'92d just come back from the Hall?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, we have.\'94\
\
The young man moistened his dry lips. His face was working curiously.\
\
\'93It\'92s all over the village about old Mrs. Inglethorp dying so suddenly.\
They do say\'97\'94 he lowered his voice cautiously\'97\'93that it\'92s poison?\'94\
\
Poirot\'92s face remained quite impassive.\
\
\'93Only the doctors can tell us that, Mr. Mace.\'94\
\
\'93Yes, exactly\'97of course\'97\'97\'94 The young man hesitated, and then his\
agitation was too much for him. He clutched Poirot by the arm, and sank\
his voice to a whisper: \'93Just tell me this, Mr. Poirot, it isn\'92t\'97it\
isn\'92t strychnine, is it?\'94\
\
I hardly heard what Poirot replied. Something evidently of a\
non-committal nature. The young man departed, and as he closed the door\
Poirot\'92s eyes met mine.\
\
\'93Yes,\'94 he said, nodding gravely. \'93He will have evidence to give at the\
inquest.\'94\
\
We went slowly upstairs again. I was opening my lips, when Poirot\
stopped me with a gesture of his hand.\
\
\'93Not now, not now, _mon ami_. I have need of reflection. My mind is in\
some disorder\'97which is not well.\'94\
\
For about ten minutes he sat in dead silence, perfectly still, except\
for several expressive motions of his eyebrows, and all the time his\
eyes grew steadily greener. At last he heaved a deep sigh.\
\
\'93It is well. The bad moment has passed. Now all is arranged and\
classified. One must never permit confusion. The case is not clear\
yet\'97no. For it is of the most complicated! It puzzles _me_. _Me_,\
Hercule Poirot! There are two facts of significance.\'94\
\
\'93And what are they?\'94\
\
\'93The first is the state of the weather yesterday. That is very\
important.\'94\
\
\'93But it was a glorious day!\'94 I interrupted. \'93Poirot, you\'92re pulling my\
leg!\'94\
\
\'93Not at all. The thermometer registered 80 degrees in the shade. Do not\
forget that, my friend. It is the key to the whole riddle!\'94\
\
\'93And the second point?\'94 I asked.\
\
\'93The important fact that Monsieur Inglethorp wears very peculiar\
clothes, has a black beard, and uses glasses.\'94\
\
\'93Poirot, I cannot believe you are serious.\'94\
\
\'93I am absolutely serious, my friend.\'94\
\
\'93But this is childish!\'94\
\
\'93No, it is very momentous.\'94\
\
\'93And supposing the Coroner\'92s jury returns a verdict of Wilful Murder\
against Alfred Inglethorp. What becomes of your theories, then?\'94\
\
\'93They would not be shaken because twelve stupid men had happened to\
make a mistake! But that will not occur. For one thing, a country jury\
is not anxious to take responsibility upon itself, and Mr. Inglethorp\
stands practically in the position of local squire. Also,\'94 he added\
placidly, \'93_I_ should not allow it!\'94\
\
\'93_You_ would not allow it?\'94\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
I looked at the extraordinary little man, divided between annoyance and\
amusement. He was so tremendously sure of himself. As though he read my\
thoughts, he nodded gently.\
\
\'93Oh, yes, _mon ami_, I would do what I say.\'94 He got up and laid his\
hand on my shoulder. His physiognomy underwent a complete change. Tears\
came into his eyes. \'93In all this, you see, I think of that poor Mrs.\
Inglethorp who is dead. She was not extravagantly loved\'97no. But she was\
very good to us Belgians\'97I owe her a debt.\'94\
\
I endeavoured to interrupt, but Poirot swept on.\
\
\'93Let me tell you this, Hastings. She would never forgive me if I let\
Alfred Inglethorp, her husband, be arrested _now_\'97when a word from me\
could save him!\'94\
\
\
\
\
\
\
CHAPTER VI. THE INQUEST\
\
In the interval before the inquest, Poirot was unfailing in his\
activity. Twice he was closeted with Mr. Wells. He also took long walks\
into the country. I rather resented his not taking me into his\
confidence, the more so as I could not in the least guess what he was\
driving at.\
\
It occurred to me that he might have been making inquiries at Raikes\'92s\
farm; so, finding him out when I called at Leastways Cottage on\
Wednesday evening, I walked over there by the fields, hoping to meet\
him. But there was no sign of him, and I hesitated to go right up to\
the farm itself. As I walked away, I met an aged rustic, who leered at\
me cunningly.\
\
\'93You\'92m from the Hall, bain\'92t you?\'94 he asked.\
\
\'93Yes. I\'92m looking for a friend of mine whom I thought might have walked\
this way.\'94\
\
\'93A little chap? As waves his hands when he talks? One of them Belgies\
from the village?\'94\
\
\'93Yes,\'94 I said eagerly. \'93He has been here, then?\'94\
\
\'93Oh, ay, he\'92s been here, right enough. More\'92n once too. Friend of\
yours, is he? Ah, you gentlemen from the Hall\'97you\'92m a pretty lot!\'94 And\
he leered more jocosely than ever.\
\
\'93Why, do the gentlemen from the Hall come here often?\'94 I asked, as\
carelessly as I could.\
\
He winked at me knowingly.\
\
\'93_One_ does, mister. Naming no names, mind. And a very liberal\
gentleman too! Oh, thank you, sir, I\'92m sure.\'94\
\
I walked on sharply. Evelyn Howard had been right then, and I\
experienced a sharp twinge of disgust, as I thought of Alfred\
Inglethorp\'92s liberality with another woman\'92s money. Had that piquant\
gipsy face been at the bottom of the crime, or was it the baser\
mainspring of money? Probably a judicious mixture of both.\
\
On one point, Poirot seemed to have a curious obsession. He once or\
twice observed to me that he thought Dorcas must have made an error in\
fixing the time of the quarrel. He suggested to her repeatedly that it\
was four-thirty, and not four o\'92clock when she had heard the voices.\
\
But Dorcas was unshaken. Quite an hour, or even more, had elapsed\
between the time when she had heard the voices and five o\'92clock, when\
she had taken tea to her mistress.\
\
The inquest was held on Friday at the Stylites Arms in the village.\
Poirot and I sat together, not being required to give evidence.\
\
The preliminaries were gone through. The jury viewed the body, and John\
Cavendish gave evidence of identification.\
\
Further questioned, he described his awakening in the early hours of\
the morning, and the circumstances of his mother\'92s death.\
\
The medical evidence was next taken. There was a breathless hush, and\
every eye was fixed on the famous London specialist, who was known to\
be one of the greatest authorities of the day on the subject of\
toxicology.\
\
In a few brief words, he summed up the result of the post-mortem. Shorn\
of its medical phraseology and technicalities, it amounted to the fact\
that Mrs. Inglethorp had met her death as the result of strychnine\
poisoning. Judging from the quantity recovered, she must have taken not\
less than three-quarters of a grain of strychnine, but probably one\
grain or slightly over.\
\
\'93Is it possible that she could have swallowed the poison by accident?\'94\
asked the Coroner.\
\
\'93I should consider it very unlikely. Strychnine is not used for\
domestic purposes, as some poisons are, and there are restrictions\
placed on its sale.\'94\
\
\'93Does anything in your examination lead you to determine how the poison\
was administered?\'94\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
\'93You arrived at Styles before Dr. Wilkins, I believe?\'94\
\
\'93That is so. The motor met me just outside the lodge gates, and I\
hurried there as fast as I could.\'94\
\
\'93Will you relate to us exactly what happened next?\'94\
\
\'93I entered Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s room. She was at that moment in a typical\
tetanic convulsion. She turned towards me, and gasped out:\
\'91Alfred\'97Alfred\'97\'97\'92\'94\
\
\'93Could the strychnine have been administered in Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s\
after-dinner coffee which was taken to her by her husband?\'94\
\
\'93Possibly, but strychnine is a fairly rapid drug in its action. The\
symptoms appear from one to two hours after it has been swallowed. It\
is retarded under certain conditions, none of which, however, appear to\
have been present in this case. I presume Mrs. Inglethorp took the\
coffee after dinner about eight o\'92clock, whereas the symptoms did not\
manifest themselves until the early hours of the morning, which, on the\
face of it, points to the drug having been taken much later in the\
evening.\'94\
\
\'93Mrs. Inglethorp was in the habit of drinking a cup of cocoa in the\
middle of the night. Could the strychnine have been administered in\
that?\'94\
\
\'93No, I myself took a sample of the cocoa remaining in the saucepan and\
had it analysed. There was no strychnine present.\'94\
\
I heard Poirot chuckle softly beside me.\
\
\'93How did you know?\'94 I whispered.\
\
\'93Listen.\'94\
\
\'93I should say\'94\'97the doctor was continuing\'97\'93that I would have been\
considerably surprised at any other result.\'94\
\
\'93Why?\'94\
\
\'93Simply because strychnine has an unusually bitter taste. It can be\
detected in a solution of one in seventy thousand, and can only be\
disguised by some strongly flavoured substance. Cocoa would be quite\
powerless to mask it.\'94\
\
One of the jury wanted to know if the same objection applied to coffee.\
\
\'93No. Coffee has a bitter taste of its own which would probably cover\
the taste of strychnine.\'94\
\
\'93Then you consider it more likely that the drug was administered in the\
coffee, but that for some unknown reason its action was delayed.\'94\
\
\'93Yes, but, the cup being completely smashed, there is no possibility of\
analyzing its contents.\'94\
\
This concluded Dr. Bauerstein\'92s evidence. Dr. Wilkins corroborated it\
on all points. Sounded as to the possibility of suicide, he repudiated\
it utterly. The deceased, he said, suffered from a weak heart, but\
otherwise enjoyed perfect health, and was of a cheerful and\
well-balanced disposition. She would be one of the last people to take\
her own life.\
\
Lawrence Cavendish was next called. His evidence was quite unimportant,\
being a mere repetition of that of his brother. Just as he was about to\
step down, he paused, and said rather hesitatingly:\
\
\'93I should like to make a suggestion if I may?\'94\
\
He glanced deprecatingly at the Coroner, who replied briskly:\
\
\'93Certainly, Mr. Cavendish, we are here to arrive at the truth of this\
matter, and welcome anything that may lead to further elucidation.\'94\
\
\'93It is just an idea of mine,\'94 explained Lawrence. \'93Of course I may be\
quite wrong, but it still seems to me that my mother\'92s death might be\
accounted for by natural means.\'94\
\
\'93How do you make that out, Mr. Cavendish?\'94\
\
\'93My mother, at the time of her death, and for some time before it, was\
taking a tonic containing strychnine.\'94\
\
\'93Ah!\'94 said the Coroner.\
\
The jury looked up, interested.\
\
\'93I believe,\'94 continued Lawrence, \'93that there have been cases where the\
cumulative effect of a drug, administered for some time, has ended by\
causing death. Also, is it not possible that she may have taken an\
overdose of her medicine by accident?\'94\
\
\'93This is the first we have heard of the deceased taking strychnine at\
the time of her death. We are much obliged to you, Mr. Cavendish.\'94\
\
Dr. Wilkins was recalled and ridiculed the idea.\
\
\'93What Mr. Cavendish suggests is quite impossible. Any doctor would tell\
you the same. Strychnine is, in a certain sense, a cumulative poison,\
but it would be quite impossible for it to result in sudden death in\
this way. There would have to be a long period of chronic symptoms\
which would at once have attracted my attention. The whole thing is\
absurd.\'94\
\
\'93And the second suggestion? That Mrs. Inglethorp may have inadvertently\
taken an overdose?\'94\
\
\'93Three, or even four doses, would not have resulted in death. Mrs.\
Inglethorp always had an extra large amount of medicine made up at a\
time, as she dealt with Coot\'92s, the Cash Chemists in Tadminster. She\
would have had to take very nearly the whole bottle to account for the\
amount of strychnine found at the post-mortem.\'94\
\
\'93Then you consider that we may dismiss the tonic as not being in any\
way instrumental in causing her death?\'94\
\
\'93Certainly. The supposition is ridiculous.\'94\
\
The same juryman who had interrupted before here suggested that the\
chemist who made up the medicine might have committed an error.\
\
\'93That, of course, is always possible,\'94 replied the doctor.\
\
But Dorcas, who was the next witness called, dispelled even that\
possibility. The medicine had not been newly made up. On the contrary,\
Mrs. Inglethorp had taken the last dose on the day of her death.\
\
So the question of the tonic was finally abandoned, and the Coroner\
proceeded with his task. Having elicited from Dorcas how she had been\
awakened by the violent ringing of her mistress\'92s bell, and had\
subsequently roused the household, he passed to the subject of the\
quarrel on the preceding afternoon.\
\
Dorcas\'92s evidence on this point was substantially what Poirot and I had\
already heard, so I will not repeat it here.\
\
The next witness was Mary Cavendish. She stood very upright, and spoke\
in a low, clear, and perfectly composed voice. In answer to the\
Coroner\'92s question, she told how, her alarm clock having aroused her at\
four-thirty as usual, she was dressing, when she was startled by the\
sound of something heavy falling.\
\
\'93That would have been the table by the bed?\'94 commented the Coroner.\
\
\'93I opened my door,\'94 continued Mary, \'93and listened. In a few minutes a\
bell rang violently. Dorcas came running down and woke my husband, and\
we all went to my mother-in-law\'92s room, but it was locked\'97\'97\'94\
\
The Coroner interrupted her.\
\
\'93I really do not think we need trouble you further on that point. We\
know all that can be known of the subsequent happenings. But I should\
be obliged if you would tell us all you overheard of the quarrel the\
day before.\'94\
\
\'93I?\'94\
\
There was a faint insolence in her voice. She raised her hand and\
adjusted the ruffle of lace at her neck, turning her head a little as\
she did so. And quite spontaneously the thought flashed across my mind:\
\'93She is gaining time!\'94\
\
\'93Yes. I understand,\'94 continued the Coroner deliberately, \'93that you were\
sitting reading on the bench just outside the long window of the\
boudoir. That is so, is it not?\'94\
\
This was news to me and glancing sideways at Poirot, I fancied that it\
was news to him as well.\
\
There was the faintest pause, the mere hesitation of a moment, before\
she answered:\
\
\'93Yes, that is so.\'94\
\
\'93And the boudoir window was open, was it not?\'94\
\
Surely her face grew a little paler as she answered:\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93Then you cannot have failed to hear the voices inside, especially as\
they were raised in anger. In fact, they would be more audible where\
you were than in the hall.\'94\
\
\'93Possibly.\'94\
\
\'93Will you repeat to us what you overheard of the quarrel?\'94\
\
\'93I really do not remember hearing anything.\'94\
\
\'93Do you mean to say you did not hear voices?\'94\
\
\'93Oh, yes, I heard the voices, but I did not hear what they said.\'94 A\
faint spot of colour came into her cheek. \'93I am not in the habit of\
listening to private conversations.\'94\
\
The Coroner persisted.\
\
\'93And you remember nothing at all? _Nothing_, Mrs. Cavendish? Not one\
stray word or phrase to make you realize that it _was_ a private\
conversation?\'94\
\
She paused, and seemed to reflect, still outwardly as calm as ever.\
\
\'93Yes; I remember. Mrs. Inglethorp said something\'97I do not remember\
exactly what\'97about causing scandal between husband and wife.\'94\
\
\'93Ah!\'94 the Coroner leant back satisfied. \'93That corresponds with what\
Dorcas heard. But excuse me, Mrs. Cavendish, although you realized it\
was a private conversation, you did not move away? You remained where\
you were?\'94\
\
I caught the momentary gleam of her tawny eyes as she raised them. I\
felt certain that at that moment she would willingly have torn the\
little lawyer, with his insinuations, into pieces, but she replied\
quietly enough:\
\
\'93No. I was very comfortable where I was. I fixed my mind on my book.\'94\
\
\'93And that is all you can tell us?\'94\
\
\'93That is all.\'94\
\
The examination was over, though I doubted if the Coroner was entirely\
satisfied with it. I think he suspected that Mary Cavendish could tell\
more if she chose.\
\
Amy Hill, shop assistant, was next called, and deposed to having sold a\
will form on the afternoon of the 17th to William Earl, under-gardener\
at Styles.\
\
William Earl and Manning succeeded her, and testified to witnessing a\
document. Manning fixed the time at about four-thirty, William was of\
the opinion that it was rather earlier.\
\
Cynthia Murdoch came next. She had, however, little to tell. She had\
known nothing of the tragedy, until awakened by Mrs. Cavendish.\
\
\'93You did not hear the table fall?\'94\
\
\'93No. I was fast asleep.\'94\
\
The Coroner smiled.\
\
\'93A good conscience makes a sound sleeper,\'94 he observed. \'93Thank you,\
Miss Murdoch, that is all.\'94\
\
\'93Miss Howard.\'94\
\
Miss Howard produced the letter written to her by Mrs. Inglethorp on\
the evening of the 17th. Poirot and I had, of course already seen it.\
It added nothing to our knowledge of the tragedy. The following is a\
facsimile:\
\
STYLES COURT\
ESSEX\
hand written note:\
\
     July 17th\
     My dear Evelyn\
     Can we not bury\
     the hachet? I have\
     found it hard to forgive\
     the things you said\
     against my dear husband\
     but I am an old woman\
     & very fond of you\
     Yours affectionately,\
    Emily Inglethorpe\
\
\
It was handed to the jury who scrutinized it attentively.\
\
\'93I fear it does not help us much,\'94 said the Coroner, with a sigh.\
\'93There is no mention of any of the events of that afternoon.\'94\
\
\'93Plain as a pikestaff to me,\'94 said Miss Howard shortly. \'93It shows\
clearly enough that my poor old friend had just found out she\'92d been\
made a fool of!\'94\
\
\'93It says nothing of the kind in the letter,\'94 the Coroner pointed out.\
\
\'93No, because Emily never could bear to put herself in the wrong. But\
_I_ know her. She wanted me back. But she wasn\'92t going to own that I\'92d\
been right. She went round about. Most people do. Don\'92t believe in it\
myself.\'94\
\
Mr. Wells smiled faintly. So, I noticed, did several of the jury. Miss\
Howard was obviously quite a public character.\
\
\'93Anyway, all this tomfoolery is a great waste of time,\'94 continued the\
lady, glancing up and down the jury disparagingly. \'93Talk\'97talk\'97talk!\
When all the time we know perfectly well\'97\'97\'94\
\
The Coroner interrupted her in an agony of apprehension:\
\
\'93Thank you, Miss Howard, that is all.\'94\
\
I fancy he breathed a sigh of relief when she complied.\
\
Then came the sensation of the day. The Coroner called Albert Mace,\
chemist\'92s assistant.\
\
It was our agitated young man of the pale face. In answer to the\
Coroner\'92s questions, he explained that he was a qualified pharmacist,\
but had only recently come to this particular shop, as the assistant\
formerly there had just been called up for the army.\
\
These preliminaries completed, the Coroner proceeded to business.\
\
\'93Mr. Mace, have you lately sold strychnine to any unauthorized person?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, sir.\'94\
\
\'93When was this?\'94\
\
\'93Last Monday night.\'94\
\
\'93Monday? Not Tuesday?\'94\
\
\'93No, sir, Monday, the 16th.\'94\
\
\'93Will you tell us to whom you sold it?\'94\
\
You could have heard a pin drop.\
\
\'93Yes, sir. It was to Mr. Inglethorp.\'94\
\
Every eye turned simultaneously to where Alfred Inglethorp was sitting,\
impassive and wooden. He started slightly, as the damning words fell\
from the young man\'92s lips. I half thought he was going to rise from his\
chair, but he remained seated, although a remarkably well acted\
expression of astonishment rose on his face.\
\
\'93You are sure of what you say?\'94 asked the Coroner sternly.\
\
\'93Quite sure, sir.\'94\
\
\'93Are you in the habit of selling strychnine indiscriminately over the\
counter?\'94\
\
The wretched young man wilted visibly under the Coroner\'92s frown.\
\
\'93Oh, no, sir\'97of course not. But, seeing it was Mr. Inglethorp of the\
Hall, I thought there was no harm in it. He said it was to poison a\
dog.\'94\
\
Inwardly I sympathized. It was only human nature to endeavour to please\
\'93The Hall\'94\'97especially when it might result in custom being transferred\
from Coot\'92s to the local establishment.\
\
\'93Is it not customary for anyone purchasing poison to sign a book?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, sir, Mr. Inglethorp did so.\'94\
\
\'93Have you got the book here?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, sir.\'94\
\
It was produced; and, with a few words of stern censure, the Coroner\
dismissed the wretched Mr. Mace.\
\
Then, amidst a breathless silence, Alfred Inglethorp was called. Did he\
realize, I wondered, how closely the halter was being drawn around his\
neck?\
\
The Coroner went straight to the point.\
\
\'93On Monday evening last, did you purchase strychnine for the purpose of\
poisoning a dog?\'94\
\
Inglethorp replied with perfect calmness:\
\
\'93No, I did not. There is no dog at Styles, except an outdoor sheepdog,\
which is in perfect health.\'94\
\
\'93You deny absolutely having purchased strychnine from Albert Mace on\
Monday last?\'94\
\
\'93I do.\'94\
\
\'93Do you also deny _this_?\'94\
\
The Coroner handed him the register in which his signature was\
inscribed.\
\
\'93Certainly I do. The hand-writing is quite different from mine. I will\
show you.\'94\
\
He took an old envelope out of his pocket, and wrote his name on it,\
handing it to the jury. It was certainly utterly dissimilar.\
\
\'93Then what is your explanation of Mr. Mace\'92s statement?\'94\
\
Alfred Inglethorp replied imperturbably:\
\
\'93Mr. Mace must have been mistaken.\'94\
\
The Coroner hesitated for a moment, and then said:\
\
\'93Mr. Inglethorp, as a mere matter of form, would you mind telling us\
where you were on the evening of Monday, July 16th?\'94\
\
\'93Really\'97I cannot remember.\'94\
\
\'93That is absurd, Mr. Inglethorp,\'94 said the Coroner sharply. \'93Think\
again.\'94\
\
Inglethorp shook his head.\
\
\'93I cannot tell you. I have an idea that I was out walking.\'94\
\
\'93In what direction?\'94\
\
\'93I really can\'92t remember.\'94\
\
The Coroner\'92s face grew graver.\
\
\'93Were you in company with anyone?\'94\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
\'93Did you meet anyone on your walk?\'94\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
\'93That is a pity,\'94 said the Coroner dryly. \'93I am to take it then that\
you decline to say where you were at the time that Mr. Mace positively\
recognized you as entering the shop to purchase strychnine?\'94\
\
\'93If you like to take it that way, yes.\'94\
\
\'93Be careful, Mr. Inglethorp.\'94\
\
Poirot was fidgeting nervously.\
\
\'93_Sacr\'e9!_\'94 he murmured. \'93Does this imbecile of a man _want_ to be\
arrested?\'94\
\
Inglethorp was indeed creating a bad impression. His futile denials\
would not have convinced a child. The Coroner, however, passed briskly\
to the next point, and Poirot drew a deep breath of relief.\
\
\'93You had a discussion with your wife on Tuesday afternoon?\'94\
\
\'93Pardon me,\'94 interrupted Alfred Inglethorp, \'93you have been misinformed.\
I had no quarrel with my dear wife. The whole story is absolutely\
untrue. I was absent from the house the entire afternoon.\'94\
\
