\'93Have you anyone who can testify to that?\'94\
\
\'93You have my word,\'94 said Inglethorp haughtily.\
\
The Coroner did not trouble to reply.\
\
\'93There are two witnesses who will swear to having heard your\
disagreement with Mrs. Inglethorp.\'94\
\
\'93Those witnesses were mistaken.\'94\
\
I was puzzled. The man spoke with such quiet assurance that I was\
staggered. I looked at Poirot. There was an expression of exultation on\
his face which I could not understand. Was he at last convinced of\
Alfred Inglethorp\'92s guilt?\
\
\'93Mr. Inglethorp,\'94 said the Coroner, \'93you have heard your wife\'92s dying\
words repeated here. Can you explain them in any way?\'94\
\
\'93Certainly I can.\'94\
\
\'93You can?\'94\
\
\'93It seems to me very simple. The room was dimly lighted. Dr. Bauerstein\
is much of my height and build, and, like me, wears a beard. In the dim\
light, and suffering as she was, my poor wife mistook him for me.\'94\
\
\'93Ah!\'94 murmured Poirot to himself. \'93But it is an idea, that!\'94\
\
\'93You think it is true?\'94 I whispered.\
\
\'93I do not say that. But it is truly an ingenious supposition.\'94\
\
\'93You read my wife\'92s last words as an accusation\'94\'97Inglethorp was\
continuing\'97\'93they were, on the contrary, an appeal to me.\'94\
\
The Coroner reflected a moment, then he said:\
\
\'93I believe, Mr. Inglethorp, that you yourself poured out the coffee,\
and took it to your wife that evening?\'94\
\
\'93I poured it out, yes. But I did not take it to her. I meant to do so,\
but I was told that a friend was at the hall door, so I laid down the\
coffee on the hall table. When I came through the hall again a few\
minutes later, it was gone.\'94\
\
This statement might, or might not, be true, but it did not seem to me\
to improve matters much for Inglethorp. In any case, he had had ample\
time to introduce the poison.\
\
At that point, Poirot nudged me gently, indicating two men who were\
sitting together near the door. One was a little, sharp, dark,\
ferret-faced man, the other was tall and fair.\
\
I questioned Poirot mutely. He put his lips to my ear.\
\
\'93Do you know who that little man is?\'94\
\
I shook my head.\
\
\'93That is Detective Inspector James Japp of Scotland Yard\'97Jimmy Japp.\
The other man is from Scotland Yard too. Things are moving quickly, my\
friend.\'94\
\
I stared at the two men intently. There was certainly nothing of the\
policeman about them. I should never have suspected them of being\
official personages.\
\
I was still staring, when I was startled and recalled by the verdict\
being given:\
\
\'93Wilful Murder against some person or persons unknown.\'94\
\
\
\
\
\
\
CHAPTER VII. POIROT PAYS HIS DEBTS\
\
As we came out of the Stylites Arms, Poirot drew me aside by a gentle\
pressure of the arm. I understood his object. He was waiting for the\
Scotland Yard men.\
\
In a few moments, they emerged, and Poirot at once stepped forward, and\
accosted the shorter of the two.\
\
\'93I fear you do not remember me, Inspector Japp.\'94\
\
\'93Why, if it isn\'92t Mr. Poirot!\'94 cried the Inspector. He turned to the\
other man. \'93You\'92ve heard me speak of Mr. Poirot? It was in 1904 he and\
I worked together\'97the Abercrombie forgery case\'97you remember, he was run\
down in Brussels. Ah, those were great days, moosier. Then, do you\
remember \'91Baron\'92 Altara? There was a pretty rogue for you! He eluded\
the clutches of half the police in Europe. But we nailed him in\
Antwerp\'97thanks to Mr. Poirot here.\'94\
\
As these friendly reminiscences were being indulged in, I drew nearer,\
and was introduced to Detective-Inspector Japp, who, in his turn,\
introduced us both to his companion, Superintendent Summerhaye.\
\
\'93I need hardly ask what you are doing here, gentlemen,\'94 remarked\
Poirot.\
\
Japp closed one eye knowingly.\
\
\'93No, indeed. Pretty clear case I should say.\'94\
\
But Poirot answered gravely:\
\
\'93There I differ from you.\'94\
\
\'93Oh, come!\'94 said Summerhaye, opening his lips for the first time.\
\'93Surely the whole thing is clear as daylight. The man\'92s caught\
red-handed. How he could be such a fool beats me!\'94\
\
But Japp was looking attentively at Poirot.\
\
\'93Hold your fire, Summerhaye,\'94 he remarked jocularly. \'93Me and Moosier\
here have met before\'97and there\'92s no man\'92s judgment I\'92d sooner take than\
his. If I\'92m not greatly mistaken, he\'92s got something up his sleeve.\
Isn\'92t that so, moosier?\'94\
\
Poirot smiled.\
\
\'93I have drawn certain conclusions\'97yes.\'94\
\
Summerhaye was still looking rather sceptical, but Japp continued his\
scrutiny of Poirot.\
\
\'93It\'92s this way,\'94 he said, \'93so far, we\'92ve only seen the case from the\
outside. That\'92s where the Yard\'92s at a disadvantage in a case of this\
kind, where the murder\'92s only out, so to speak, after the inquest. A\
lot depends on being on the spot first thing, and that\'92s where Mr.\
Poirot\'92s had the start of us. We shouldn\'92t have been here as soon as\
this even, if it hadn\'92t been for the fact that there was a smart doctor\
on the spot, who gave us the tip through the Coroner. But you\'92ve been\
on the spot from the first, and you may have picked up some little\
hints. From the evidence at the inquest, Mr. Inglethorp murdered his\
wife as sure as I stand here, and if anyone but you hinted the contrary\
I\'92d laugh in his face. I must say I was surprised the jury didn\'92t bring\
it in Wilful Murder against him right off. I think they would have, if\
it hadn\'92t been for the Coroner\'97he seemed to be holding them back.\'94\
\
\'93Perhaps, though, you have a warrant for his arrest in your pocket\
now,\'94 suggested Poirot.\
\
A kind of wooden shutter of officialdom came down from Japp\'92s\
expressive countenance.\
\
\'93Perhaps I have, and perhaps I haven\'92t,\'94 he remarked dryly.\
\
Poirot looked at him thoughtfully.\
\
\'93I am very anxious, Messieurs, that he should not be arrested.\'94\
\
\'93I dare say,\'94 observed Summerhaye sarcastically.\
\
Japp was regarding Poirot with comical perplexity.\
\
\'93Can\'92t you go a little further, Mr. Poirot? A wink\'92s as good as a\
nod\'97from you. You\'92ve been on the spot\'97and the Yard doesn\'92t want to make\
any mistakes, you know.\'94\
\
Poirot nodded gravely.\
\
\'93That is exactly what I thought. Well, I will tell you this. Use your\
warrant: Arrest Mr. Inglethorp. But it will bring you no kudos\'97the case\
against him will be dismissed at once! _Comme \'e7a!_\'94 And he snapped his\
fingers expressively.\
\
Japp\'92s face grew grave, though Summerhaye gave an incredulous snort.\
\
As for me, I was literally dumb with astonishment. I could only\
conclude that Poirot was mad.\
\
Japp had taken out a handkerchief, and was gently dabbing his brow.\
\
\'93I daren\'92t do it, Mr. Poirot. _I_\'92d take your word, but there\'92s others\
over me who\'92ll be asking what the devil I mean by it. Can\'92t you give me\
a little more to go on?\'94\
\
Poirot reflected a moment.\
\
\'93It can be done,\'94 he said at last. \'93I admit I do not wish it. It forces\
my hand. I would have preferred to work in the dark just for the\
present, but what you say is very just\'97the word of a Belgian policeman,\
whose day is past, is not enough! And Alfred Inglethorp must not be\
arrested. That I have sworn, as my friend Hastings here knows. See,\
then, my good Japp, you go at once to Styles?\'94\
\
\'93Well, in about half an hour. We\'92re seeing the Coroner and the doctor\
first.\'94\
\
\'93Good. Call for me in passing\'97the last house in the village. I will go\
with you. At Styles, Mr. Inglethorp will give you, or if he refuses\'97as\
is probable\'97I will give you such proofs that shall satisfy you that the\
case against him could not possibly be sustained. Is that a bargain?\'94\
\
\'93That\'92s a bargain,\'94 said Japp heartily. \'93And, on behalf of the Yard,\
I\'92m much obliged to you, though I\'92m bound to confess I can\'92t at present\
see the faintest possible loop-hole in the evidence, but you always\
were a marvel! So long, then, moosier.\'94\
\
The two detectives strode away, Summerhaye with an incredulous grin on\
his face.\
\
\'93Well, my friend,\'94 cried Poirot, before I could get in a word, \'93what do\
you think? _Mon Dieu!_ I had some warm moments in that court; I did not\
figure to myself that the man would be so pig-headed as to refuse to\
say anything at all. Decidedly, it was the policy of an imbecile.\'94\
\
\'93H\'92m! There are other explanations besides that of imbecility,\'94 I\
remarked. \'93For, if the case against him is true, how could he defend\
himself except by silence?\'94\
\
\'93Why, in a thousand ingenious ways,\'94 cried Poirot. \'93See; say that it is\
I who have committed this murder, I can think of seven most plausible\
stories! Far more convincing than Mr. Inglethorp\'92s stony denials!\'94\
\
I could not help laughing.\
\
\'93My dear Poirot, I am sure you are capable of thinking of seventy! But,\
seriously, in spite of what I heard you say to the detectives, you\
surely cannot still believe in the possibility of Alfred Inglethorp\'92s\
innocence?\'94\
\
\'93Why not now as much as before? Nothing has changed.\'94\
\
\'93But the evidence is so conclusive.\'94\
\
\'93Yes, too conclusive.\'94\
\
We turned in at the gate of Leastways Cottage, and proceeded up the now\
familiar stairs.\
\
\'93Yes, yes, too conclusive,\'94 continued Poirot, almost to himself. \'93Real\
evidence is usually vague and unsatisfactory. It has to be\
examined\'97sifted. But here the whole thing is cut and dried. No, my\
friend, this evidence has been very cleverly manufactured\'97so cleverly\
that it has defeated its own ends.\'94\
\
\'93How do you make that out?\'94\
\
\'93Because, so long as the evidence against him was vague and intangible,\
it was very hard to disprove. But, in his anxiety, the criminal has\
drawn the net so closely that one cut will set Inglethorp free.\'94\
\
I was silent. And in a minute or two, Poirot continued:\
\
\'93Let us look at the matter like this. Here is a man, let us say, who\
sets out to poison his wife. He has lived by his wits as the saying\
goes. Presumably, therefore, he has some wits. He is not altogether a\
fool. Well, how does he set about it? He goes boldly to the village\
chemist\'92s and purchases strychnine under his own name, with a trumped\
up story about a dog which is bound to be proved absurd. He does not\
employ the poison that night. No, he waits until he has had a violent\
quarrel with her, of which the whole household is cognisant, and which\
naturally directs their suspicions upon him. He prepares no defence\'97no\
shadow of an alibi, yet he knows the chemist\'92s assistant must\
necessarily come forward with the facts. Bah! Do not ask me to believe\
that any man could be so idiotic! Only a lunatic, who wished to commit\
suicide by causing himself to be hanged, would act so!\'94\
\
\'93Still\'97I do not see\'97\'97\'94 I began.\
\
\'93Neither do I see. I tell you, _mon ami_, it puzzles me. _Me_\'97Hercule\
Poirot!\'94\
\
\'93But if you believe him innocent, how do you explain his buying the\
strychnine?\'94\
\
\'93Very simply. He did _not_ buy it.\'94\
\
\'93But Mace recognized him!\'94\
\
\'93I beg your pardon, he saw a man with a black beard like Mr.\
Inglethorp\'92s, and wearing glasses like Mr. Inglethorp, and dressed in\
Mr. Inglethorp\'92s rather noticeable clothes. He could not recognize a\
man whom he had probably only seen in the distance, since, you\
remember, he himself had only been in the village a fortnight, and Mrs.\
Inglethorp dealt principally with Coot\'92s in Tadminster.\'94\
\
\'93Then you think\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93_Mon ami_, do you remember the two points I laid stress upon? Leave\
the first one for the moment, what was the second?\'94\
\
\'93The important fact that Alfred Inglethorp wears peculiar clothes, has\
a black beard, and uses glasses,\'94 I quoted.\
\
\'93Exactly. Now suppose anyone wished to pass himself off as John or\
Lawrence Cavendish. Would it be easy?\'94\
\
\'93No,\'94 I said thoughtfully. \'93Of course an actor\'97\'97\'94\
\
But Poirot cut me short ruthlessly.\
\
\'93And why would it not be easy? I will tell you, my friend: Because they\
are both clean-shaven men. To make up successfully as one of these two\
in broad daylight, it would need an actor of genius, and a certain\
initial facial resemblance. But in the case of Alfred Inglethorp, all\
that is changed. His clothes, his beard, the glasses which hide his\
eyes\'97those are the salient points about his personal appearance. Now,\
what is the first instinct of the criminal? To divert suspicion from\
himself, is it not so? And how can he best do that? By throwing it on\
someone else. In this instance, there was a man ready to his hand.\
Everybody was predisposed to believe in Mr. Inglethorp\'92s guilt. It was\
a foregone conclusion that he would be suspected; but, to make it a\
sure thing there must be tangible proof\'97such as the actual buying of\
the poison, and that, with a man of the peculiar appearance of Mr.\
Inglethorp, was not difficult. Remember, this young Mace had never\
actually spoken to Mr. Inglethorp. How should he doubt that the man in\
his clothes, with his beard and his glasses, was not Alfred\
Inglethorp?\'94\
\
\'93It may be so,\'94 I said, fascinated by Poirot\'92s eloquence. \'93But, if that\
was the case, why does he not say where he was at six o\'92clock on Monday\
evening?\'94\
\
\'93Ah, why indeed?\'94 said Poirot, calming down. \'93If he were arrested, he\
probably would speak, but I do not want it to come to that. I must make\
him see the gravity of his position. There is, of course, something\
discreditable behind his silence. If he did not murder his wife, he is,\
nevertheless, a scoundrel, and has something of his own to conceal,\
quite apart from the murder.\'94\
\
\'93What can it be?\'94 I mused, won over to Poirot\'92s views for the moment,\
although still retaining a faint conviction that the obvious deduction\
was the correct one.\
\
\'93Can you not guess?\'94 asked Poirot, smiling.\
\
\'93No, can you?\'94\
\
\'93Oh, yes, I had a little idea sometime ago\'97and it has turned out to be\
correct.\'94\
\
\'93You never told me,\'94 I said reproachfully.\
\
Poirot spread out his hands apologetically.\
\
\'93Pardon me, _mon ami_, you were not precisely _sympathique_.\'94 He turned\
to me earnestly. \'93Tell me\'97you see now that he must not be arrested?\'94\
\
\'93Perhaps,\'94 I said doubtfully, for I was really quite indifferent to the\
fate of Alfred Inglethorp, and thought that a good fright would do him\
no harm.\
\
Poirot, who was watching me intently, gave a sigh.\
\
\'93Come, my friend,\'94 he said, changing the subject, \'93apart from Mr.\
Inglethorp, how did the evidence at the inquest strike you?\'94\
\
\'93Oh, pretty much what I expected.\'94\
\
\'93Did nothing strike you as peculiar about it?\'94\
\
My thoughts flew to Mary Cavendish, and I hedged:\
\
\'93In what way?\'94\
\
\'93Well, Mr. Lawrence Cavendish\'92s evidence for instance?\'94\
\
I was relieved.\
\
\'93Oh, Lawrence! No, I don\'92t think so. He\'92s always a nervous chap.\'94\
\
\'93His suggestion that his mother might have been poisoned accidentally\
by means of the tonic she was taking, that did not strike you as\
strange\'97 _hein?_\'94\
\
\'93No, I can\'92t say it did. The doctors ridiculed it of course. But it was\
quite a natural suggestion for a layman to make.\'94\
\
\'93But Monsieur Lawrence is not a layman. You told me yourself that he\
had started by studying medicine, and that he had taken his degree.\'94\
\
\'93Yes, that\'92s true. I never thought of that.\'94 I was rather startled. \'93It\
_is_ odd.\'94\
\
Poirot nodded.\
\
\'93From the first, his behaviour has been peculiar. Of all the household,\
he alone would be likely to recognize the symptoms of strychnine\
poisoning, and yet we find him the only member of the family to uphold\
strenuously the theory of death from natural causes. If it had been\
Monsieur John, I could have understood it. He has no technical\
knowledge, and is by nature unimaginative. But Monsieur Lawrence\'97no!\
And now, to-day, he puts forward a suggestion that he himself must have\
known was ridiculous. There is food for thought in this, _mon ami!_\'94\
\
\'93It\'92s very confusing,\'94 I agreed.\
\
\'93Then there is Mrs. Cavendish,\'94 continued Poirot. \'93That\'92s another who\
is not telling all she knows! What do you make of her attitude?\'94\
\
\'93I don\'92t know what to make of it. It seems inconceivable that she\
should be shielding Alfred Inglethorp. Yet that is what it looks like.\'94\
\
Poirot nodded reflectively.\
\
\'93Yes, it is queer. One thing is certain, she overheard a good deal more\
of that \'91private conversation\'92 than she was willing to admit.\'94\
\
\'93And yet she is the last person one would accuse of stooping to\
eavesdrop!\'94\
\
\'93Exactly. One thing her evidence _has_ shown me. I made a mistake.\
Dorcas was quite right. The quarrel did take place earlier in the\
afternoon, about four o\'92clock, as she said.\'94\
\
I looked at him curiously. I had never understood his insistence on\
that point.\
\
\'93Yes, a good deal that was peculiar came out to-day,\'94 continued Poirot.\
\'93Dr. Bauerstein, now, what was _he_ doing up and dressed at that hour\
in the morning? It is astonishing to me that no one commented on the\
fact.\'94\
\
\'93He has insomnia, I believe,\'94 I said doubtfully.\
\
\'93Which is a very good, or a very bad explanation,\'94 remarked Poirot. \'93It\
covers everything, and explains nothing. I shall keep my eye on our\
clever Dr. Bauerstein.\'94\
\
\'93Any more faults to find with the evidence?\'94 I inquired satirically.\
\
\'93_Mon ami_,\'94 replied Poirot gravely, \'93when you find that people are not\
telling you the truth\'97look out! Now, unless I am much mistaken, at the\
inquest to-day only one\'97at most, two persons were speaking the truth\
without reservation or subterfuge.\'94\
\
\'93Oh, come now, Poirot! I won\'92t cite Lawrence, or Mrs. Cavendish. But\
there\'92s John\'97and Miss Howard, surely they were speaking the truth?\'94\
\
\'93Both of them, my friend? One, I grant you, but both\'97\'97!\'94\
\
His words gave me an unpleasant shock. Miss Howard\'92s evidence,\
unimportant as it was, had been given in such a downright\
straightforward manner that it had never occurred to me to doubt her\
sincerity. Still, I had a great respect for Poirot\'92s sagacity\'97except on\
the occasions when he was what I described to myself as \'93foolishly\
pig-headed.\'94\
\
\'93Do you really think so?\'94 I asked. \'93Miss Howard had always seemed to me\
so essentially honest\'97almost uncomfortably so.\'94\
\
Poirot gave me a curious look, which I could not quite fathom. He\
seemed to speak, and then checked himself.\
\
\'93Miss Murdoch too,\'94 I continued, \'93there\'92s nothing untruthful about\
_her_.\'94\
\
\'93No. But it was strange that she never heard a sound, sleeping next\
door; whereas Mrs. Cavendish, in the other wing of the building,\
distinctly heard the table fall.\'94\
\
\'93Well, she\'92s young. And she sleeps soundly.\'94\
\
\'93Ah, yes, indeed! She must be a famous sleeper, that one!\'94\
\
I did not quite like the tone of his voice, but at that moment a smart\
knock reached our ears, and looking out of the window we perceived the\
two detectives waiting for us below.\
\
Poirot seized his hat, gave a ferocious twist to his moustache, and,\
carefully brushing an imaginary speck of dust from his sleeve, motioned\
me to precede him down the stairs; there we joined the detectives and\
set out for Styles.\
\
I think the appearance of the two Scotland Yard men was rather a\
shock\'97especially to John, though of course after the verdict, he had\
realized that it was only a matter of time. Still, the presence of the\
detectives brought the truth home to him more than anything else could\
have done.\
\
Poirot had conferred with Japp in a low tone on the way up, and it was\
the latter functionary who requested that the household, with the\
exception of the servants, should be assembled together in the\
drawing-room. I realized the significance of this. It was up to Poirot\
to make his boast good.\
\
Personally, I was not sanguine. Poirot might have excellent reasons for\
his belief in Inglethorp\'92s innocence, but a man of the type of\
Summerhaye would require tangible proofs, and these I doubted if Poirot\
could supply.\
\
Before very long we had all trooped into the drawing-room, the door of\
which Japp closed. Poirot politely set chairs for everyone. The\
Scotland Yard men were the cynosure of all eyes. I think that for the\
first time we realized that the thing was not a bad dream, but a\
tangible reality. We had read of such things\'97now we ourselves were\
actors in the drama. To-morrow the daily papers, all over England,\
would blazon out the news in staring headlines:\
\
\'93MYSTERIOUS TRAGEDY IN ESSEX\'94\
\
    \'93WEALTHY LADY POISONED\'94\
\
\
There would be pictures of Styles, snap-shots of \'93The family leaving\
the Inquest\'94\'97the village photographer had not been idle! All the things\
that one had read a hundred times\'97things that happen to other people,\
not to oneself. And now, in this house, a murder had been committed. In\
front of us were \'93the detectives in charge of the case.\'94 The well-known\
glib phraseology passed rapidly through my mind in the interval before\
Poirot opened the proceedings.\
\
I think everyone was a little surprised that it should be he and not\
one of the official detectives who took the initiative.\
\
\'93_Mesdames_ and _messieurs_,\'94 said Poirot, bowing as though he were a\
celebrity about to deliver a lecture, \'93I have asked you to come here\
all together, for a certain object. That object, it concerns Mr. Alfred\
Inglethorp.\'94\
\
Inglethorp was sitting a little by himself\'97I think, unconsciously,\
everyone had drawn his chair slightly away from him\'97and he gave a faint\
start as Poirot pronounced his name.\
\
\'93Mr. Inglethorp,\'94 said Poirot, addressing him directly, \'93a very dark\
shadow is resting on this house\'97the shadow of murder.\'94\
\
Inglethorp shook his head sadly.\
\
\'93My poor wife,\'94 he murmured. \'93Poor Emily! It is terrible.\'94\
\
\'93I do not think, monsieur,\'94 said Poirot pointedly, \'93that you quite\
realize how terrible it may be\'97for you.\'94 And as Inglethorp did not\
appear to understand, he added: \'93Mr. Inglethorp, you are standing in\
very grave danger.\'94\
\
The two detectives fidgeted. I saw the official caution \'93Anything you\
say will be used in evidence against you,\'94 actually hovering on\
Summerhaye\'92s lips. Poirot went on.\
\
\'93Do you understand now, monsieur?\'94\
\
\'93No. What do you mean?\'94\
\
\'93I mean,\'94 said Poirot deliberately, \'93that you are suspected of\
poisoning your wife.\'94\
\
A little gasp ran round the circle at this plain speaking.\
\
\'93Good heavens!\'94 cried Inglethorp, starting up. \'93What a monstrous idea!\
_I_\'97poison my dearest Emily!\'94\
\
\'93I do not think\'94\'97Poirot watched him narrowly\'97\'93that you quite realize\
the unfavourable nature of your evidence at the inquest. Mr.\
Inglethorp, knowing what I have now told you, do you still refuse to\
say where you were at six o\'92clock on Monday afternoon?\'94\
\
With a groan, Alfred Inglethorp sank down again and buried his face in\
his hands. Poirot approached and stood over him.\
\
\'93Speak!\'94 he cried menacingly.\
\
With an effort, Inglethorp raised his face from his hands. Then, slowly\
and deliberately, he shook his head.\
\
\'93You will not speak?\'94\
\
\'93No. I do not believe that anyone could be so monstrous as to accuse me\
of what you say.\'94\
\
Poirot nodded thoughtfully, like a man whose mind is made up.\
\
\'93_Soit!_\'94 he said. \'93Then I must speak for you.\'94\
\
Alfred Inglethorp sprang up again.\
\
\'93You? How can you speak? You do not know\'97\'97\'94 he broke off abruptly.\
\
Poirot turned to face us. \'93_Mesdames_ and _messieurs_! I speak! Listen!\
I, Hercule Poirot, affirm that the man who entered the chemist\'92s shop,\
and purchased strychnine at six o\'92clock on Monday last was not Mr.\
Inglethorp, for at six o\'92clock on that day Mr. Inglethorp was escorting\
Mrs. Raikes back to her home from a neighbouring farm. I can produce no\
less than five witnesses to swear to having seen them together, either\
at six or just after and, as you may know, the Abbey Farm, Mrs.\
Raikes\'92s home, is at least two and a half miles distant from the\
village. There is absolutely no question as to the alibi!\'94\
\
\
\
\
\
\
CHAPTER VIII. FRESH SUSPICIONS\
\
There was a moment\'92s stupefied silence. Japp, who was the least\
surprised of any of us, was the first to speak.\
\
\'93My word,\'94 he cried, \'93you\'92re the goods! And no mistake, Mr. Poirot!\
These witnesses of yours are all right, I suppose?\'94\
\
\'93_Voil\'e0!_ I have prepared a list of them\'97names and addresses. You must\
see them, of course. But you will find it all right.\'94\
\
\'93I\'92m sure of that.\'94 Japp lowered his voice. \'93I\'92m much obliged to you. A\
pretty mare\'92s nest arresting him would have been.\'94 He turned to\
Inglethorp. \'93But, if you\'92ll excuse me, sir, why couldn\'92t you say all\
this at the inquest?\'94\
\
\'93I will tell you why,\'94 interrupted Poirot. \'93There was a certain\
rumour\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93A most malicious and utterly untrue one,\'94 interrupted Alfred\
Inglethorp in an agitated voice.\
\
\'93And Mr. Inglethorp was anxious to have no scandal revived just at\
present. Am I right?\'94\
\
\'93Quite right.\'94 Inglethorp nodded. \'93With my poor Emily not yet buried,\
can you wonder I was anxious that no more lying rumours should be\
started.\'94\
\
\'93Between you and me, sir,\'94 remarked Japp, \'93I\'92d sooner have any amount\
of rumours than be arrested for murder. And I venture to think your\
poor lady would have felt the same. And, if it hadn\'92t been for Mr.\
Poirot here, arrested you would have been, as sure as eggs is eggs!\'94\
\
\'93I was foolish, no doubt,\'94 murmured Inglethorp. \'93But you do not know,\
inspector, how I have been persecuted and maligned.\'94 And he shot a\
baleful glance at Evelyn Howard.\
\
\'93Now, sir,\'94 said Japp, turning briskly to John, \'93I should like to see\
the lady\'92s bedroom, please, and after that I\'92ll have a little chat with\
the servants. Don\'92t you bother about anything. Mr. Poirot, here, will\
show me the way.\'94\
\
As they all went out of the room, Poirot turned and made me a sign to\
follow him upstairs. There he caught me by the arm, and drew me aside.\
\
\'93Quick, go to the other wing. Stand there\'97just this side of the baize\
door. Do not move till I come.\'94 Then, turning rapidly, he rejoined the\
two detectives.\
\
I followed his instructions, taking up my position by the baize door,\
and wondering what on earth lay behind the request. Why was I to stand\
in this particular spot on guard? I looked thoughtfully down the\
corridor in front of me. An idea struck me. With the exception of\
Cynthia Murdoch\'92s, everyone\'92s room was in this left wing. Had that\
anything to do with it? Was I to report who came or went? I stood\
faithfully at my post. The minutes passed. Nobody came. Nothing\
happened.\
\
It must have been quite twenty minutes before Poirot rejoined me.\
\
\'93You have not stirred?\'94\
\
\'93No, I\'92ve stuck here like a rock. Nothing\'92s happened.\'94\
\
\'93Ah!\'94 Was he pleased, or disappointed? \'93You\'92ve seen nothing at all?\'94\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
\'93But you have probably heard something? A big bump\'97eh, _mon ami?_\'94\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
\'93Is it possible? Ah, but I am vexed with myself! I am not usually\
clumsy. I made but a slight gesture\'94\'97I know Poirot\'92s gestures\'97\'93with the\
left hand, and over went the table by the bed!\'94\
\
He looked so childishly vexed and crest-fallen that I hastened to\
console him.\
\
\'93Never mind, old chap. What does it matter? Your triumph downstairs\
excited you. I can tell you, that was a surprise to us all. There must\
be more in this affair of Inglethorp\'92s with Mrs. Raikes than we\
thought, to make him hold his tongue so persistently. What are you\
going to do now? Where are the Scotland Yard fellows?\'94\
\
\'93Gone down to interview the servants. I showed them all our exhibits. I\
am disappointed in Japp. He has no method!\'94\
\
\'93Hullo!\'94 I said, looking out of the window. \'93Here\'92s Dr. Bauerstein. I\
believe you\'92re right about that man, Poirot. I don\'92t like him.\'94\
\
\'93He is clever,\'94 observed Poirot meditatively.\
\
\'93Oh, clever as the devil! I must say I was overjoyed to see him in the\
plight he was in on Tuesday. You never saw such a spectacle!\'94 And I\
described the doctor\'92s adventure. \'93He looked a regular scarecrow!\
Plastered with mud from head to foot.\'94\
\
\'93You saw him, then?\'94\
\
\'93Yes. Of course, he didn\'92t want to come in\'97it was just after dinner\'97but\
Mr. Inglethorp insisted.\'94\
\
\'93What?\'94 Poirot caught me violently by the shoulders. \'93Was Dr.\
Bauerstein here on Tuesday evening? Here? And you never told me? Why\
did you not tell me? Why? Why?\'94\
\
He appeared to be in an absolute frenzy.\
\
\'93My dear Poirot,\'94 I expostulated, \'93I never thought it would interest\
you. I didn\'92t know it was of any importance.\'94\
\
\'93Importance? It is of the first importance! So Dr. Bauerstein was here\
on Tuesday night\'97the night of the murder. Hastings, do you not see?\
That alters everything\'97everything!\'94\
\
I had never seen him so upset. Loosening his hold of me, he\
mechanically straightened a pair of candlesticks, still murmuring to\
himself: \'93Yes, that alters everything\'97everything.\'94\
\
Suddenly he seemed to come to a decision.\
\
\'93_Allons!_\'94 he said. \'93We must act at once. Where is Mr. Cavendish?\'94\
\
John was in the smoking-room. Poirot went straight to him.\
\
\'93Mr. Cavendish, I have some important business in Tadminster. A new\
clue. May I take your motor?\'94\
\
\'93Why, of course. Do you mean at once?\'94\
\
\'93If you please.\'94\
\
John rang the bell, and ordered round the car. In another ten minutes,\
we were racing down the park and along the high road to Tadminster.\
\
\'93Now, Poirot,\'94 I remarked resignedly, \'93perhaps you will tell me what\
all this is about?\'94\
\
\'93Well, _mon ami_, a good deal you can guess for yourself. Of course you\
realize that, now Mr. Inglethorp is out of it, the whole position is\
greatly changed. We are face to face with an entirely new problem. We\
know now that there is one person who did not buy the poison. We have\
cleared away the manufactured clues. Now for the real ones. I have\
ascertained that anyone in the household, with the exception of Mrs.\
Cavendish, who was playing tennis with you, could have personated Mr.\
Inglethorp on Monday evening. In the same way, we have his statement\
that he put the coffee down in the hall. No one took much notice of\
that at the inquest\'97but now it has a very different significance. We\
must find out who did take that coffee to Mrs. Inglethorp eventually,\
or who passed through the hall whilst it was standing there. From your\
account, there are only two people whom we can positively say did not\
go near the coffee\'97Mrs. Cavendish, and Mademoiselle Cynthia.\'94\
\
\'93Yes, that is so.\'94 I felt an inexpressible lightening of the heart.\
Mary Cavendish could certainly not rest under suspicion.\
\
\'93In clearing Alfred Inglethorp,\'94 continued Poirot, \'93I have been obliged\
to show my hand sooner than I intended. As long as I might be thought\
to be pursuing him, the criminal would be off his guard. Now, he will\
be doubly careful. Yes\'97doubly careful.\'94 He turned to me abruptly. \'93Tell\
me, Hastings, you yourself\'97have you no suspicions of anybody?\'94\
\
I hesitated. To tell the truth, an idea, wild and extravagant in\
itself, had once or twice that morning flashed through my brain. I had\
rejected it as absurd, nevertheless it persisted.\
\
\'93You couldn\'92t call it a suspicion,\'94 I murmured. \'93It\'92s so utterly\
foolish.\'94\
\
\'93Come now,\'94 urged Poirot encouragingly. \'93Do not fear. Speak your mind.\
You should always pay attention to your instincts.\'94\
\
\'93Well then,\'94 I blurted out, \'93it\'92s absurd\'97but I suspect Miss Howard of\
not telling all she knows!\'94\
\
\'93Miss Howard?\'94\
\
\'93Yes\'97you\'92ll laugh at me\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93Not at all. Why should I?\'94\
\
\'93I can\'92t help feeling,\'94 I continued blunderingly; \'93that we\'92ve rather\
left her out of the possible suspects, simply on the strength of her\
having been away from the place. But, after all, she was only fifteen\
miles away. A car would do it in half an hour. Can we say positively\
that she was away from Styles on the night of the murder?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, my friend,\'94 said Poirot unexpectedly, \'93we can. One of my first\
actions was to ring up the hospital where she was working.\'94\
\
\'93Well?\'94\
\
\'93Well, I learnt that Miss Howard had been on afternoon duty on Tuesday,\
and that\'97a convoy coming in unexpectedly\'97she had kindly offered to\
remain on night duty, which offer was gratefully accepted. That\
disposes of that.\'94\
\
\'93Oh!\'94 I said, rather nonplussed. \'93Really,\'94 I continued, \'93it\'92s her\
extraordinary vehemence against Inglethorp that started me off\
suspecting her. I can\'92t help feeling she\'92d do anything against him. And\
I had an idea she might know something about the destroying of the\
will. She might have burnt the new one, mistaking it for the earlier\
one in his favour. She is so terribly bitter against him.\'94\
\
\'93You consider her vehemence unnatural?\'94\
\
\'93Y\'97es. She is so very violent. I wondered really whether she is quite\
sane on that point.\'94\
\
Poirot shook his head energetically.\
\
\'93No, no, you are on a wrong tack there. There is nothing weak-minded or\
degenerate about Miss Howard. She is an excellent specimen of\
well-balanced English beef and brawn. She is sanity itself.\'94\
\
\'93Yet her hatred of Inglethorp seems almost a mania. My idea was\'97a very\
ridiculous one, no doubt\'97that she had intended to poison him\'97and that,\
in some way, Mrs. Inglethorp got hold of it by mistake. But I don\'92t at\
all see how it could have been done. The whole thing is absurd and\
ridiculous to the last degree.\'94\
\
\'93Still you are right in one thing. It is always wise to suspect\
everybody until you can prove logically, and to your own satisfaction,\
that they are innocent. Now, what reasons are there against Miss\
Howard\'92s having deliberately poisoned Mrs. Inglethorp?\'94\
\
\'93Why, she was devoted to her!\'94 I exclaimed.\
\
\'93Tcha! Tcha!\'94 cried Poirot irritably. \'93You argue like a child. If Miss\
Howard were capable of poisoning the old lady, she would be quite\
equally capable of simulating devotion. No, we must look elsewhere. You\
are perfectly correct in your assumption that her vehemence against\
Alfred Inglethorp is too violent to be natural; but you are quite wrong\
in the deduction you draw from it. I have drawn my own deductions,\
which I believe to be correct, but I will not speak of them at\
present.\'94 He paused a minute, then went on. \'93Now, to my way of\
thinking, there is one insuperable objection to Miss Howard\'92s being the\
murderess.\'94\
\
\'93And that is?\'94\
\
\'93That in no possible way could Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s death benefit Miss\
Howard. Now there is no murder without a motive.\'94\
\
I reflected.\
\
\'93Could not Mrs. Inglethorp have made a will in her favour?\'94\
\
Poirot shook his head.\
\
\'93But you yourself suggested that possibility to Mr. Wells?\'94\
\
Poirot smiled.\
\
\'93That was for a reason. I did not want to mention the name of the\
person who was actually in my mind. Miss Howard occupied very much the\
same position, so I used her name instead.\'94\
\
\'93Still, Mrs. Inglethorp might have done so. Why, that will, made on the\
afternoon of her death may\'97\'97\'94\
\
But Poirot\'92s shake of the head was so energetic that I stopped.\
\
\'93No, my friend. I have certain little ideas of my own about that will.\
But I can tell you this much\'97it was not in Miss Howard\'92s favour.\'94\
\
I accepted his assurance, though I did not really see how he could be\
so positive about the matter.\
\
\'93Well,\'94 I said, with a sigh, \'93we will acquit Miss Howard, then. It is\
partly your fault that I ever came to suspect her. It was what you said\
about her evidence at the inquest that set me off.\'94\
\
Poirot looked puzzled.\
\
\'93What did I say about her evidence at the inquest?\'94\
\
\'93Don\'92t you remember? When I cited her and John Cavendish as being above\
suspicion?\'94\
\
\'93Oh\'97ah\'97yes.\'94 He seemed a little confused, but recovered himself. \'93By\
the way, Hastings, there is something I want you to do for me.\'94\
\
\'93Certainly. What is it?\'94\
\
\'93Next time you happen to be alone with Lawrence Cavendish, I want you\
to say this to him. \'91I have a message for you, from Poirot. He says:\
\'93Find the extra coffee-cup, and you can rest in peace!\'94\'92 Nothing more.\
Nothing less.\'94\
\
\'93\'91Find the extra coffee-cup, and you can rest in peace.\'92 Is that\
right?\'94 I asked, much mystified.\
\
\'93Excellent.\'94\
\
\'93But what does it mean?\'94\
\
\'93Ah, that I will leave you to find out. You have access to the facts.\
Just say that to him, and see what he says.\'94\
\
\'93Very well\'97but it\'92s all extremely mysterious.\'94\
\
We were running into Tadminster now, and Poirot directed the car to the\
\'93Analytical Chemist.\'94\
\
Poirot hopped down briskly, and went inside. In a few minutes he was\
back again.\
\
\'93There,\'94 he said. \'93That is all my business.\'94\
\
\'93What were you doing there?\'94 I asked, in lively curiosity.\
\
\'93I left something to be analysed.\'94\
\
\'93Yes, but what?\'94\
\
\'93The sample of cocoa I took from the saucepan in the bedroom.\'94\
\
\'93But that has already been tested!\'94 I cried, stupefied. \'93Dr. Bauerstein\
had it tested, and you yourself laughed at the possibility of there\
being strychnine in it.\'94\
\
\'93I know Dr. Bauerstein had it tested,\'94 replied Poirot quietly.\
\
\'93Well, then?\'94\
\
\'93Well, I have a fancy for having it analysed again, that is all.\'94\
\
And not another word on the subject could I drag out of him.\
\
This proceeding of Poirot\'92s, in respect of the cocoa, puzzled me\
intensely. I could see neither rhyme nor reason in it. However, my\
confidence in him, which at one time had rather waned, was fully\
restored since his belief in Alfred Inglethorp\'92s innocence had been so\
triumphantly vindicated.\
\
The funeral of Mrs. Inglethorp took place the following day, and on\
Monday, as I came down to a late breakfast, John drew me aside, and\
informed me that Mr. Inglethorp was leaving that morning, to take up\
his quarters at the Stylites Arms until he should have completed his\
plans.\
\
\'93And really it\'92s a great relief to think he\'92s going, Hastings,\'94\
continued my honest friend. \'93It was bad enough before, when we thought\
he\'92d done it, but I\'92m hanged if it isn\'92t worse now, when we all feel\
guilty for having been so down on the fellow. The fact is, we\'92ve\
treated him abominably. Of course, things did look black against him. I\
don\'92t see how anyone could blame us for jumping to the conclusions we\
did. Still, there it is, we were in the wrong, and now there\'92s a\
beastly feeling that one ought to make amends; which is difficult, when\
one doesn\'92t like the fellow a bit better than one did before. The whole\
thing\'92s damned awkward! And I\'92m thankful he\'92s had the tact to take\
himself off. It\'92s a good thing Styles wasn\'92t the mater\'92s to leave to\
him. Couldn\'92t bear to think of the fellow lording it here. He\'92s welcome\
to her money.\'94\
\
\'93You\'92ll be able to keep up the place all right?\'94 I asked.\
\
\'93Oh, yes. There are the death duties, of course, but half my father\'92s\
money goes with the place, and Lawrence will stay with us for the\
present, so there is his share as well. We shall be pinched at first,\
of course, because, as I once told you, I am in a bit of a hole\
financially myself. Still, the Johnnies will wait now.\'94\
\
In the general relief at Inglethorp\'92s approaching departure, we had the\
most genial breakfast we had experienced since the tragedy. Cynthia,\
whose young spirits were naturally buoyant, was looking quite her\
pretty self again, and we all, with the exception of Lawrence, who\
seemed unalterably gloomy and nervous, were quietly cheerful, at the\
opening of a new and hopeful future.\
\
The papers, of course, had been full of the tragedy. Glaring headlines,\
sandwiched biographies of every member of the household, subtle\
innuendoes, the usual familiar tag about the police having a clue.\
Nothing was spared us. It was a slack time. The war was momentarily\
inactive, and the newspapers seized with avidity on this crime in\
fashionable life: \'93The Mysterious Affair at Styles\'94 was the topic of\
the moment.\
\
Naturally it was very annoying for the Cavendishes. The house was\
constantly besieged by reporters, who were consistently denied\
admission, but who continued to haunt the village and the grounds,\
where they lay in wait with cameras, for any unwary members of the\
household. We all lived in a blast of publicity. The Scotland Yard men\
came and went, examining, questioning, lynx-eyed and reserved of\
tongue. Towards what end they were working, we did not know. Had they\
any clue, or would the whole thing remain in the category of\
undiscovered crimes?\
\
After breakfast, Dorcas came up to me rather mysteriously, and asked if\
she might have a few words with me.\
\
\'93Certainly. What is it, Dorcas?\'94\
\
\'93Well, it\'92s just this, sir. You\'92ll be seeing the Belgian gentleman\
to-day perhaps?\'94 I nodded. \'93Well, sir, you know how he asked me so\
particular if the mistress, or anyone else, had a green dress?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, yes. You have found one?\'94 My interest was aroused.\
\
\'93No, not that, sir. But since then I\'92ve remembered what the young\
gentlemen\'94\'97John and Lawrence were still the \'93young gentlemen\'94 to\
Dorcas\'97\'93call the \'91dressing-up box.\'92 It\'92s up in the front attic, sir. A\
great chest, full of old clothes and fancy dresses, and what not. And\
it came to me sudden like that there might be a green dress amongst\
them. So, if you\'92d tell the Belgian gentleman\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93I will tell him, Dorcas,\'94 I promised.\
\
\'93Thank you very much, sir. A very nice gentleman he is, sir. And quite\
a different class from them two detectives from London, what goes\
prying about, and asking questions. I don\'92t hold with foreigners as a\
rule, but from what the newspapers say I make out as how these brave\
Belges isn\'92t the ordinary run of foreigners, and certainly he\'92s a most\
polite spoken gentleman.\'94\
\
Dear old Dorcas! As she stood there, with her honest face upturned to\
mine, I thought what a fine specimen she was of the old-fashioned\
servant that is so fast dying out.\
\
I thought I might as well go down to the village at once, and look up\
Poirot; but I met him half-way, coming up to the house, and at once\
gave him Dorcas\'92s message.\
\
\'93Ah, the brave Dorcas! We will look at the chest, although\'97but no\
matter\'97we will examine it all the same.\'94\
\
We entered the house by one of the windows. There was no one in the\
hall, and we went straight up to the attic.\
\
Sure enough, there was the chest, a fine old piece, all studded with\
brass nails, and full to overflowing with every imaginable type of\
garment.\
\
Poirot bundled everything out on the floor with scant ceremony. There\
were one or two green fabrics of varying shades; but Poirot shook his\
head over them all. He seemed somewhat apathetic in the search, as\
though he expected no great results from it. Suddenly he gave an\
exclamation.\
\
\'93What is it?\'94\
\
\'93Look!\'94\
\
The chest was nearly empty, and there, reposing right at the bottom,\
was a magnificent black beard.\
\
\'93_Oh\'f3!_\'94 said Poirot. \'93_Oh\'f3!_\'94 He turned it over in his hands,\
examining it closely. \'93New,\'94 he remarked. \'93Yes, quite new.\'94\
\
After a moment\'92s hesitation, he replaced it in the chest, heaped all\
the other things on top of it as before, and made his way briskly\
downstairs. He went straight to the pantry, where we found Dorcas\
busily polishing her silver.\
\
Poirot wished her good morning with Gallic politeness, and went on:\
\
\'93We have been looking through that chest, Dorcas. I am much obliged to\
you for mentioning it. There is, indeed, a fine collection there. Are\
they often used, may I ask?\'94\
\
\'93Well, sir, not very often nowadays, though from time to time we do\
have what the young gentlemen call \'91a dress-up night.\'92 And very funny\
it is sometimes, sir. Mr. Lawrence, he\'92s wonderful. Most comic! I shall\
never forget the night he came down as the Char of Persia, I think he\
called it\'97a sort of Eastern King it was. He had the big paper knife in\
his hand, and \'91Mind, Dorcas,\'92 he says, \'91you\'92ll have to be very\
respectful. This is my specially sharpened scimitar, and it\'92s off with\
your head if I\'92m at all displeased with you!\'92 Miss Cynthia, she was\
what they call an Apache, or some such name\'97a Frenchified sort of\
cut-throat, I take it to be. A real sight she looked. You\'92d never have\
believed a pretty young lady like that could have made herself into\
such a ruffian. Nobody would have known her.\'94\
\
\'93These evenings must have been great fun,\'94 said Poirot genially. \'93I\
suppose Mr. Lawrence wore that fine black beard in the chest upstairs,\
when he was Shah of Persia?\'94\
\
\'93He did have a beard, sir,\'94 replied Dorcas, smiling. \'93And well I know\
it, for he borrowed two skeins of my black wool to make it with! And\
I\'92m sure it looked wonderfully natural at a distance. I didn\'92t know as\
there was a beard up there at all. It must have been got quite lately,\
I think. There was a red wig, I know, but nothing else in the way of\
hair. Burnt corks they use mostly\'97though \'91tis messy getting it off\
again. Miss Cynthia was a nigger once, and, oh, the trouble she had.\'94\
\
\'93So Dorcas knows nothing about that black beard,\'94 said Poirot\
thoughtfully, as we walked out into the hall again.\
\
\'93Do you think it is _the_ one?\'94 I whispered eagerly.\
\
Poirot nodded.\
\
\'93I do. You notice it had been trimmed?\'94\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
\'93Yes. It was cut exactly the shape of Mr. Inglethorp\'92s, and I found one\
or two snipped hairs. Hastings, this affair is very deep.\'94\
\
\'93Who put it in the chest, I wonder?\'94\
\
\'93Someone with a good deal of intelligence,\'94 remarked Poirot dryly. \'93You\
realize that he chose the one place in the house to hide it where its\
presence would not be remarked? Yes, he is intelligent. But we must be\
more intelligent. We must be so intelligent that he does not suspect us\
of being intelligent at all.\'94\
\
I acquiesced.\
\
\'93There, _mon ami_, you will be of great assistance to me.\'94\
\
I was pleased with the compliment. There had been times when I hardly\
thought that Poirot appreciated me at my true worth.\
\
\'93Yes,\'94 he continued, staring at me thoughtfully, \'93you will be\
invaluable.\'94\
\
This was naturally gratifying, but Poirot\'92s next words were not so\
welcome.\
\
\'93I must have an ally in the house,\'94 he observed reflectively.\
\
\'93You have me,\'94 I protested.\
\
\'93True, but you are not sufficient.\'94\
\
I was hurt, and showed it. Poirot hurried to explain himself.\
\
\'93You do not quite take my meaning. You are known to be working with me.\
I want somebody who is not associated with us in any way.\'94\
\
\'93Oh, I see. How about John?\'94\
\
\'93No, I think not.\'94\
\
\'93The dear fellow isn\'92t perhaps very bright,\'94 I said thoughtfully.\
\
\'93Here comes Miss Howard,\'94 said Poirot suddenly. \'93She is the very\
person. But I am in her black books, since I cleared Mr. Inglethorp.\
Still, we can but try.\'94\
\
With a nod that was barely civil, Miss Howard assented to Poirot\'92s\
request for a few minutes\'92 conversation.\
\
We went into the little morning-room, and Poirot closed the door.\
\
\'93Well, Monsieur Poirot,\'94 said Miss Howard impatiently, \'93what is it? Out\
with it. I\'92m busy.\'94\
\
\'93Do you remember, mademoiselle, that I once asked you to help me?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, I do.\'94 The lady nodded. \'93And I told you I\'92d help you with\
pleasure\'97to hang Alfred Inglethorp.\'94\
\
\'93Ah!\'94 Poirot studied her seriously. \'93Miss Howard, I will ask you one\
question. I beg of you to reply to it truthfully.\'94\
\
\'93Never tell lies,\'94 replied Miss Howard.\
\
\'93It is this. Do you still believe that Mrs. Inglethorp was poisoned by\
her husband?\'94\
\
\'93What do you mean?\'94 she asked sharply. \'93You needn\'92t think your pretty\
explanations influence me in the slightest. I\'92ll admit that it wasn\'92t\
he who bought strychnine at the chemist\'92s shop. What of that? I dare\
say he soaked fly paper, as I told you at the beginning.\'94\
\
\'93That is arsenic\'97not strychnine,\'94 said Poirot mildly.\
\
\'93What does that matter? Arsenic would put poor Emily out of the way\
just as well as strychnine. If I\'92m convinced he did it, it doesn\'92t\
matter a jot to me _how_ he did it.\'94\
\
\'93Exactly. _If_ you are convinced he did it,\'94 said Poirot quietly. \'93I\
will put my question in another form. Did you ever in your heart of\
hearts believe that Mrs. Inglethorp was poisoned by her husband?\'94\
\
\'93Good heavens!\'94 cried Miss Howard. \'93Haven\'92t I always told you the man\
is a villain? Haven\'92t I always told you he would murder her in her bed?\
Haven\'92t I always hated him like poison?\'94\
\
\'93Exactly,\'94 said Poirot. \'93That bears out my little idea entirely.\'94\
\
\'93What little idea?\'94\
\
\'93Miss Howard, do you remember a conversation that took place on the day\
of my friend\'92s arrival here? He repeated it to me, and there is a\
sentence of yours that has impressed me very much. Do you remember\
affirming that if a crime had been committed, and anyone you loved had\
been murdered, you felt certain that you would know by instinct who the\
criminal was, even if you were quite unable to prove it?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, I remember saying that. I believe it too. I suppose you think it\
nonsense?\'94\
\
\'93Not at all.\'94\
\
\'93And yet you will pay no attention to my instinct against Alfred\
Inglethorp.\'94\
\
\'93No,\'94 said Poirot curtly. \'93Because your instinct is not against Mr.\
Inglethorp.\'94\
\
\'93What?\'94\
\
\'93No. You wish to believe he committed the crime. You believe him\
capable of committing it. But your instinct tells you he did not commit\
it. It tells you more\'97shall I go on?\'94\
\
She was staring at him, fascinated, and made a slight affirmative\
movement of the hand.\
\
\'93Shall I tell you why you have been so vehement against Mr. Inglethorp?\
It is because you have been trying to believe what you wish to believe.\
It is because you are trying to drown and stifle your instinct, which\
tells you another name\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93No, no, no!\'94 cried Miss Howard wildly, flinging up her hands. \'93Don\'92t\
say it! Oh, don\'92t say it! It isn\'92t true! It can\'92t be true. I don\'92t know\
what put such a wild\'97such a dreadful\'97idea into my head!\'94\
\
\'93I am right, am I not?\'94 asked Poirot.\
\
\'93Yes, yes; you must be a wizard to have guessed. But it can\'92t be\
so\'97it\'92s too monstrous, too impossible. It _must_ be Alfred Inglethorp.\'94\
\
Poirot shook his head gravely.\
\
\'93Don\'92t ask me about it,\'94 continued Miss Howard, \'93because I shan\'92t tell\
you. I won\'92t admit it, even to myself. I must be mad to think of such a\
thing.\'94\
\
Poirot nodded, as if satisfied.\
\
\'93I will ask you nothing. It is enough for me that it is as I thought.\
And I\'97I, too, have an instinct. We are working together towards a\
common end.\'94\
\
\'93Don\'92t ask me to help you, because I won\'92t. I wouldn\'92t lift a finger\
to\'97to\'97\'97\'94 She faltered.\
\
\'93You will help me in spite of yourself. I ask you nothing\'97but you will\
be my ally. You will not be able to help yourself. You will do the only\
thing that I want of you.\'94\
\
\'93And that is?\'94\
\
\'93You will watch!\'94\
\
Evelyn Howard bowed her head.\
\
\'93Yes, I can\'92t help doing that. I am always watching\'97always hoping I\
shall be proved wrong.\'94\
\
\'93If we are wrong, well and good,\'94 said Poirot. \'93No one will be more\
pleased than I shall. But, if we are right? If we are right, Miss\
Howard, on whose side are you then?\'94\
\
\'93I don\'92t know, I don\'92t know\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93Come now.\'94\
\
\'93It could be hushed up.\'94\
\
\'93There must be no hushing up.\'94\
\
\'93But Emily herself\'97\'97\'94 She broke off.\
\
\'93Miss Howard,\'94 said Poirot gravely, \'93this is unworthy of you.\'94\
\
Suddenly she took her face from her hands.\
\
\'93Yes,\'94 she said quietly, \'93that was not Evelyn Howard who spoke!\'94 She\
flung her head up proudly. \'93_This_ is Evelyn Howard! And she is on the\
side of Justice! Let the cost be what it may.\'94 And with these words,\
she walked firmly out of the room.\
\
\'93There,\'94 said Poirot, looking after her, \'93goes a very valuable ally.\
That woman, Hastings, has got brains as well as a heart.\'94\
\
I did not reply.\
\
\'93Instinct is a marvellous thing,\'94 mused Poirot. \'93It can neither be\
explained nor ignored.\'94\
\
\'93You and Miss Howard seem to know what you are talking about,\'94 I\
observed coldly. \'93Perhaps you don\'92t realize that _I_ am still in the\
dark.\'94\
\
\'93Really? Is that so, _mon ami?_\'94\
\
\'93Yes. Enlighten me, will you?\'94\
\
Poirot studied me attentively for a moment or two. Then, to my intense\
surprise, he shook his head decidedly.\
\
\'93No, my friend.\'94\
\
\'93Oh, look here, why not?\'94\
\
\'93Two is enough for a secret.\'94\
\
\'93Well, I think it is very unfair to keep back facts from me.\'94\
\
\'93I am not keeping back facts. Every fact that I know is in your\
possession. You can draw your own deductions from them. This time it is\
a question of ideas.\'94\
\
\'93Still, it would be interesting to know.\'94\
\
Poirot looked at me very earnestly, and again shook his head.\
\
\'93You see,\'94 he said sadly, \'93_you_ have no instincts.\'94\
\
\'93It was intelligence you were requiring just now,\'94 I pointed out.\
\
\'93The two often go together,\'94 said Poirot enigmatically.\
\
The remark seemed so utterly irrelevant that I did not even take the\
trouble to answer it. But I decided that if I made any interesting and\
important discoveries\'97as no doubt I should\'97I would keep them to myself,\
and surprise Poirot with the ultimate result.\
\
There are times when it is one\'92s duty to assert oneself.\
\
\
\
\
\
\
CHAPTER IX. DR. BAUERSTEIN\
\
I had had no opportunity as yet of passing on Poirot\'92s message to\
Lawrence. But now, as I strolled out on the lawn, still nursing a\
grudge against my friend\'92s high-handedness, I saw Lawrence on the\
croquet lawn, aimlessly knocking a couple of very ancient balls about,\
with a still more ancient mallet.\
\
It struck me that it would be a good opportunity to deliver my message.\
Otherwise, Poirot himself might relieve me of it. It was true that I\
did not quite gather its purport, but I flattered myself that by\
Lawrence\'92s reply, and perhaps a little skillful cross-examination on my\
part, I should soon perceive its significance. Accordingly I accosted\
him.\
\
\'93I\'92ve been looking for you,\'94 I remarked untruthfully.\
\
\'93Have you?\'94\
\
\'93Yes. The truth is, I\'92ve got a message for you\'97from Poirot.\'94\
\
\'93Yes?\'94\
\
\'93He told me to wait until I was alone with you,\'94 I said, dropping my\
voice significantly, and watching him intently out of the corner of my\
eye. I have always been rather good at what is called, I believe,\
creating an atmosphere.\
\
\'93Well?\'94\
\
There was no change of expression in the dark melancholic face. Had he\
any idea of what I was about to say?\
\
\'93This is the message.\'94 I dropped my voice still lower. \'93\'91Find the extra\
coffee-cup, and you can rest in peace.\'92\'94\
\
\'93What on earth does he mean?\'94 Lawrence stared at me in quite unaffected\
astonishment.\
\
\'93Don\'92t you know?\'94\
\
\'93Not in the least. Do you?\'94\
\
I was compelled to shake my head.\
\
\'93What extra coffee-cup?\'94\
\
\'93I don\'92t know.\'94\
\
\'93He\'92d better ask Dorcas, or one of the maids, if he wants to know about\
coffee-cups. It\'92s their business, not mine. I don\'92t know anything about\
the coffee-cups, except that we\'92ve got some that are never used, which\
are a perfect dream! Old Worcester. You\'92re not a connoisseur, are you,\
Hastings?\'94\
\
I shook my head.\
\
\'93You miss a lot. A really perfect bit of old china\'97it\'92s pure delight to\
handle it, or even to look at it.\'94\
\
\'93Well, what am I to tell Poirot?\'94\
\
\'93Tell him I don\'92t know what he\'92s talking about. It\'92s double Dutch to\
me.\'94\
\
\'93All right.\'94\
\
I was moving off towards the house again when he suddenly called me\
back.\
\
\'93I say, what was the end of that message? Say it over again, will you?\'94\
\
\'93\'91Find the extra coffee-cup, and you can rest in peace.\'92 Are you sure\
you don\'92t know what it means?\'94 I asked him earnestly.\
\
He shook his head.\
\
\'93No,\'94 he said musingly, \'93I don\'92t. I\'97I wish I did.\'94\
\
The boom of the gong sounded from the house, and we went in together.\
Poirot had been asked by John to remain to lunch, and was already\
seated at the table.\
\
By tacit consent, all mention of the tragedy was barred. We conversed\
on the war, and other outside topics. But after the cheese and biscuits\
had been handed round, and Dorcas had left the room, Poirot suddenly\
leant forward to Mrs. Cavendish.\
\
\'93Pardon me, madame, for recalling unpleasant memories, but I have a\
little idea\'94\'97Poirot\'92s \'93little ideas\'94 were becoming a perfect\
byword\'97\'93and would like to ask one or two questions.\'94\
\
\'93Of me? Certainly.\'94\
\
\'93You are too amiable, madame. What I want to ask is this: the door\
leading into Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s room from that of Mademoiselle Cynthia,\
it was bolted, you say?\'94\
\
\'93Certainly it was bolted,\'94 replied Mary Cavendish, rather surprised. \'93I\
said so at the inquest.\'94\
\
\'93Bolted?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94 She looked perplexed.\
\
\'93I mean,\'94 explained Poirot, \'93you are sure it was bolted, and not merely\
locked?\'94\
\
\'93Oh, I see what you mean. No, I don\'92t know. I said bolted, meaning that\
it was fastened, and I could not open it, but I believe all the doors\
were found bolted on the inside.\'94\
\
\'93Still, as far as you are concerned, the door might equally well have\
been locked?\'94\
\
\'93Oh, yes.\'94\
\
\'93You yourself did not happen to notice, madame, when you entered Mrs.\
Inglethorp\'92s room, whether that door was bolted or not?\'94\
\
\'93I\'97I believe it was.\'94\
\
\'93But you did not see it?\'94\
\
\'93No. I\'97never looked.\'94\
\
\'93But _I_ did,\'94 interrupted Lawrence suddenly. \'93I happened to notice\
that it _was_ bolted.\'94\
\
\'93Ah, that settles it.\'94 And Poirot looked crestfallen.\
\
I could not help rejoicing that, for once, one of his \'93little ideas\'94\
had come to naught.\
\
After lunch Poirot begged me to accompany him home. I consented rather\
stiffly.\
\
\'93You are annoyed, is it not so?\'94 he asked anxiously, as we walked\
through the park.\
\
\'93Not at all,\'94 I said coldly.\
\
\'93That is well. That lifts a great load from my mind.\'94\
\
This was not quite what I had intended. I had hoped that he would have\
observed the stiffness of my manner. Still, the fervour of his words\
went towards the appeasing of my just displeasure. I thawed.\
\
\'93I gave Lawrence your message,\'94 I said.\
\
\'93And what did he say? He was entirely puzzled?\'94\
\
\'93Yes. I am quite sure he had no idea of what you meant.\'94\
\
I had expected Poirot to be disappointed; but, to my surprise, he\
replied that that was as he had thought, and that he was very glad. My\
pride forbade me to ask any questions.\
\
Poirot switched off on another tack.\
\
\'93Mademoiselle Cynthia was not at lunch to-day? How was that?\'94\
\
\'93She is at the hospital again. She resumed work to-day.\'94\
\
\'93Ah, she is an industrious little demoiselle. And pretty too. She is\
like pictures I have seen in Italy. I would rather like to see that\
dispensary of hers. Do you think she would show it to me?\'94\
\
\'93I am sure she would be delighted. It\'92s an interesting little place.\'94\
\
\'93Does she go there every day?\'94\
\
\'93She has all Wednesdays off, and comes back to lunch on Saturdays.\
Those are her only times off.\'94\
\
\'93I will remember. Women are doing great work nowadays, and Mademoiselle\
Cynthia is clever\'97oh, yes, she has brains, that little one.\'94\
\
\'93Yes. I believe she has passed quite a stiff exam.\'94\
\
\'93Without doubt. After all, it is very responsible work. I suppose they\
have very strong poisons there?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, she showed them to us. They are kept locked up in a little\
cupboard. I believe they have to be very careful. They always take out\
the key before leaving the room.\'94\
\
\'93Indeed. It is near the window, this cupboard?\'94\
\
\'93No, right the other side of the room. Why?\'94\
\
Poirot shrugged his shoulders.\
\
\'93I wondered. That is all. Will you come in?\'94\
\
We had reached the cottage.\
\
\'93No. I think I\'92ll be getting back. I shall go round the long way\
through the woods.\'94\
\
The woods round Styles were very beautiful. After the walk across the\
open park, it was pleasant to saunter lazily through the cool glades.\
There was hardly a breath of wind, the very chirp of the birds was\
faint and subdued. I strolled on a little way, and finally flung myself\
down at the foot of a grand old beech-tree. My thoughts of mankind were\
kindly and charitable. I even forgave Poirot for his absurd secrecy. In\
fact, I was at peace with the world. Then I yawned.\
\
I thought about the crime, and it struck me as being very unreal and\
far off.\
\
I yawned again.\
\
Probably, I thought, it really never happened. Of course, it was all a\
bad dream. The truth of the matter was that it was Lawrence who had\
murdered Alfred Inglethorp with a croquet mallet. But it was absurd of\
John to make such a fuss about it, and to go shouting out: \'93I tell you\
I won\'92t have it!\'94\
\
I woke up with a start.\
\
At once I realized that I was in a very awkward predicament. For, about\
twelve feet away from me, John and Mary Cavendish were standing facing\
each other, and they were evidently quarrelling. And, quite as\
evidently, they were unaware of my vicinity, for before I could move or\
speak John repeated the words which had aroused me from my dream.\
\
\'93I tell you, Mary, I won\'92t have it.\'94\
\
Mary\'92s voice came, cool and liquid:\
\
\'93Have _you_ any right to criticize my actions?\'94\
\
\'93It will be the talk of the village! My mother was only buried on\
Saturday, and here you are gadding about with the fellow.\'94\
\
\'93Oh,\'94 she shrugged her shoulders, \'93if it is only village gossip that\
you mind!\'94\
\
\'93But it isn\'92t. I\'92ve had enough of the fellow hanging about. He\'92s a\
Polish Jew, anyway.\'94\
\
\'93A tinge of Jewish blood is not a bad thing. It leavens the\'94\'97she looked\
at him\'97\'93stolid stupidity of the ordinary Englishman.\'94\
\
Fire in her eyes, ice in her voice. I did not wonder that the blood\
rose to John\'92s face in a crimson tide.\
\
\'93Mary!\'94\
\
\'93Well?\'94 Her tone did not change.\
\
The pleading died out of his voice.\
\
\'93Am I to understand that you will continue to see Bauerstein against my\
express wishes?\'94\
\
\'93If I choose.\'94\
\
\'93You defy me?\'94\
\
\'93No, but I deny your right to criticize my actions. Have _you_ no\
friends of whom I should disapprove?\'94\
\
John fell back a pace. The colour ebbed slowly from his face.\
\
\'93What do you mean?\'94 he said, in an unsteady voice.\
\
\'93You see!\'94 said Mary quietly. \'93You _do_ see, don\'92t you, that _you_ have\
no right to dictate to _me_ as to the choice of my friends?\'94\
\
John glanced at her pleadingly, a stricken look on his face.\
\
\'93No right? Have I _no_ right, Mary?\'94 he said unsteadily. He stretched\
out his hands. \'93Mary\'97\'97\'94\
\
For a moment, I thought she wavered. A softer expression came over her\
face, then suddenly she turned almost fiercely away.\
\
\'93None!\'94\
\
She was walking away when John sprang after her, and caught her by the\
arm.\
\
\'93Mary\'94\'97his voice was very quiet now\'97\'93are you in love with this fellow\
Bauerstein?\'94\
\
She hesitated, and suddenly there swept across her face a strange\
expression, old as the hills, yet with something eternally young about\
it. So might some Egyptian sphinx have smiled.\
\
She freed herself quietly from his arm, and spoke over her shoulder.\
\
\'93Perhaps,\'94 she said; and then swiftly passed out of the little glade,\
leaving John standing there as though he had been turned to stone.\
\
Rather ostentatiously, I stepped forward, crackling some dead branches\
with my feet as I did so. John turned. Luckily, he took it for granted\
that I had only just come upon the scene.\
\
\'93Hullo, Hastings. Have you seen the little fellow safely back to his\
cottage? Quaint little chap! Is he any good, though, really?\'94\
\
\'93He was considered one of the finest detectives of his day.\'94\
\
\'93Oh, well, I suppose there must be something in it, then. What a rotten\
world it is, though!\'94\
\
\'93You find it so?\'94 I asked.\
\
\'93Good Lord, yes! There\'92s this terrible business to start with. Scotland\
Yard men in and out of the house like a jack-in-the-box! Never know\
where they won\'92t turn up next. Screaming headlines in every paper in\
the country\'97damn all journalists, I say! Do you know there was a whole\
crowd staring in at the lodge gates this morning. Sort of Madame\
Tussaud\'92s chamber of horrors business that can be seen for nothing.\
Pretty thick, isn\'92t it?\'94\
\
\'93Cheer up, John!\'94 I said soothingly. \'93It can\'92t last for ever.\'94\
\
\'93Can\'92t it, though? It can last long enough for us never to be able to\
hold up our heads again.\'94\
\
\'93No, no, you\'92re getting morbid on the subject.\'94\
\
\'93Enough to make a man morbid, to be stalked by beastly journalists and\
stared at by gaping moon-faced idiots, wherever he goes! But there\'92s\
worse than that.\'94\
\
\'93What?\'94\
\
John lowered his voice:\
\
\'93Have you ever thought, Hastings\'97it\'92s a nightmare to me\'97who did it? I\
can\'92t help feeling sometimes it must have been an accident.\
Because\'97because\'97who could have done it? Now Inglethorp\'92s out of the\
way, there\'92s no one else; no one, I mean, except\'97one of us.\'94\
\
Yes, indeed, that was nightmare enough for any man! One of us? Yes,\
surely it must be so, unless\'97\'97-\
\
A new idea suggested itself to my mind. Rapidly, I considered it. The\
light increased. Poirot\'92s mysterious doings, his hints\'97they all fitted\
in. Fool that I was not to have thought of this possibility before, and\
what a relief for us all.\
\
\'93No, John,\'94 I said, \'93it isn\'92t one of us. How could it be?\'94\
\
\'93I know, but, still, who else is there?\'94\
\
\'93Can\'92t you guess?\'94\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
I looked cautiously round, and lowered my voice.\
\
\'93Dr. Bauerstein!\'94 I whispered.\
\
\'93Impossible!\'94\
\
\'93Not at all.\'94\
\
\'93But what earthly interest could he have in my mother\'92s death?\'94\
\
\'93That I don\'92t see,\'94 I confessed, \'93but I\'92ll tell you this: Poirot thinks\
so.\'94\
\
\'93Poirot? Does he? How do you know?\'94\
\
I told him of Poirot\'92s intense excitement on hearing that Dr.\
Bauerstein had been at Styles on the fatal night, and added:\
\
\'93He said twice: \'91That alters everything.\'92 And I\'92ve been thinking. You\
know Inglethorp said he had put down the coffee in the hall? Well, it\
was just then that Bauerstein arrived. Isn\'92t it possible that, as\
Inglethorp brought him through the hall, the doctor dropped something\
into the coffee in passing?\'94\
\
\'93H\'92m,\'94 said John. \'93It would have been very risky.\'94\
\
\'93Yes, but it was possible.\'94\
\
\'93And then, how could he know it was her coffee? No, old fellow, I don\'92t\
think that will wash.\'94\
\
But I had remembered something else.\
\
\'93You\'92re quite right. That wasn\'92t how it was done. Listen.\'94 And I then\
told him of the cocoa sample which Poirot had taken to be analysed.\
\
John interrupted just as I had done.\
\
\'93But, look here, Bauerstein had had it analysed already?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, yes, that\'92s the point. I didn\'92t see it either until now. Don\'92t\
you understand? Bauerstein had it analysed\'97that\'92s just it! If\
Bauerstein\'92s the murderer, nothing could be simpler than for him to\
substitute some ordinary cocoa for his sample, and send that to be\
tested. And of course they would find no strychnine! But no one would\
dream of suspecting Bauerstein, or think of taking another\
sample\'97except Poirot,\'94 I added, with belated recognition.\
\
\'93Yes, but what about the bitter taste that cocoa won\'92t disguise?\'94\
\
\'93Well, we\'92ve only his word for that. And there are other possibilities.\
He\'92s admittedly one of the world\'92s greatest toxicologists\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93One of the world\'92s greatest what? Say it again.\'94\
\
\'93He knows more about poisons than almost anybody,\'94 I explained. \'93Well,\
my idea is, that perhaps he\'92s found some way of making strychnine\
tasteless. Or it may not have been strychnine at all, but some obscure\
drug no one has ever heard of, which produces much the same symptoms.\'94\
\
\'93H\'92m, yes, that might be,\'94 said John. \'93But look here, how could he have\
got at the cocoa? That wasn\'92t downstairs?\'94\
\
\'93No, it wasn\'92t,\'94 I admitted reluctantly.\
\
And then, suddenly, a dreadful possibility flashed through my mind. I\
hoped and prayed it would not occur to John also. I glanced sideways at\
him. He was frowning perplexedly, and I drew a deep breath of relief,\
for the terrible thought that had flashed across my mind was this: that\
Dr. Bauerstein might have had an accomplice.\
\
Yet surely it could not be! Surely no woman as beautiful as Mary\
Cavendish could be a murderess. Yet beautiful women had been known to\
poison.\
\
And suddenly I remembered that first conversation at tea on the day of\
my arrival, and the gleam in her eyes as she had said that poison was a\
woman\'92s weapon. How agitated she had been on that fatal Tuesday\
evening! Had Mrs. Inglethorp discovered something between her and\
Bauerstein, and threatened to tell her husband? Was it to stop that\
denunciation that the crime had been committed?\
\
Then I remembered that enigmatical conversation between Poirot and\
Evelyn Howard. Was this what they had meant? Was this the monstrous\
possibility that Evelyn had tried not to believe?\
\
Yes, it all fitted in.\
\
No wonder Miss Howard had suggested \'93hushing it up.\'94 Now I understood\
that unfinished sentence of hers: \'93Emily herself\'97\'97\'94 And in my heart I\
agreed with her. Would not Mrs. Inglethorp have preferred to go\
unavenged rather than have such terrible dishonour fall upon the name\
of Cavendish.\
\
\'93There\'92s another thing,\'94 said John suddenly, and the unexpected sound\
of his voice made me start guiltily. \'93Something which makes me doubt if\
what you say can be true.\'94\
\
\'93What\'92s that?\'94 I asked, thankful that he had gone away from the subject\
of how the poison could have been introduced into the cocoa.\
\
\'93Why, the fact that Bauerstein demanded a post-mortem. He needn\'92t have\
done so. Little Wilkins would have been quite content to let it go at\
heart disease.\'94\
\
\'93Yes,\'94 I said doubtfully. \'93But we don\'92t know. Perhaps he thought it\
safer in the long run. Someone might have talked afterwards. Then the\
Home Office might have ordered exhumation. The whole thing would have\
come out, then, and he would have been in an awkward position, for no\
one would have believed that a man of his reputation could have been\
deceived into calling it heart disease.\'94\
\
\'93Yes, that\'92s possible,\'94 admitted John. \'93Still,\'94 he added, \'93I\'92m blest if\
I can see what his motive could have been.\'94\
\
I trembled.\
\
\'93Look here,\'94 I said, \'93I may be altogether wrong. And, remember, all\
this is in confidence.\'94\
\
\'93Oh, of course\'97that goes without saying.\'94\
\
We had walked, as we talked, and now we passed through the little gate\
into the garden. Voices rose near at hand, for tea was spread out under\
the sycamore-tree, as it had been on the day of my arrival.\
\
Cynthia was back from the hospital, and I placed my chair beside her,\
and told her of Poirot\'92s wish to visit the dispensary.\
\
\'93Of course! I\'92d love him to see it. He\'92d better come to tea there one\
day. I must fix it up with him. He\'92s such a dear little man! But he\
_is_ funny. He made me take the brooch out of my tie the other day, and\
put it in again, because he said it wasn\'92t straight.\'94\
\
I laughed.\
\
\'93It\'92s quite a mania with him.\'94\
\
\'93Yes, isn\'92t it?\'94\
\
We were silent for a minute or two, and then, glancing in the direction\
of Mary Cavendish, and dropping her voice, Cynthia said:\
\
\'93Mr. Hastings.\'94\
\
\'93Yes?\'94\
\
\'93After tea, I want to talk to you.\'94\
\
Her glance at Mary had set me thinking. I fancied that between these\
two there existed very little sympathy. For the first time, it occurred\
to me to wonder about the girl\'92s future. Mrs. Inglethorp had made no\
provisions of any kind for her, but I imagined that John and Mary would\
probably insist on her making her home with them\'97at any rate until the\
end of the war. John, I knew, was very fond of her, and would be sorry\
to let her go.\
\
John, who had gone into the house, now reappeared. His good-natured\
face wore an unaccustomed frown of anger.\
\
\'93Confound those detectives! I can\'92t think what they\'92re after! They\'92ve\
been in every room in the house\'97turning things inside out, and upside\
down. It really is too bad! I suppose they took advantage of our all\
being out. I shall go for that fellow Japp, when I next see him!\'94\
\
\'93Lot of Paul Prys,\'94 grunted Miss Howard.\
\
Lawrence opined that they had to make a show of doing something.\
\
Mary Cavendish said nothing.\
\
After tea, I invited Cynthia to come for a walk, and we sauntered off\
into the woods together.\
\
\'93Well?\'94 I inquired, as soon as we were protected from prying eyes by\
the leafy screen.\
\
With a sigh, Cynthia flung herself down, and tossed off her hat. The\
sunlight, piercing through the branches, turned the auburn of her hair\
to quivering gold.\
\
\'93Mr. Hastings\'97you are always so kind, and you know such a lot.\'94\
\
It struck me at this moment that Cynthia was really a very charming\
girl! Much more charming than Mary, who never said things of that kind.\
\
\'93Well?\'94 I asked benignantly, as she hesitated.\
\
\'93I want to ask your advice. What shall I do?\'94\
\
\'93Do?\'94\
\
\'93Yes. You see, Aunt Emily always told me I should be provided for. I\
suppose she forgot, or didn\'92t think she was likely to die\'97anyway, I am\
_not_ provided for! And I don\'92t know what to do. Do you think I ought\
to go away from here at once?\'94\
\
\'93Good heavens, no! They don\'92t want to part with you, I\'92m sure.\'94\
\
Cynthia hesitated a moment, plucking up the grass with her tiny hands.\
Then she said: \'93Mrs. Cavendish does. She hates me.\'94\
\
\'93Hates you?\'94 I cried, astonished.\
\
Cynthia nodded.\
\
\'93Yes. I don\'92t know why, but she can\'92t bear me; and _he_ can\'92t, either.\'94\
\
\'93There I know you\'92re wrong,\'94 I said warmly. \'93On the contrary, John is\
very fond of you.\'94\
\
\'93Oh, yes\'97_John_. I meant Lawrence. Not, of course, that I care whether\
Lawrence hates me or not. Still, it\'92s rather horrid when no one loves\
you, isn\'92t it?\'94\
\
\'93But they do, Cynthia dear,\'94 I said earnestly. \'93I\'92m sure you are\
mistaken. Look, there is John\'97and Miss Howard\'97\'97\'94\
\
Cynthia nodded rather gloomily. \'93Yes, John likes me, I think, and of\
course Evie, for all her gruff ways, wouldn\'92t be unkind to a fly. But\
Lawrence never speaks to me if he can help it, and Mary can hardly\
bring herself to be civil to me. She wants Evie to stay on, is begging\
her to, but she doesn\'92t want me, and\'97and\'97I don\'92t know what to do.\'94\
Suddenly the poor child burst out crying.\
\
I don\'92t know what possessed me. Her beauty, perhaps, as she sat there,\
with the sunlight glinting down on her head; perhaps the sense of\
relief at encountering someone who so obviously could have no\
connection with the tragedy; perhaps honest pity for her youth and\
loneliness. Anyway, I leant forward, and taking her little hand, I said\
awkwardly:\
\
\'93Marry me, Cynthia.\'94\
\
Unwittingly, I had hit upon a sovereign remedy for her tears. She sat\
up at once, drew her hand away, and said, with some asperity:\
\
\'93Don\'92t be silly!\'94\
\
I was a little annoyed.\
\
\'93I\'92m not being silly. I am asking you to do me the honour of becoming\
my wife.\'94\
\
To my intense surprise, Cynthia burst out laughing, and called me a\
\'93funny dear.\'94\
\
\'93It\'92s perfectly sweet of you,\'94 she said, \'93but you know you don\'92t want\
to!\'94\
\
\'93Yes, I do. I\'92ve got\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93Never mind what you\'92ve got. You don\'92t really want to\'97and I don\'92t\
either.\'94\
\
\'93Well, of course, that settles it,\'94 I said stiffly. \'93But I don\'92t see\
anything to laugh at. There\'92s nothing funny about a proposal.\'94\
\
\'93No, indeed,\'94 said Cynthia. \'93Somebody might accept you next time.\
Good-bye, you\'92ve cheered me up _very_ much.\'94\
\
And, with a final uncontrollable burst of merriment, she vanished\
through the trees.\
\
Thinking over the interview, it struck me as being profoundly\
unsatisfactory.\
\
It occurred to me suddenly that I would go down to the village, and\
look up Bauerstein. Somebody ought to be keeping an eye on the fellow.\
At the same time, it would be wise to allay any suspicions he might\
have as to his being suspected. I remembered how Poirot had relied on\
my diplomacy. Accordingly, I went to the little house with the\
\'93Apartments\'94 card inserted in the window, where I knew he lodged, and\
tapped on the door.\
\
An old woman came and opened it.\
\
\'93Good afternoon,\'94 I said pleasantly. \'93Is Dr. Bauerstein in?\'94\
\
She stared at me.\
\
\'93Haven\'92t you heard?\'94\
\
\'93Heard what?\'94\
\
\'93About him.\'94\
\
\'93What about him?\'94\
\
\'93He\'92s took.\'94\
\
\'93Took? Dead?\'94\
\
\'93No, took by the perlice.\'94\
\
