\'93By the police!\'94 I gasped. \'93Do you mean they\'92ve arrested him?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, that\'92s it, and\'97\'97\'94\
\
I waited to hear no more, but tore up the village to find Poirot.\
\
\
\
\
\
\
CHAPTER X. THE ARREST\
\
To my extreme annoyance, Poirot was not in, and the old Belgian who\
answered my knock informed me that he believed he had gone to London.\
\
I was dumbfounded. What on earth could Poirot be doing in London! Was\
it a sudden decision on his part, or had he already made up his mind\
when he parted from me a few hours earlier?\
\
I retraced my steps to Styles in some annoyance. With Poirot away, I\
was uncertain how to act. Had he foreseen this arrest? Had he not, in\
all probability, been the cause of it? Those questions I could not\
resolve. But in the meantime what was I to do? Should I announce the\
arrest openly at Styles, or not? Though I did not acknowledge it to\
myself, the thought of Mary Cavendish was weighing on me. Would it not\
be a terrible shock to her? For the moment, I set aside utterly any\
suspicions of her. She could not be implicated\'97otherwise I should have\
heard some hint of it.\
\
Of course, there was no possibility of being able permanently to\
conceal Dr. Bauerstein\'92s arrest from her. It would be announced in\
every newspaper on the morrow. Still, I shrank from blurting it out. If\
only Poirot had been accessible, I could have asked his advice. What\
possessed him to go posting off to London in this unaccountable way?\
\
In spite of myself, my opinion of his sagacity was immeasurably\
heightened. I would never have dreamt of suspecting the doctor, had not\
Poirot put it into my head. Yes, decidedly, the little man was clever.\
\
After some reflecting, I decided to take John into my confidence, and\
leave him to make the matter public or not, as he thought fit.\
\
He gave vent to a prodigious whistle, as I imparted the news.\
\
\'93Great Scott! You _were_ right, then. I couldn\'92t believe it at the\
time.\'94\
\
\'93No, it is astonishing until you get used to the idea, and see how it\
makes everything fit in. Now, what are we to do? Of course, it will be\
generally known to-morrow.\'94\
\
John reflected.\
\
\'93Never mind,\'94 he said at last, \'93we won\'92t say anything at present. There\
is no need. As you say, it will be known soon enough.\'94\
\
But to my intense surprise, on getting down early the next morning, and\
eagerly opening the newspapers, there was not a word about the arrest!\
There was a column of mere padding about \'93The Styles Poisoning Case,\'94\
but nothing further. It was rather inexplicable, but I supposed that,\
for some reason or other, Japp wished to keep it out of the papers. It\
worried me just a little, for it suggested the possibility that there\
might be further arrests to come.\
\
After breakfast, I decided to go down to the village, and see if Poirot\
had returned yet; but, before I could start, a well-known face blocked\
one of the windows, and the well-known voice said:\
\
\'93_Bonjour, mon ami!_\'94\
\
\'93Poirot,\'94 I exclaimed, with relief, and seizing him by both hands, I\
dragged him into the room. \'93I was never so glad to see anyone. Listen,\
I have said nothing to anybody but John. Is that right?\'94\
\
\'93My friend,\'94 replied Poirot, \'93I do not know what you are talking\
about.\'94\
\
\'93Dr. Bauerstein\'92s arrest, of course,\'94 I answered impatiently.\
\
\'93Is Bauerstein arrested, then?\'94\
\
\'93Did you not know it?\'94\
\
\'93Not the least in the world.\'94 But, pausing a moment, he added: \'93Still,\
it does not surprise me. After all, we are only four miles from the\
coast.\'94\
\
\'93The coast?\'94 I asked, puzzled. \'93What has that got to do with it?\'94\
\
Poirot shrugged his shoulders.\
\
\'93Surely, it is obvious!\'94\
\
\'93Not to me. No doubt I am very dense, but I cannot see what the\
proximity of the coast has got to do with the murder of Mrs.\
Inglethorp.\'94\
\
\'93Nothing at all, of course,\'94 replied Poirot, smiling. \'93But we were\
speaking of the arrest of Dr. Bauerstein.\'94\
\
\'93Well, he is arrested for the murder of Mrs. Inglethorp\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93What?\'94 cried Poirot, in apparently lively astonishment. \'93Dr.\
Bauerstein arrested for the murder of Mrs. Inglethorp?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93Impossible! That would be too good a farce! Who told you that, my\
friend?\'94\
\
\'93Well, no one exactly told me,\'94 I confessed. \'93But he is arrested.\'94\
\
\'93Oh, yes, very likely. But for espionage, _mon ami_.\'94\
\
\'93Espionage?\'94 I gasped.\
\
\'93Precisely.\'94\
\
\'93Not for poisoning Mrs. Inglethorp?\'94\
\
\'93Not unless our friend Japp has taken leave of his senses,\'94 replied\
Poirot placidly.\
\
\'93But\'97but I thought you thought so too?\'94\
\
Poirot gave me one look, which conveyed a wondering pity, and his full\
sense of the utter absurdity of such an idea.\
\
\'93Do you mean to say,\'94 I asked, slowly adapting myself to the new idea,\
\'93that Dr. Bauerstein is a spy?\'94\
\
Poirot nodded.\
\
\'93Have you never suspected it?\'94\
\
\'93It never entered my head.\'94\
\
\'93It did not strike you as peculiar that a famous London doctor should\
bury himself in a little village like this, and should be in the habit\
of walking about at all hours of the night, fully dressed?\'94\
\
\'93No,\'94 I confessed, \'93I never thought of such a thing.\'94\
\
\'93He is, of course, a German by birth,\'94 said Poirot thoughtfully,\
\'93though he has practised so long in this country that nobody thinks of\
him as anything but an Englishman. He was naturalized about fifteen\
years ago. A very clever man\'97a Jew, of course.\'94\
\
\'93The blackguard!\'94 I cried indignantly.\
\
\'93Not at all. He is, on the contrary, a patriot. Think what he stands to\
lose. I admire the man myself.\'94\
\
But I could not look at it in Poirot\'92s philosophical way.\
\
\'93And this is the man with whom Mrs. Cavendish has been wandering about\
all over the country!\'94 I cried indignantly.\
\
\'93Yes. I should fancy he had found her very useful,\'94 remarked Poirot.\
\'93So long as gossip busied itself in coupling their names together, any\
other vagaries of the doctor\'92s passed unobserved.\'94\
\
\'93Then you think he never really cared for her?\'94 I asked eagerly\'97rather\
too eagerly, perhaps, under the circumstances.\
\
\'93That, of course, I cannot say, but\'97shall I tell you my own private\
opinion, Hastings?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93Well, it is this: that Mrs. Cavendish does not care, and never has\
cared one little jot about Dr. Bauerstein!\'94\
\
\'93Do you really think so?\'94 I could not disguise my pleasure.\
\
\'93I am quite sure of it. And I will tell you why.\'94\
\
\'93Yes?\'94\
\
\'93Because she cares for someone else, _mon ami_.\'94\
\
\'93Oh!\'94 What did he mean? In spite of myself, an agreeable warmth spread\
over me. I am not a vain man where women are concerned, but I\
remembered certain evidences, too lightly thought of at the time,\
perhaps, but which certainly seemed to indicate\'97\'97\
\
My pleasing thoughts were interrupted by the sudden entrance of Miss\
Howard. She glanced round hastily to make sure there was no one else in\
the room, and quickly produced an old sheet of brown paper. This she\
handed to Poirot, murmuring as she did so the cryptic words:\
\
\'93On top of the wardrobe.\'94 Then she hurriedly left the room.\
\
Poirot unfolded the sheet of paper eagerly, and uttered an exclamation\
of satisfaction. He spread it out on the table.\
\
\'93Come here, Hastings. Now tell me, what is that initial\'97J. or L.?\'94\
\
It was a medium sized sheet of paper, rather dusty, as though it had\
lain by for some time. But it was the label that was attracting\
Poirot\'92s attention. At the top, it bore the printed stamp of Messrs.\
Parkson\'92s, the well-known theatrical costumiers, and it was addressed\
to \'93\'97(the debatable initial) Cavendish, Esq., Styles Court, Styles St.\
Mary, Essex.\'94\
\
\'93It might be T., or it might be L.,\'94 I said, after studying the thing\
for a minute or two. \'93It certainly isn\'92t a J.\'94\
\
\'93Good,\'94 replied Poirot, folding up the paper again. \'93I, also, am of\
your way of thinking. It is an L., depend upon it!\'94\
\
\'93Where did it come from?\'94 I asked curiously. \'93Is it important?\'94\
\
\'93Moderately so. It confirms a surmise of mine. Having deduced its\
existence, I set Miss Howard to search for it, and, as you see, she has\
been successful.\'94\
\
\'93What did she mean by \'91On the top of the wardrobe\'92?\'94\
\
\'93She meant,\'94 replied Poirot promptly, \'93that she found it on top of a\
wardrobe.\'94\
\
\'93A funny place for a piece of brown paper,\'94 I mused.\
\
\'93Not at all. The top of a wardrobe is an excellent place for brown\
paper and cardboard boxes. I have kept them there myself. Neatly\
arranged, there is nothing to offend the eye.\'94\
\
\'93Poirot,\'94 I asked earnestly, \'93have you made up your mind about this\
crime?\'94\
\
\'93Yes\'97that is to say, I believe I know how it was committed.\'94\
\
\'93Ah!\'94\
\
\'93Unfortunately, I have no proof beyond my surmise, unless\'97\'97\'94 With\
sudden energy, he caught me by the arm, and whirled me down the hall,\
calling out in French in his excitement: \'93Mademoiselle Dorcas,\
Mademoiselle Dorcas, _un moment, s\'92il vous pla\'eet!_\'94\
\
Dorcas, quite flurried by the noise, came hurrying out of the pantry.\
\
\'93My good Dorcas, I have an idea\'97a little idea\'97if it should prove\
justified, what magnificent chance! Tell me, on Monday, not Tuesday,\
Dorcas, but Monday, the day before the tragedy, did anything go wrong\
with Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s bell?\'94\
\
Dorcas looked very surprised.\
\
\'93Yes, sir, now you mention it, it did; though I don\'92t know how you came\
to hear of it. A mouse, or some such, must have nibbled the wire\
through. The man came and put it right on Tuesday morning.\'94\
\
With a long drawn exclamation of ecstasy, Poirot led the way back to\
the morning-room.\
\
\'93See you, one should not ask for outside proof\'97no, reason should be\
enough. But the flesh is weak, it is consolation to find that one is on\
the right track. Ah, my friend, I am like a giant refreshed. I run! I\
leap!\'94\
\
And, in very truth, run and leap he did, gambolling wildly down the\
stretch of lawn outside the long window.\
\
\'93What is your remarkable little friend doing?\'94 asked a voice behind me,\
and I turned to find Mary Cavendish at my elbow. She smiled, and so did\
I. \'93What is it all about?\'94\
\
\'93Really, I can\'92t tell you. He asked Dorcas some question about a bell,\
and appeared so delighted with her answer that he is capering about as\
you see!\'94\
\
Mary laughed.\
\
\'93How ridiculous! He\'92s going out of the gate. Isn\'92t he coming back\
to-day?\'94\
\
\'93I don\'92t know. I\'92ve given up trying to guess what he\'92ll do next.\'94\
\
\'93Is he quite mad, Mr. Hastings?\'94\
\
\'93I honestly don\'92t know. Sometimes, I feel sure he is as mad as a\
hatter; and then, just as he is at his maddest, I find there is method\
in his madness.\'94\
\
\'93I see.\'94\
\
In spite of her laugh, Mary was looking thoughtful this morning. She\
seemed grave, almost sad.\
\
It occurred to me that it would be a good opportunity to tackle her on\
the subject of Cynthia. I began rather tactfully, _I_ thought, but I\
had not gone far before she stopped me authoritatively.\
\
\'93You are an excellent advocate, I have no doubt, Mr. Hastings, but in\
this case your talents are quite thrown away. Cynthia will run no risk\
of encountering any unkindness from me.\'94\
\
I began to stammer feebly that I hoped she hadn\'92t thought\'97\'97 But again\
she stopped me, and her words were so unexpected that they quite drove\
Cynthia, and her troubles, out of my mind.\
\
\'93Mr. Hastings,\'94 she said, \'93do you think I and my husband are happy\
together?\'94\
\
I was considerably taken aback, and murmured something about it\'92s not\
being my business to think anything of the sort.\
\
\'93Well,\'94 she said quietly, \'93whether it is your business or not, I will\
tell you that we are _not_ happy.\'94\
\
I said nothing, for I saw that she had not finished.\
\
She began slowly, walking up and down the room, her head a little bent,\
and that slim, supple figure of hers swaying gently as she walked. She\
stopped suddenly, and looked up at me.\
\
\'93You don\'92t know anything about me, do you?\'94 she asked. \'93Where I come\
from, who I was before I married John\'97anything, in fact? Well, I will\
tell you. I will make a father confessor of you. You are kind, I\
think\'97yes, I am sure you are kind.\'94\
\
Somehow, I was not quite as elated as I might have been. I remembered\
that Cynthia had begun her confidences in much the same way. Besides, a\
father confessor should be elderly, it is not at all the role for a\
young man.\
\
\'93My father was English,\'94 said Mrs. Cavendish, \'93but my mother was a\
Russian.\'94\
\
\'93Ah,\'94 I said, \'93now I understand\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93Understand what?\'94\
\
\'93A hint of something foreign\'97different\'97that there has always been about\
you.\'94\
\
\'93My mother was very beautiful, I believe. I don\'92t know, because I never\
saw her. She died when I was quite a little child. I believe there was\
some tragedy connected with her death\'97she took an overdose of some\
sleeping draught by mistake. However that may be, my father was\
broken-hearted. Shortly afterwards, he went into the Consular Service.\
Everywhere he went, I went with him. When I was twenty-three, I had\
been nearly all over the world. It was a splendid life\'97I loved it.\'94\
\
There was a smile on her face, and her head was thrown back. She seemed\
living in the memory of those old glad days.\
\
\'93Then my father died. He left me very badly off. I had to go and live\
with some old aunts in Yorkshire.\'94 She shuddered. \'93You will understand\
me when I say that it was a deadly life for a girl brought up as I had\
been. The narrowness, the deadly monotony of it, almost drove me mad.\'94\
She paused a minute, and added in a different tone: \'93And then I met\
John Cavendish.\'94\
\
\'93Yes?\'94\
\
\'93You can imagine that, from my aunts\'92 point of view, it was a very good\
match for me. But I can honestly say it was not this fact which weighed\
with me. No, he was simply a way of escape from the insufferable\
monotony of my life.\'94\
\
I said nothing, and after a moment, she went on:\
\
\'93Don\'92t misunderstand me. I was quite honest with him. I told him, what\
was true, that I liked him very much, that I hoped to come to like him\
more, but that I was not in any way what the world calls \'91in love\'92 with\
him. He declared that that satisfied him, and so\'97we were married.\'94\
\
She waited a long time, a little frown had gathered on her forehead.\
She seemed to be looking back earnestly into those past days.\
\
\'93I think\'97I am sure\'97he cared for me at first. But I suppose we were not\
well matched. Almost at once, we drifted apart. He\'97it is not a pleasing\
thing for my pride, but it is the truth\'97tired of me very soon.\'94 I must\
have made some murmur of dissent, for she went on quickly: \'93Oh, yes, he\
did! Not that it matters now\'97now that we\'92ve come to the parting of the\
ways.\'94\
\
\'93What do you mean?\'94\
\
She answered quietly:\
\
\'93I mean that I am not going to remain at Styles.\'94\
\
\'93You and John are not going to live here?\'94\
\
\'93John may live here, but I shall not.\'94\
\
\'93You are going to leave him?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93But why?\'94\
\
She paused a long time, and said at last:\
\
\'93Perhaps\'97because I want to be\'97free!\'94\
\
And, as she spoke, I had a sudden vision of broad spaces, virgin tracts\
of forests, untrodden lands\'97and a realization of what freedom would\
mean to such a nature as Mary Cavendish. I seemed to see her for a\
moment as she was, a proud wild creature, as untamed by civilization as\
some shy bird of the hills. A little cry broke from her lips:\
\
\'93You don\'92t know, you don\'92t know, how this hateful place has been prison\
to me!\'94\
\
\'93I understand,\'94 I said, \'93but\'97but don\'92t do anything rash.\'94\
\
\'93Oh, rash!\'94 Her voice mocked at my prudence.\
\
Then suddenly I said a thing I could have bitten out my tongue for:\
\
\'93You know that Dr. Bauerstein has been arrested?\'94\
\
An instant coldness passed like a mask over her face, blotting out all\
expression.\
\
\'93John was so kind as to break that to me this morning.\'94\
\
\'93Well, what do you think?\'94 I asked feebly.\
\
\'93Of what?\'94\
\
\'93Of the arrest?\'94\
\
\'93What should I think? Apparently he is a German spy; so the gardener\
had told John.\'94\
\
Her face and voice were absolutely cold and expressionless. Did she\
care, or did she not?\
\
She moved away a step or two, and fingered one of the flower vases.\
\
\'93These are quite dead. I must do them again. Would you mind\
moving\'97thank you, Mr. Hastings.\'94 And she walked quietly past me out of\
the window, with a cool little nod of dismissal.\
\
No, surely she could not care for Bauerstein. No woman could act her\
part with that icy unconcern.\
\
Poirot did not make his appearance the following morning, and there was\
no sign of the Scotland Yard men.\
\
But, at lunch-time, there arrived a new piece of evidence\'97or rather\
lack of evidence. We had vainly tried to trace the fourth letter, which\
Mrs. Inglethorp had written on the evening preceding her death. Our\
efforts having been in vain, we had abandoned the matter, hoping that\
it might turn up of itself one day. And this is just what did happen,\
in the shape of a communication, which arrived by the second post from\
a firm of French music publishers, acknowledging Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s\
cheque, and regretting they had been unable to trace a certain series\
of Russian folksongs. So the last hope of solving the mystery, by means\
of Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s correspondence on the fatal evening, had to be\
abandoned.\
\
Just before tea, I strolled down to tell Poirot of the new\
disappointment, but found, to my annoyance, that he was once more out.\
\
\'93Gone to London again?\'94\
\
\'93Oh, no, monsieur, he has but taken the train to Tadminster. \'91To see a\
young lady\'92s dispensary,\'92 he said.\'94\
\
\'93Silly ass!\'94 I ejaculated. \'93I told him Wednesday was the one day she\
wasn\'92t there! Well, tell him to look us up to-morrow morning, will\
you?\'94\
\
\'93Certainly, monsieur.\'94\
\
But, on the following day, no sign of Poirot. I was getting angry. He\
was really treating us in the most cavalier fashion.\
\
After lunch, Lawrence drew me aside, and asked if I was going down to\
see him.\
\
\'93No, I don\'92t think I shall. He can come up here if he wants to see us.\'94\
\
\'93Oh!\'94 Lawrence looked indeterminate. Something unusually nervous and\
excited in his manner roused my curiosity.\
\
\'93What is it?\'94 I asked. \'93I could go if there\'92s anything special.\'94\
\
\'93It\'92s nothing much, but\'97well, if you are going, will you tell him\'97\'97\'94 he\
dropped his voice to a whisper\'97\'93I think I\'92ve found the extra\
coffee-cup!\'94\
\
I had almost forgotten that enigmatical message of Poirot\'92s, but now my\
curiosity was aroused afresh.\
\
Lawrence would say no more, so I decided that I would descend from my\
high horse, and once more seek out Poirot at Leastways Cottage.\
\
This time I was received with a smile. Monsieur Poirot was within.\
Would I mount? I mounted accordingly.\
\
Poirot was sitting by the table, his head buried in his hands. He\
sprang up at my entrance.\
\
\'93What is it?\'94 I asked solicitously. \'93You are not ill, I trust?\'94\
\
\'93No, no, not ill. But I decide an affair of great moment.\'94\
\
\'93Whether to catch the criminal or not?\'94 I asked facetiously.\
\
But, to my great surprise, Poirot nodded gravely.\
\
\'93\'91To speak or not to speak,\'92 as your so great Shakespeare says, \'91that\
is the question.\'92\'94\
\
I did not trouble to correct the quotation.\
\
\'93You are not serious, Poirot?\'94\
\
\'93I am of the most serious. For the most serious of all things hangs in\
the balance.\'94\
\
\'93And that is?\'94\
\
\'93A woman\'92s happiness, _mon ami_,\'94 he said gravely.\
\
I did not quite know what to say.\
\
\'93The moment has come,\'94 said Poirot thoughtfully, \'93and I do not know\
what to do. For, see you, it is a big stake for which I play. No one\
but I, Hercule Poirot, would attempt it!\'94 And he tapped himself proudly\
on the breast.\
\
After pausing a few minutes respectfully, so as not to spoil his\
effect, I gave him Lawrence\'92s message.\
\
\'93Aha!\'94 he cried. \'93So he has found the extra coffee-cup. That is good.\
He has more intelligence than would appear, this long-faced Monsieur\
Lawrence of yours!\'94\
\
I did not myself think very highly of Lawrence\'92s intelligence; but I\
forebore to contradict Poirot, and gently took him to task for\
forgetting my instructions as to which were Cynthia\'92s days off.\
\
\'93It is true. I have the head of a sieve. However, the other young lady\
was most kind. She was sorry for my disappointment, and showed me\
everything in the kindest way.\'94\
\
\'93Oh, well, that\'92s all right, then, and you must go to tea with Cynthia\
another day.\'94\
\
I told him about the letter.\
\
\'93I am sorry for that,\'94 he said. \'93I always had hopes of that letter. But\
no, it was not to be. This affair must all be unravelled from within.\'94\
He tapped his forehead. \'93These little grey cells. It is \'91up to them\'92\'97as\
you say over here.\'94 Then, suddenly, he asked: \'93Are you a judge of\
finger-marks, my friend?\'94\
\
\'93No,\'94 I said, rather surprised, \'93I know that there are no two\
finger-marks alike, but that\'92s as far as my science goes.\'94\
\
\'93Exactly.\'94\
\
He unlocked a little drawer, and took out some photographs which he\
laid on the table.\
\
\'93I have numbered them, 1, 2, 3. Will you describe them to me?\'94\
\
I studied the proofs attentively.\
\
\'93All greatly magnified, I see. No. 1, I should say, are a man\'92s\
finger-prints; thumb and first finger. No. 2 are a lady\'92s; they are\
much smaller, and quite different in every way. No. 3\'94\'97I paused for\
some time\'97\'93there seem to be a lot of confused finger-marks, but here,\
very distinctly, are No. 1\'92s.\'94\
\
\'93Overlapping the others?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93You recognize them beyond fail?\'94\
\
\'93Oh, yes; they are identical.\'94\
\
Poirot nodded, and gently taking the photographs from me locked them up\
again.\
\
\'93I suppose,\'94 I said, \'93that as usual, you are not going to explain?\'94\
\
\'93On the contrary. No. 1 were the finger-prints of Monsieur Lawrence.\
No. 2 were those of Mademoiselle Cynthia. They are not important. I\
merely obtained them for comparison. No. 3 is a little more\
complicated.\'94\
\
\'93Yes?\'94\
\
\'93It is, as you see, highly magnified. You may have noticed a sort of\
blur extending all across the picture. I will not describe to you the\
special apparatus, dusting powder, etc., which I used. It is a\
well-known process to the police, and by means of it you can obtain a\
photograph of the finger-prints of any object in a very short space of\
time. Well, my friend, you have seen the finger-marks\'97it remains to\
tell you the particular object on which they had been left.\'94\
\
\'93Go on\'97I am really excited.\'94\
\
\'93_Eh bien!_ Photo No. 3 represents the highly magnified surface of a\
tiny bottle in the top poison cupboard of the dispensary in the Red\
Cross Hospital at Tadminster\'97which sounds like the house that Jack\
built!\'94\
\
\'93Good heavens!\'94 I exclaimed. \'93But what were Lawrence Cavendish\'92s\
finger-marks doing on it? He never went near the poison cupboard the\
day we were there!\'94\
\
\'93Oh, yes, he did!\'94\
\
\'93Impossible! We were all together the whole time.\'94\
\
Poirot shook his head.\
\
\'93No, my friend, there was a moment when you were not all together.\
There was a moment when you could not have been all together, or it\
would not have been necessary to call to Monsieur Lawrence to come and\
join you on the balcony.\'94\
\
\'93I\'92d forgotten that,\'94 I admitted. \'93But it was only for a moment.\'94\
\
\'93Long enough.\'94\
\
\'93Long enough for what?\'94\
\
Poirot\'92s smile became rather enigmatical.\
\
\'93Long enough for a gentleman who had once studied medicine to gratify a\
very natural interest and curiosity.\'94\
\
Our eyes met. Poirot\'92s were pleasantly vague. He got up and hummed a\
little tune. I watched him suspiciously.\
\
\'93Poirot,\'94 I said, \'93what was in this particular little bottle?\'94\
\
Poirot looked out of the window.\
\
\'93Hydro-chloride of strychnine,\'94 he said, over his shoulder, continuing\
to hum.\
\
\'93Good heavens!\'94 I said it quite quietly. I was not surprised. I had\
expected that answer.\
\
\'93They use the pure hydro-chloride of strychnine very little\'97only\
occasionally for pills. It is the official solution, Liq. Strychnine\
Hydro-clor. that is used in most medicines. That is why the\
finger-marks have remained undisturbed since then.\'94\
\
\'93How did you manage to take this photograph?\'94\
\
\'93I dropped my hat from the balcony,\'94 explained Poirot simply. \'93Visitors\
were not permitted below at that hour, so, in spite of my many\
apologies, Mademoiselle Cynthia\'92s colleague had to go down and fetch it\
for me.\'94\
\
\'93Then you knew what you were going to find?\'94\
\
\'93No, not at all. I merely realized that it was possible, from your\
story, for Monsieur Lawrence to go to the poison cupboard. The\
possibility had to be confirmed, or eliminated.\'94\
\
\'93Poirot,\'94 I said, \'93your gaiety does not deceive me. This is a very\
important discovery.\'94\
\
\'93I do not know,\'94 said Poirot. \'93But one thing does strike me. No doubt\
it has struck you too.\'94\
\
\'93What is that?\'94\
\
\'93Why, that there is altogether too much strychnine about this case.\
This is the third time we run up against it. There was strychnine in\
Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s tonic. There is the strychnine sold across the\
counter at Styles St. Mary by Mace. Now we have more strychnine,\
handled by one of the household. It is confusing; and, as you know, I\
do not like confusion.\'94\
\
Before I could reply, one of the other Belgians opened the door and\
stuck his head in.\
\
\'93There is a lady below, asking for Mr Hastings.\'94\
\
\'93A lady?\'94\
\
I jumped up. Poirot followed me down the narrow stairs. Mary Cavendish\
was standing in the doorway.\
\
\'93I have been visiting an old woman in the village,\'94 she explained, \'93and\
as Lawrence told me you were with Monsieur Poirot I thought I would\
call for you.\'94\
\
\'93Alas, madame,\'94 said Poirot, \'93I thought you had come to honour me with\
a visit!\'94\
\
\'93I will some day, if you ask me,\'94 she promised him, smiling.\
\
\'93That is well. If you should need a father confessor, madame\'94\'97she\
started ever so slightly\'97\'93remember, Papa Poirot is always at your\
service.\'94\
\
She stared at him for a few minutes, as though seeking to read some\
deeper meaning into his words. Then she turned abruptly away.\
\
\'93Come, will you not walk back with us too, Monsieur Poirot?\'94\
\
\'93Enchanted, madame.\'94\
\
All the way to Styles, Mary talked fast and feverishly. It struck me\
that in some way she was nervous of Poirot\'92s eyes.\
\
The weather had broken, and the sharp wind was almost autumnal in its\
shrewishness. Mary shivered a little, and buttoned her black sports\
coat closer. The wind through the trees made a mournful noise, like\
some great giant sighing.\
\
We walked up to the great door of Styles, and at once the knowledge\
came to us that something was wrong.\
\
Dorcas came running out to meet us. She was crying and wringing her\
hands. I was aware of other servants huddled together in the\
background, all eyes and ears.\
\
\'93Oh, m\'92am! Oh, m\'92am! I don\'92t know how to tell you\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93What is it, Dorcas?\'94 I asked impatiently. \'93Tell us at once.\'94\
\
\'93It\'92s those wicked detectives. They\'92ve arrested him\'97they\'92ve arrested\
Mr. Cavendish!\'94\
\
\'93Arrested Lawrence?\'94 I gasped.\
\
I saw a strange look come into Dorcas\'92s eyes.\
\
\'93No, sir. Not Mr. Lawrence\'97Mr. John.\'94\
\
Behind me, with a wild cry, Mary Cavendish fell heavily against me, and\
as I turned to catch her I met the quiet triumph in Poirot\'92s eyes.\
\
\
\
\
\
\
CHAPTER XI. THE CASE FOR THE PROSECUTION\
\
The trial of John Cavendish for the murder of his stepmother took place\
two months later.\
\
Of the intervening weeks I will say little, but my admiration and\
sympathy went out unfeignedly to Mary Cavendish. She ranged herself\
passionately on her husband\'92s side, scorning the mere idea of his\
guilt, and fought for him tooth and nail.\
\
I expressed my admiration to Poirot, and he nodded thoughtfully.\
\
\'93Yes, she is of those women who show at their best in adversity. It\
brings out all that is sweetest and truest in them. Her pride and her\
jealousy have\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93Jealousy?\'94 I queried.\
\
\'93Yes. Have you not realized that she is an unusually jealous woman? As\
I was saying, her pride and jealousy have been laid aside. She thinks\
of nothing but her husband, and the terrible fate that is hanging over\
him.\'94\
\
He spoke very feelingly, and I looked at him earnestly, remembering\
that last afternoon, when he had been deliberating whether or not to\
speak. With his tenderness for \'93a woman\'92s happiness,\'94 I felt glad that\
the decision had been taken out of his hands.\
\
\'93Even now,\'94 I said, \'93I can hardly believe it. You see, up to the very\
last minute, I thought it was Lawrence!\'94\
\
Poirot grinned.\
\
\'93I know you did.\'94\
\
\'93But John! My old friend John!\'94\
\
\'93Every murderer is probably somebody\'92s old friend,\'94 observed Poirot\
philosophically. \'93You cannot mix up sentiment and reason.\'94\
\
\'93I must say I think you might have given me a hint.\'94\
\
\'93Perhaps, _mon ami_, I did not do so, just because he _was_ your old\
friend.\'94\
\
I was rather disconcerted by this, remembering how I had busily passed\
on to John what I believed to be Poirot\'92s views concerning Bauerstein.\
He, by the way, had been acquitted of the charge brought against him.\
Nevertheless, although he had been too clever for them this time, and\
the charge of espionage could not be brought home to him, his wings\
were pretty well clipped for the future.\
\
I asked Poirot whether he thought John would be condemned. To my\
intense surprise, he replied that, on the contrary, he was extremely\
likely to be acquitted.\
\
\'93But, Poirot\'97\'97\'94 I protested.\
\
\'93Oh, my friend, have I not said to you all along that I have no proofs.\
It is one thing to know that a man is guilty, it is quite another\
matter to prove him so. And, in this case, there is terribly little\
evidence. That is the whole trouble. I, Hercule Poirot, know, but I\
lack the last link in my chain. And unless I can find that missing\
link\'97\'97\'94 He shook his head gravely.\
\
\'93When did you first suspect John Cavendish?\'94 I asked, after a minute or\
two.\
\
\'93Did you not suspect him at all?\'94\
\
\'93No, indeed.\'94\
\
\'93Not after that fragment of conversation you overheard between Mrs.\
Cavendish and her mother-in-law, and her subsequent lack of frankness\
at the inquest?\'94\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
\'93Did you not put two and two together, and reflect that if it was not\
Alfred Inglethorp who was quarrelling with his wife\'97and you remember,\
he strenuously denied it at the inquest\'97it must be either Lawrence or\
John. Now, if it was Lawrence, Mary Cavendish\'92s conduct was just as\
inexplicable. But if, on the other hand, it was John, the whole thing\
was explained quite naturally.\'94\
\
\'93So,\'94 I cried, a light breaking in upon me, \'93it was John who quarrelled\
with his mother that afternoon?\'94\
\
\'93Exactly.\'94\
\
\'93And you have known this all along?\'94\
\
\'93Certainly. Mrs. Cavendish\'92s behaviour could only be explained that\
way.\'94\
\
\'93And yet you say he may be acquitted?\'94\
\
Poirot shrugged his shoulders.\
\
\'93Certainly I do. At the police court proceedings, we shall hear the\
case for the prosecution, but in all probability his solicitors will\
advise him to reserve his defence. That will be sprung upon us at the\
trial. And\'97ah, by the way, I have a word of caution to give you, my\
friend. I must not appear in the case.\'94\
\
\'93What?\'94\
\
\'93No. Officially, I have nothing to do with it. Until I have found that\
last link in my chain, I must remain behind the scenes. Mrs. Cavendish\
must think I am working for her husband, not against him.\'94\
\
\'93I say, that\'92s playing it a bit low down,\'94 I protested.\
\
\'93Not at all. We have to deal with a most clever and unscrupulous man,\
and we must use any means in our power\'97otherwise he will slip through\
our fingers. That is why I have been careful to remain in the\
background. All the discoveries have been made by Japp, and Japp will\
take all the credit. If I am called upon to give evidence at all\'94\'97he\
smiled broadly\'97\'93it will probably be as a witness for the defence.\'94\
\
I could hardly believe my ears.\
\
\'93It is quite _en r\'e8gle_,\'94 continued Poirot. \'93Strangely enough, I can\
give evidence that will demolish one contention of the prosecution.\'94\
\
\'93Which one?\'94\
\
\'93The one that relates to the destruction of the will. John Cavendish\
did not destroy that will.\'94\
\
Poirot was a true prophet. I will not go into the details of the police\
court proceedings, as it involves many tiresome repetitions. I will\
merely state baldly that John Cavendish reserved his defence, and was\
duly committed for trial.\
\
September found us all in London. Mary took a house in Kensington,\
Poirot being included in the family party.\
\
I myself had been given a job at the War Office, so was able to see\
them continually.\
\
As the weeks went by, the state of Poirot\'92s nerves grew worse and\
worse. That \'93last link\'94 he talked about was still lacking. Privately, I\
hoped it might remain so, for what happiness could there be for Mary,\
if John were not acquitted?\
\
On September 15th John Cavendish appeared in the dock at the Old\
Bailey, charged with \'93The Wilful Murder of Emily Agnes Inglethorp,\'94 and\
pleaded \'93Not Guilty.\'94\
\
Sir Ernest Heavywether, the famous K.C., had been engaged to defend\
him.\
\
Mr. Philips, K.C., opened the case for the Crown.\
\
The murder, he said, was a most premeditated and cold-blooded one. It\
was neither more nor less than the deliberate poisoning of a fond and\
trusting woman by the stepson to whom she had been more than a mother.\
Ever since his boyhood, she had supported him. He and his wife had\
lived at Styles Court in every luxury, surrounded by her care and\
attention. She had been their kind and generous benefactress.\
\
He proposed to call witnesses to show how the prisoner, a profligate\
and spendthrift, had been at the end of his financial tether, and had\
also been carrying on an intrigue with a certain Mrs. Raikes, a\
neighbouring farmer\'92s wife. This having come to his stepmother\'92s ears,\
she taxed him with it on the afternoon before her death, and a quarrel\
ensued, part of which was overheard. On the previous day, the prisoner\
had purchased strychnine at the village chemist\'92s shop, wearing a\
disguise by means of which he hoped to throw the onus of the crime upon\
another man\'97to wit, Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s husband, of whom he had been\
bitterly jealous. Luckily for Mr. Inglethorp, he had been able to\
produce an unimpeachable alibi.\
\
On the afternoon of July 17th, continued Counsel, immediately after the\
quarrel with her son, Mrs. Inglethorp made a new will. This will was\
found destroyed in the grate of her bedroom the following morning, but\
evidence had come to light which showed that it had been drawn up in\
favour of her husband. Deceased had already made a will in his favour\
before her marriage, but\'97and Mr. Philips wagged an expressive\
forefinger\'97the prisoner was not aware of that. What had induced the\
deceased to make a fresh will, with the old one still extant, he could\
not say. She was an old lady, and might possibly have forgotten the\
former one; or\'97this seemed to him more likely\'97she may have had an idea\
that it was revoked by her marriage, as there had been some\
conversation on the subject. Ladies were not always very well versed in\
legal knowledge. She had, about a year before, executed a will in\
favour of the prisoner. He would call evidence to show that it was the\
prisoner who ultimately handed his stepmother her coffee on the fatal\
night. Later in the evening, he had sought admission to her room, on\
which occasion, no doubt, he found an opportunity of destroying the\
will which, as far as he knew, would render the one in his favour\
valid.\
\
The prisoner had been arrested in consequence of the discovery, in his\
room, by Detective Inspector Japp\'97a most brilliant officer\'97of the\
identical phial of strychnine which had been sold at the village\
chemist\'92s to the supposed Mr. Inglethorp on the day before the murder.\
It would be for the jury to decide whether or not these damning facts\
constituted an overwhelming proof of the prisoner\'92s guilt.\
\
And, subtly implying that a jury which did not so decide, was quite\
unthinkable, Mr. Philips sat down and wiped his forehead.\
\
The first witnesses for the prosecution were mostly those who had been\
called at the inquest, the medical evidence being again taken first.\
\
Sir Ernest Heavywether, who was famous all over England for the\
unscrupulous manner in which he bullied witnesses, only asked two\
questions.\
\
\'93I take it, Dr. Bauerstein, that strychnine, as a drug, acts quickly?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93And that you are unable to account for the delay in this case?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93Thank you.\'94\
\
Mr. Mace identified the phial handed him by Counsel as that sold by him\
to \'93Mr. Inglethorp.\'94 Pressed, he admitted that he only knew Mr.\
Inglethorp by sight. He had never spoken to him. The witness was not\
cross-examined.\
\
Alfred Inglethorp was called, and denied having purchased the poison.\
He also denied having quarrelled with his wife. Various witnesses\
testified to the accuracy of these statements.\
\
The gardeners\'92 evidence, as to the witnessing of the will was taken,\
and then Dorcas was called.\
\
Dorcas, faithful to her \'93young gentlemen,\'94 denied strenuously that it\
could have been John\'92s voice she heard, and resolutely declared, in the\
teeth of everything, that it was Mr. Inglethorp who had been in the\
boudoir with her mistress. A rather wistful smile passed across the\
face of the prisoner in the dock. He knew only too well how useless her\
gallant defiance was, since it was not the object of the defence to\
deny this point. Mrs. Cavendish, of course, could not be called upon to\
give evidence against her husband.\
\
After various questions on other matters, Mr. Philips asked:\
\
\'93In the month of June last, do you remember a parcel arriving for Mr.\
Lawrence Cavendish from Parkson\'92s?\'94\
\
Dorcas shook her head.\
\
\'93I don\'92t remember, sir. It may have done, but Mr. Lawrence was away\
from home part of June.\'94\
\
\'93In the event of a parcel arriving for him whilst he was away, what\
would be done with it?\'94\
\
\'93It would either be put in his room or sent on after him.\'94\
\
\'93By you?\'94\
\
\'93No, sir, I should leave it on the hall table. It would be Miss Howard\
who would attend to anything like that.\'94\
\
Evelyn Howard was called and, after being examined on other points, was\
questioned as to the parcel.\
\
\'93Don\'92t remember. Lots of parcels come. Can\'92t remember one special one.\'94\
\
\'93You do not know if it was sent after Mr. Lawrence Cavendish to Wales,\
or whether it was put in his room?\'94\
\
\'93Don\'92t think it was sent after him. Should have remembered it if it\
was.\'94\
\
\'93Supposing a parcel arrived addressed to Mr. Lawrence Cavendish, and\
afterwards it disappeared, should you remark its absence?\'94\
\
\'93No, don\'92t think so. I should think someone had taken charge of it.\'94\
\
\'93I believe, Miss Howard, that it was you who found this sheet of brown\
paper?\'94 He held up the same dusty piece which Poirot and I had examined\
in the morning-room at Styles.\
\
\'93Yes, I did.\'94\
\
\'93How did you come to look for it?\'94\
\
\'93The Belgian detective who was employed on the case asked me to search\
for it.\'94\
\
\'93Where did you eventually discover it?\'94\
\
\'93On the top of\'97of\'97a wardrobe.\'94\
\
\'93On top of the prisoner\'92s wardrobe?\'94\
\
\'93I\'97I believe so.\'94\
\
\'93Did you not find it yourself?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93Then you must know where you found it?\'94\
\
\'93Yes, it was on the prisoner\'92s wardrobe.\'94\
\
\'93That is better.\'94\
\
An assistant from Parkson\'92s, Theatrical Costumiers, testified that on\
June 29th, they had supplied a black beard to Mr. L. Cavendish, as\
requested. It was ordered by letter, and a postal order was enclosed.\
No, they had not kept the letter. All transactions were entered in\
their books. They had sent the beard, as directed, to \'93L. Cavendish,\
Esq., Styles Court.\'94\
\
Sir Ernest Heavywether rose ponderously.\
\
\'93Where was the letter written from?\'94\
\
\'93From Styles Court.\'94\
\
\'93The same address to which you sent the parcel?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93And the letter came from there?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
Like a beast of prey, Heavywether fell upon him:\
\
\'93How do you know?\'94\
\
\'93I\'97I don\'92t understand.\'94\
\
\'93How do you know that letter came from Styles? Did you notice the\
postmark?\'94\
\
\'93No\'97but\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93Ah, you did _not_ notice the postmark! And yet you affirm so\
confidently that it came from Styles. It might, in fact, have been any\
postmark?\'94\
\
\'93Y\'97es.\'94\
\
\'93In fact, the letter, though written on stamped notepaper, might have\
been posted from anywhere? From Wales, for instance?\'94\
\
The witness admitted that such might be the case, and Sir Ernest\
signified that he was satisfied.\
\
Elizabeth Wells, second housemaid at Styles, stated that after she had\
gone to bed she remembered that she had bolted the front door, instead\
of leaving it on the latch as Mr. Inglethorp had requested. She had\
accordingly gone downstairs again to rectify her error. Hearing a\
slight noise in the West wing, she had peeped along the passage, and\
had seen Mr. John Cavendish knocking at Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s door.\
\
Sir Ernest Heavywether made short work of her, and under his unmerciful\
bullying she contradicted herself hopelessly, and Sir Ernest sat down\
again with a satisfied smile on his face.\
\
With the evidence of Annie, as to the candle grease on the floor, and\
as to seeing the prisoner take the coffee into the boudoir, the\
proceedings were adjourned until the following day.\
\
As we went home, Mary Cavendish spoke bitterly against the prosecuting\
counsel.\
\
\'93That hateful man! What a net he has drawn around my poor John! How he\
twisted every little fact until he made it seem what it wasn\'92t!\'94\
\
\'93Well,\'94 I said consolingly, \'93it will be the other way about to-morrow.\'94\
\
\'93Yes,\'94 she said meditatively; then suddenly dropped her voice. \'93Mr.\
Hastings, you do not think\'97surely it could not have been Lawrence\'97Oh,\
no, that could not be!\'94\
\
But I myself was puzzled, and as soon as I was alone with Poirot I\
asked him what he thought Sir Ernest was driving at.\
\
\'93Ah!\'94 said Poirot appreciatively. \'93He is a clever man, that Sir\
Ernest.\'94\
\
\'93Do you think he believes Lawrence guilty?\'94\
\
\'93I do not think he believes or cares anything! No, what he is trying\
for is to create such confusion in the minds of the jury that they are\
divided in their opinion as to which brother did it. He is endeavouring\
to make out that there is quite as much evidence against Lawrence as\
against John\'97and I am not at all sure that he will not succeed.\'94\
\
Detective-inspector Japp was the first witness called when the trial\
was reopened, and gave his evidence succinctly and briefly. After\
relating the earlier events, he proceeded:\
\
\'93Acting on information received, Superintendent Summerhaye and myself\
searched the prisoner\'92s room, during his temporary absence from the\
house. In his chest of drawers, hidden beneath some underclothing, we\
found: first, a pair of gold-rimmed pince-nez similar to those worn by\
Mr. Inglethorp\'94\'97these were exhibited\'97\'93secondly, this phial.\'94\
\
The phial was that already recognized by the chemist\'92s assistant, a\
tiny bottle of blue glass, containing a few grains of a white\
crystalline powder, and labelled: \'93Strychnine Hydro-chloride. POISON.\'94\
\
A fresh piece of evidence discovered by the detectives since the police\
court proceedings was a long, almost new piece of blotting-paper. It\
had been found in Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s cheque book, and on being reversed\
at a mirror, showed clearly the words: \'93. . . erything of which I die\
possessed I leave to my beloved husband Alfred Ing...\'94 This placed\
beyond question the fact that the destroyed will had been in favour of\
the deceased lady\'92s husband. Japp then produced the charred fragment of\
paper recovered from the grate, and this, with the discovery of the\
beard in the attic, completed his evidence.\
\
But Sir Ernest\'92s cross-examination was yet to come.\
\
\'93What day was it when you searched the prisoner\'92s room?\'94\
\
\'93Tuesday, the 24th of July.\'94\
\
\'93Exactly a week after the tragedy?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93You found these two objects, you say, in the chest of drawers. Was the\
drawer unlocked?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93Does it not strike you as unlikely that a man who had committed a\
crime should keep the evidence of it in an unlocked drawer for anyone\
to find?\'94\
\
\'93He might have stowed them there in a hurry.\'94\
\
\'93But you have just said it was a whole week since the crime. He would\
have had ample time to remove them and destroy them.\'94\
\
\'93Perhaps.\'94\
\
\'93There is no perhaps about it. Would he, or would he not have had\
plenty of time to remove and destroy them?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93Was the pile of underclothes under which the things were hidden heavy\
or light?\'94\
\
\'93Heavyish.\'94\
\
\'93In other words, it was winter underclothing. Obviously, the prisoner\
would not be likely to go to that drawer?\'94\
\
\'93Perhaps not.\'94\
\
\'93Kindly answer my question. Would the prisoner, in the hottest week of\
a hot summer, be likely to go to a drawer containing winter\
underclothing. Yes, or no?\'94\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
\'93In that case, is it not possible that the articles in question might\
have been put there by a third person, and that the prisoner was quite\
unaware of their presence?\'94\
\
\'93I should not think it likely.\'94\
\
\'93But it is possible?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93That is all.\'94\
\
More evidence followed. Evidence as to the financial difficulties in\
which the prisoner had found himself at the end of July. Evidence as to\
his intrigue with Mrs. Raikes\'97poor Mary, that must have been bitter\
hearing for a woman of her pride. Evelyn Howard had been right in her\
facts, though her animosity against Alfred Inglethorp had caused her to\
jump to the conclusion that he was the person concerned.\
\
Lawrence Cavendish was then put into the box. In a low voice, in answer\
to Mr. Philips\'92 questions, he denied having ordered anything from\
Parkson\'92s in June. In fact, on June 29th, he had been staying away, in\
Wales.\
\
Instantly, Sir Ernest\'92s chin was shooting pugnaciously forward.\
\
\'93You deny having ordered a black beard from Parkson\'92s on June 29th?\'94\
\
\'93I do.\'94\
\
\'93Ah! In the event of anything happening to your brother, who will\
inherit Styles Court?\'94\
\
The brutality of the question called a flush to Lawrence\'92s pale face.\
The judge gave vent to a faint murmur of disapprobation, and the\
prisoner in the dock leant forward angrily.\
\
Heavywether cared nothing for his client\'92s anger.\
\
\'93Answer my question, if you please.\'94\
\
\'93I suppose,\'94 said Lawrence quietly, \'93that I should.\'94\
\
\'93What do you mean by you \'91suppose\'92? Your brother has no children. You\
_would_ inherit it, wouldn\'92t you?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93Ah, that\'92s better,\'94 said Heavywether, with ferocious geniality. \'93And\
you\'92d inherit a good slice of money too, wouldn\'92t you?\'94\
\
\'93Really, Sir Ernest,\'94 protested the judge, \'93these questions are not\
relevant.\'94\
\
Sir Ernest bowed, and having shot his arrow proceeded.\
\
\'93On Tuesday, the 17th July, you went, I believe, with another guest, to\
visit the dispensary at the Red Cross Hospital in Tadminster?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
\'93Did you\'97while you happened to be alone for a few seconds\'97unlock the\
poison cupboard, and examine some of the bottles?\'94\
\
\'93I\'97I\'97may have done so.\'94\
\
\'93I put it to you that you did do so?\'94\
\
\'93Yes.\'94\
\
Sir Ernest fairly shot the next question at him.\
\
\'93Did you examine one bottle in particular?\'94\
\
\'93No, I do not think so.\'94\
\
\'93Be careful, Mr. Cavendish. I am referring to a little bottle of\
Hydro-chloride of Strychnine.\'94\
\
Lawrence was turning a sickly greenish colour.\
\
\'93N\'97o\'97I am sure I didn\'92t.\'94\
\
\'93Then how do you account for the fact that you left the unmistakable\
impress of your finger-prints on it?\'94\
\
The bullying manner was highly efficacious with a nervous disposition.\
\
\'93I\'97I suppose I must have taken up the bottle.\'94\
\
\'93I suppose so too! Did you abstract any of the contents of the bottle?\'94\
\
\'93Certainly not.\'94\
\
\'93Then why did you take it up?\'94\
\
\'93I once studied to be a doctor. Such things naturally interest me.\'94\
\
\'93Ah! So poisons \'91naturally interest\'92 you, do they? Still, you waited to\
be alone before gratifying that \'91interest\'92 of yours?\'94\
\
\'93That was pure chance. If the others had been there, I should have done\
just the same.\'94\
\
\'93Still, as it happens, the others were not there?\'94\
\
\'93No, but\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93In fact, during the whole afternoon, you were only alone for a couple\
of minutes, and it happened\'97I say, it happened\'97to be during those two\
minutes that you displayed your \'91natural interest\'92 in Hydro-chloride of\
Strychnine?\'94\
\
Lawrence stammered pitiably.\
\
\'93I\'97I\'97\'97\'94\
\
With a satisfied and expressive countenance, Sir Ernest observed:\
\
\'93I have nothing more to ask you, Mr. Cavendish.\'94\
\
This bit of cross-examination had caused great excitement in court. The\
heads of the many fashionably attired women present were busily laid\
together, and their whispers became so loud that the judge angrily\
threatened to have the court cleared if there was not immediate\
silence.\
\
There was little more evidence. The hand-writing experts were called\
upon for their opinion of the signature of \'93Alfred Inglethorp\'94 in the\
chemist\'92s poison register. They all declared unanimously that it was\
certainly not his hand-writing, and gave it as their view that it might\
be that of the prisoner disguised. Cross-examined, they admitted that\
it might be the prisoner\'92s hand-writing cleverly counterfeited.\
\
Sir Ernest Heavywether\'92s speech in opening the case for the defence was\
not a long one, but it was backed by the full force of his emphatic\
manner. Never, he said, in the course of his long experience, had he\
known a charge of murder rest on slighter evidence. Not only was it\
entirely circumstantial, but the greater part of it was practically\
unproved. Let them take the testimony they had heard and sift it\
impartially. The strychnine had been found in a drawer in the\
prisoner\'92s room. That drawer was an unlocked one, as he had pointed\
out, and he submitted that there was no evidence to prove that it was\
the prisoner who had concealed the poison there. It was, in fact, a\
wicked and malicious attempt on the part of some third person to fix\
the crime on the prisoner. The prosecution had been unable to produce a\
shred of evidence in support of their contention that it was the\
prisoner who ordered the black beard from Parkson\'92s. The quarrel which\
had taken place between prisoner and his stepmother was freely\
admitted, but both it and his financial embarrassments had been grossly\
exaggerated.\
\
His learned friend\'97Sir Ernest nodded carelessly at Mr. Philips\'97had\
stated that if the prisoner were an innocent man, he would have come\
forward at the inquest to explain that it was he, and not Mr.\
Inglethorp, who had been the participator in the quarrel. He thought\
the facts had been misrepresented. What had actually occurred was this.\
The prisoner, returning to the house on Tuesday evening, had been\
authoritatively told that there had been a violent quarrel between Mr.\
and Mrs. Inglethorp. No suspicion had entered the prisoner\'92s head that\
anyone could possibly have mistaken his voice for that of Mr.\
Inglethorp. He naturally concluded that his stepmother had had two\
quarrels.\
\
The prosecution averred that on Monday, July 16th, the prisoner had\
entered the chemist\'92s shop in the village, disguised as Mr. Inglethorp.\
The prisoner, on the contrary, was at that time at a lonely spot called\
Marston\'92s Spinney, where he had been summoned by an anonymous note,\
couched in blackmailing terms, and threatening to reveal certain\
matters to his wife unless he complied with its demands. The prisoner\
had, accordingly, gone to the appointed spot, and after waiting there\
vainly for half an hour had returned home. Unfortunately, he had met\
with no one on the way there or back who could vouch for the truth of\
his story, but luckily he had kept the note, and it would be produced\
as evidence.\
\
As for the statement relating to the destruction of the will, the\
prisoner had formerly practised at the Bar, and was perfectly well\
aware that the will made in his favour a year before was automatically\
revoked by his stepmother\'92s remarriage. He would call evidence to show\
who did destroy the will, and it was possible that that might open up\
quite a new view of the case.\
\
Finally, he would point out to the jury that there was evidence against\
other people besides John Cavendish. He would direct their attention to\
the fact that the evidence against Mr. Lawrence Cavendish was quite as\
strong, if not stronger than that against his brother.\
\
He would now call the prisoner.\
\
John acquitted himself well in the witness-box. Under Sir Ernest\'92s\
skilful handling, he told his tale credibly and well. The anonymous\
note received by him was produced, and handed to the jury to examine.\
The readiness with which he admitted his financial difficulties, and\
the disagreement with his stepmother, lent value to his denials.\
\
At the close of his examination, he paused, and said:\
\
\'93I should like to make one thing clear. I utterly reject and disapprove\
of Sir Ernest Heavywether\'92s insinuations against my brother. My\
brother, I am convinced, had no more to do with the crime than I have.\'94\
\
Sir Ernest merely smiled, and noted with a sharp eye that John\'92s\
protest had produced a very favourable impression on the jury.\
\
Then the cross-examination began.\
\
\'93I understand you to say that it never entered your head that the\
witnesses at the inquest could possibly have mistaken your voice for\
that of Mr. Inglethorp. Is not that very surprising?\'94\
\
\'93No, I don\'92t think so. I was told there had been a quarrel between my\
mother and Mr. Inglethorp, and it never occurred to me that such was\
not really the case.\'94\
\
\'93Not when the servant Dorcas repeated certain fragments of the\
conversation\'97fragments which you must have recognized?\'94\
\
\'93I did not recognize them.\'94\
\
\'93Your memory must be unusually short!\'94\
\
\'93No, but we were both angry, and, I think, said more than we meant. I\
paid very little attention to my mother\'92s actual words.\'94\
\
Mr. Philips\'92 incredulous sniff was a triumph of forensic skill. He\
passed on to the subject of the note.\
\
\'93You have produced this note very opportunely. Tell me, is there\
nothing familiar about the hand-writing of it?\'94\
\
\'93Not that I know of.\'94\
\
\'93Do you not think that it bears a marked resemblance to your own\
hand-writing\'97carelessly disguised?\'94\
\
\'93No, I do not think so.\'94\
\
\'93I put it to you that it is your own hand-writing!\'94\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
\'93I put it to you that, anxious to prove an alibi, you conceived the\
idea of a fictitious and rather incredible appointment, and wrote this\
note yourself in order to bear out your statement!\'94\
\
\'93No.\'94\
\
\'93Is it not a fact that, at the time you claim to have been waiting\
about at a solitary and unfrequented spot, you were really in the\
chemist\'92s shop in Styles St. Mary, where you purchased strychnine in\
the name of Alfred Inglethorp?\'94\
\
\'93No, that is a lie.\'94\
\
\'93I put it to you that, wearing a suit of Mr. Inglethorp\'92s clothes, with\
a black beard trimmed to resemble his, you were there\'97and signed the\
register in his name!\'94\
\
\'93That is absolutely untrue.\'94\
\
\'93Then I will leave the remarkable similarity of hand-writing between\
the note, the register, and your own, to the consideration of the\
jury,\'94 said Mr. Philips, and sat down with the air of a man who has\
done his duty, but who was nevertheless horrified by such deliberate\
perjury.\
\
After this, as it was growing late, the case was adjourned till Monday.\
\
Poirot, I noticed, was looking profoundly discouraged. He had that\
little frown between the eyes that I knew so well.\
\
\'93What is it, Poirot?\'94 I inquired.\
\
\'93Ah, _mon ami_, things are going badly, badly.\'94\
\
In spite of myself, my heart gave a leap of relief. Evidently there was\
a likelihood of John Cavendish being acquitted.\
\
When we reached the house, my little friend waved aside Mary\'92s offer of\
tea.\
\
\'93No, I thank you, madame. I will mount to my room.\'94\
\
I followed him. Still frowning, he went across to the desk and took out\
a small pack of patience cards. Then he drew up a chair to the table,\
and, to my utter amazement, began solemnly to build card houses!\
\
My jaw dropped involuntarily, and he said at once:\
\
\'93No, _mon ami_, I am not in my second childhood! I steady my nerves,\
that is all. This employment requires precision of the fingers. With\
precision of the fingers goes precision of the brain. And never have I\
needed that more than now!\'94\
\
\'93What is the trouble?\'94 I asked.\
\
With a great thump on the table, Poirot demolished his carefully built\
up edifice.\
\
\'93It is this, _mon ami!_ That I can build card houses seven stories\
high, but I cannot\'94\'97thump\'97\'93find\'94\'97thump\'97\'93 that last link of which I\
spoke to you.\'94\
\
I could not quite tell what to say, so I held my peace, and he began\
slowly building up the cards again, speaking in jerks as he did so.\
\
\'93It is done\'97so! By placing\'97one card\'97on another\'97with\
mathematical\'97precision!\'94\
\
I watched the card house rising under his hands, story by story. He\
never hesitated or faltered. It was really almost like a conjuring\
trick.\
\
\'93What a steady hand you\'92ve got,\'94 I remarked. \'93I believe I\'92ve only seen\
your hand shake once.\'94\
\
\'93On an occasion when I was enraged, without doubt,\'94 observed Poirot,\
with great placidity.\
\
\'93Yes indeed! You were in a towering rage. Do you remember? It was when\
you discovered that the lock of the despatch-case in Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s\
bedroom had been forced. You stood by the mantelpiece, twiddling the\
things on it in your usual fashion, and your hand shook like a leaf! I\
must say\'97\'97\'94\
\
But I stopped suddenly. For Poirot, uttering a hoarse and inarticulate\
cry, again annihilated his masterpiece of cards, and putting his hands\
over his eyes swayed backwards and forwards, apparently suffering the\
keenest agony.\
\
\'93Good heavens, Poirot!\'94 I cried. \'93What is the matter? Are you taken\
ill?\'94\
\
\'93No, no,\'94 he gasped. \'93It is\'97it is\'97that I have an idea!\'94\
\
\'93Oh!\'94 I exclaimed, much relieved. \'93One of your \'91little ideas\'92?\'94\
\
\'93Ah, _ma foi_, no!\'94 replied Poirot frankly. \'93This time it is an idea\
gigantic! Stupendous! And you\'97_you_, my friend, have given it to me!\'94\
\
Suddenly clasping me in his arms, he kissed me warmly on both cheeks,\
and before I had recovered from my surprise ran headlong from the room.\
\
Mary Cavendish entered at that moment.\
\
\'93What _is_ the matter with Monsieur Poirot? He rushed past me crying\
out: \'91A garage! For the love of Heaven, direct me to a garage, madame!\'92\
And, before I could answer, he had dashed out into the street.\'94\
\
I hurried to the window. True enough, there he was, tearing down the\
street, hatless, and gesticulating as he went. I turned to Mary with a\
gesture of despair.\
\
\'93He\'92ll be stopped by a policeman in another minute. There he goes,\
round the corner!\'94\
\
Our eyes met, and we stared helplessly at one another.\
\
\'93What can be the matter?\'94\
\
I shook my head.\
\
\'93I don\'92t know. He was building card houses, when suddenly he said he\
had an idea, and rushed off as you saw.\'94\
\
\'93Well,\'94 said Mary, \'93I expect he will be back before dinner.\'94\
\
But night fell, and Poirot had not returned.\
\
\
\
\
\
\
CHAPTER XII. THE LAST LINK\
\
Poirot\'92s abrupt departure had intrigued us all greatly. Sunday morning\
wore away, and still he did not reappear. But about three o\'92clock a\
ferocious and prolonged hooting outside drove us to the window, to see\
Poirot alighting from a car, accompanied by Japp and Summerhaye. The\
little man was transformed. He radiated an absurd complacency. He bowed\
with exaggerated respect to Mary Cavendish.\
\
\'93Madame, I have your permission to hold a little _r\'e9union_ in the\
_salon_? It is necessary for everyone to attend.\'94\
\
Mary smiled sadly.\
\
\'93You know, Monsieur Poirot, that you have _carte blanche_ in every\
way.\'94\
\
\'93You are too amiable, madame.\'94\
\
Still beaming, Poirot marshalled us all into the drawing-room, bringing\
forward chairs as he did so.\
\
\'93Miss Howard\'97here. Mademoiselle Cynthia. Monsieur Lawrence. The good\
Dorcas. And Annie. _Bien!_ We must delay our proceedings a few minutes\
until Mr. Inglethorp arrives. I have sent him a note.\'94\
\
Miss Howard rose immediately from her seat.\
\
\'93If that man comes into the house, I leave it!\'94\
\
\'93No, no!\'94 Poirot went up to her and pleaded in a low voice.\
\
Finally Miss Howard consented to return to her chair. A few minutes\
later Alfred Inglethorp entered the room.\
\
The company once assembled, Poirot rose from his seat with the air of a\
popular lecturer, and bowed politely to his audience.\
\
\'93_Messieurs, mesdames_, as you all know, I was called in by Monsieur\
John Cavendish to investigate this case. I at once examined the bedroom\
of the deceased which, by the advice of the doctors, had been kept\
locked, and was consequently exactly as it had been when the tragedy\
occurred. I found: first, a fragment of green material; second, a stain\
on the carpet near the window, still damp; thirdly, an empty box of\
bromide powders.\
\
\'93To take the fragment of green material first, I found it caught in the\
bolt of the communicating door between that room and the adjoining one\
occupied by Mademoiselle Cynthia. I handed the fragment over to the\
police who did not consider it of much importance. Nor did they\
recognize it for what it was\'97a piece torn from a green land armlet.\'94\
\
There was a little stir of excitement.\
\
\'93Now there was only one person at Styles who worked on the land\'97Mrs.\
Cavendish. Therefore it must have been Mrs. Cavendish who entered the\
deceased\'92s room through the door communicating with Mademoiselle\
Cynthia\'92s room.\'94\
\
\'93But that door was bolted on the inside!\'94 I cried.\
\
\'93When I examined the room, yes. But in the first place we have only her\
word for it, since it was she who tried that particular door and\
reported it fastened. In the ensuing confusion she would have had ample\
opportunity to shoot the bolt across. I took an early opportunity of\
verifying my conjectures. To begin with, the fragment corresponds\
exactly with a tear in Mrs. Cavendish\'92s armlet. Also, at the inquest,\
Mrs. Cavendish declared that she had heard, from her own room, the fall\
of the table by the bed. I took an early opportunity of testing that\
statement by stationing my friend Monsieur Hastings in the left wing of\
the building, just outside Mrs. Cavendish\'92s door. I myself, in company\
with the police, went to the deceased\'92s room, and whilst there I,\
apparently accidentally, knocked over the table in question, but found\
that, as I had expected, Monsieur Hastings had heard no sound at all.\
This confirmed my belief that Mrs. Cavendish was not speaking the truth\
when she declared that she had been dressing in her room at the time of\
the tragedy. In fact, I was convinced that, far from having been in her\
own room, Mrs. Cavendish was actually in the deceased\'92s room when the\
alarm was given.\'94\
\
I shot a quick glance at Mary. She was very pale, but smiling.\
\
\'93I proceeded to reason on that assumption. Mrs. Cavendish is in her\
mother-in-law\'92s room. We will say that she is seeking for something and\
has not yet found it. Suddenly Mrs. Inglethorp awakens and is seized\
with an alarming paroxysm. She flings out her arm, overturning the bed\
table, and then pulls desperately at the bell. Mrs. Cavendish,\
startled, drops her candle, scattering the grease on the carpet. She\
picks it up, and retreats quickly to Mademoiselle Cynthia\'92s room,\
closing the door behind her. She hurries out into the passage, for the\
servants must not find her where she is. But it is too late! Already\
footsteps are echoing along the gallery which connects the two wings.\
What can she do? Quick as thought, she hurries back to the young girl\'92s\
room, and starts shaking her awake. The hastily aroused household come\
trooping down the passage. They are all busily battering at Mrs.\
Inglethorp\'92s door. It occurs to nobody that Mrs. Cavendish has not\
arrived with the rest, but\'97and this is significant\'97I can find no one\
who saw her come from the other wing.\'94 He looked at Mary Cavendish. \'93Am\
I right, madame?\'94\
\
She bowed her head.\
\
\'93Quite right, monsieur. You understand that, if I had thought I would\
do my husband any good by revealing these facts, I would have done so.\
But it did not seem to me to bear upon the question of his guilt or\
innocence.\'94\
\
\'93In a sense, that is correct, madame. But it cleared my mind of many\
misconceptions, and left me free to see other facts in their true\
significance.\'94\
\
\'93The will!\'94 cried Lawrence. \'93Then it was you, Mary, who destroyed the\
will?\'94\
\
She shook her head, and Poirot shook his also.\
\
\'93No,\'94 he said quietly. \'93There is only one person who could possibly\
have destroyed that will\'97Mrs. Inglethorp herself!\'94\
\
\'93Impossible!\'94 I exclaimed. \'93She had only made it out that very\
afternoon!\'94\
\
\'93Nevertheless, _mon ami_, it was Mrs. Inglethorp. Because, in no other\
way can you account for the fact that, on one of the hottest days of\
the year, Mrs. Inglethorp ordered a fire to be lighted in her room.\'94\
\
I gave a gasp. What idiots we had been never to think of that fire as\
being incongruous! Poirot was continuing:\
\
\'93The temperature on that day, messieurs, was 80 degrees in the shade.\
Yet Mrs. Inglethorp ordered a fire! Why? Because she wished to destroy\
something, and could think of no other way. You will remember that, in\
consequence of the War economics practiced at Styles, no waste paper\
was thrown away. There was therefore no means of destroying a thick\
document such as a will. The moment I heard of a fire being lighted in\
Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s room, I leaped to the conclusion that it was to\
destroy some important document\'97possibly a will. So the discovery of\
the charred fragment in the grate was no surprise to me. I did not, of\
course, know at the time that the will in question had only been made\
this afternoon, and I will admit that, when I learnt that fact, I fell\
into a grievous error. I came to the conclusion that Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s\
determination to destroy her will arose as a direct consequence of the\
quarrel she had that afternoon, and that therefore the quarrel took\
place after, and not before the making of the will.\
\
\'93Here, as we know, I was wrong, and I was forced to abandon that idea.\
I faced the problem from a new standpoint. Now, at four o\'92clock, Dorcas\
overheard her mistress saying angrily: \'91You need not think that any\
fear of publicity, or scandal between husband and wife will deter me.\'94\
I conjectured, and conjectured rightly, that these words were\
addressed, not to her husband, but to Mr. John Cavendish. At five\
o\'92clock, an hour later, she uses almost the same words, but the\
standpoint is different. She admits to Dorcas, \'91I don\'92t know what to\
do; scandal between husband and wife is a dreadful thing.\'92 At four\
o\'92clock she has been angry, but completely mistress of herself. At five\
o\'92clock she is in violent distress, and speaks of having had a great\
shock.\
\
\'93Looking at the matter psychologically, I drew one deduction which I\
was convinced was correct. The second \'91scandal\'92 she spoke of was not\
the same as the first\'97and it concerned herself!\
\
\'93Let us reconstruct. At four o\'92clock, Mrs. Inglethorp quarrels with her\
son, and threatens to denounce him to his wife\'97who, by the way,\
overheard the greater part of the conversation. At four-thirty, Mrs.\
Inglethorp, in consequence of a conversation on the validity of wills,\
makes a will in favour of her husband, which the two gardeners witness.\
At five o\'92clock, Dorcas finds her mistress in a state of considerable\
agitation, with a slip of paper\'97\'91a letter,\'92 Dorcas thinks\'97in her hand,\
and it is then that she orders the fire in her room to be lighted.\
Presumably, then, between four-thirty and five o\'92clock, something has\
occurred to occasion a complete revolution of feeling, since she is now\
as anxious to destroy the will, as she was before to make it. What was\
that something?\
\
\'93As far as we know, she was quite alone during that half-hour. Nobody\
entered or left that boudoir. What then occasioned this sudden change\
of sentiment?\
\
\'93One can only guess, but I believe my guess to be correct. Mrs.\
Inglethorp had no stamps in her desk. We know this, because later she\
asked Dorcas to bring her some. Now in the opposite corner of the room\
stood her husband\'92s desk\'97locked. She was anxious to find some stamps,\
and, according to my theory, she tried her own keys in the desk. That\
one of them fitted I know. She therefore opened the desk, and in\
searching for the stamps she came across something else\'97that slip of\
paper which Dorcas saw in her hand, and which assuredly was never meant\
for Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s eyes. On the other hand, Mrs. Cavendish believed\
that the slip of paper to which her mother-in-law clung so tenaciously\
was a written proof of her own husband\'92s infidelity. She demanded it\
from Mrs. Inglethorp who assured her, quite truly, that it had nothing\
to do with that matter. Mrs. Cavendish did not believe her. She thought\
that Mrs. Inglethorp was shielding her stepson. Now Mrs. Cavendish is a\
very resolute woman, and, behind her mask of reserve, she was madly\
jealous of her husband. She determined to get hold of that paper at all\
costs, and in this resolution chance came to her aid. She happened to\
pick up the key of Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s despatch-case, which had been lost\
that morning. She knew that her mother-in-law invariably kept all\
important papers in this particular case.\
\
\'93Mrs. Cavendish, therefore, made her plans as only a woman driven\
desperate through jealousy could have done. Some time in the evening\
she unbolted the door leading into Mademoiselle Cynthia\'92s room.\
Possibly she applied oil to the hinges, for I found that it opened\
quite noiselessly when I tried it. She put off her project until the\
early hours of the morning as being safer, since the servants were\
accustomed to hearing her move about her room at that time. She dressed\
completely in her land kit, and made her way quietly through\
Mademoiselle Cynthia\'92s room into that of Mrs. Inglethorp.\'94\
\
He paused a moment, and Cynthia interrupted:\
\
\'93But I should have woken up if anyone had come through my room?\'94\
\
\'93Not if you were drugged, mademoiselle.\'94\
\
\'93Drugged?\'94\
\
\'93_Mais, oui!_\'94\
\
\'93You remember\'94\'97he addressed us collectively again\'97\'93that through all the\
tumult and noise next door Mademoiselle Cynthia slept. That admitted of\
two possibilities. Either her sleep was feigned\'97which I did not\
believe\'97or her unconsciousness was indeed by artificial means.\
\
\'93With this latter idea in my mind, I examined all the coffee-cups most\
carefully, remembering that it was Mrs. Cavendish who had brought\
Mademoiselle Cynthia her coffee the night before. I took a sample from\
each cup, and had them analysed\'97with no result. I had counted the cups\
carefully, in the event of one having been removed. Six persons had\
taken coffee, and six cups were duly found. I had to confess myself\
mistaken.\
\
\'93Then I discovered that I had been guilty of a very grave oversight.\
Coffee had been brought in for seven persons, not six, for Dr.\
Bauerstein had been there that evening. This changed the face of the\
whole affair, for there was now one cup missing. The servants noticed\
nothing, since Annie, the housemaid, who took in the coffee, brought in\
seven cups, not knowing that Mr. Inglethorp never drank it, whereas\
Dorcas, who cleared them away the following morning, found six as\
usual\'97or strictly speaking she found five, the sixth being the one\
found broken in Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s room.\
\
\'93I was confident that the missing cup was that of Mademoiselle Cynthia.\
I had an additional reason for that belief in the fact that all the\
cups found contained sugar, which Mademoiselle Cynthia never took in\
her coffee. My attention was attracted by the story of Annie about some\
\'91salt\'92 on the tray of cocoa which she took every night to Mrs.\
Inglethorp\'92s room. I accordingly secured a sample of that cocoa, and\
sent it to be analysed.\'94\
\
\'93But that had already been done by Dr. Bauerstein,\'94 said Lawrence\
quickly.\
\
\'93Not exactly. The analyst was asked by him to report whether strychnine\
was, or was not, present. He did not have it tested, as I did, for a\
narcotic.\'94\
\
\'93For a narcotic?\'94\
\
\'93Yes. Here is the analyst\'92s report. Mrs. Cavendish administered a safe,\
but effectual, narcotic to both Mrs. Inglethorp and Mademoiselle\
Cynthia. And it is possible that she had a _mauvais quart d\'92heure_ in\
consequence! Imagine her feelings when her mother-in-law is suddenly\
taken ill and dies, and immediately after she hears the word \'91Poison\'92!\
She has believed that the sleeping draught she administered was\
perfectly harmless, but there is no doubt that for one terrible moment\
she must have feared that Mrs. Inglethorp\'92s death lay at her door. She\
is seized with panic, and under its influence she hurries downstairs,\
and quickly drops the coffee-cup and saucer used by Mademoiselle\
Cynthia into a large brass vase, where it is discovered later by\
Monsieur Lawrence. The remains of the cocoa she dare not touch. Too\
many eyes are upon her. Guess at her relief when strychnine is\
mentioned, and she discovers that after all the tragedy is not her\
doing.\
\
\'93We are now able to account for the symptoms of strychnine poisoning\
being so long in making their appearance. A narcotic taken with\
strychnine will delay the action of the poison for some hours.\'94\
\
Poirot paused. Mary looked up at him, the colour slowly rising in her\
face.\
\
\'93All you have said is quite true, Monsieur Poirot. It was the most\
awful hour of my life. I shall never forget it. But you are wonderful.\
I understand now\'97\'97\'94\
\
\'93What I meant when I told you that you could safely confess to Papa\
Poirot, eh? But you would not trust me.\'94\
\
\'93I see everything now,\'94 said Lawrence. \'93The drugged cocoa, taken on top\
of the poisoned coffee, amply accounts for the delay.\'94\
\
\'93Exactly. But was the coffee poisoned, or was it not? We come to a\
little difficulty here, since Mrs. Inglethorp never drank it.\'94\
\
\'93What?\'94 The cry of surprise was universal.\
\
\'93No. You will remember my speaking of a stain on the carpet in Mrs.\
Inglethorp\'92s room? There were some peculiar points about that stain. It\
was still damp, it exhaled a strong odour of coffee, and imbedded in\
the nap of the carpet I found some little splinters of china. What had\
happened was plain to me, for not two minutes before I had placed my\
little case on the table near the window, and the table, tilting up,\
had deposited it upon the floor on precisely the identical spot. In\
exactly the same way, Mrs. Inglethorp had laid down her cup of coffee\
on reaching her room the night before, and the treacherous table had\
played her the same trick.\
\
\'93What happened next is mere guess work on my part, but I should say\
that Mrs. Inglethorp picked up the broken cup and placed it on the\
table by the bed. Feeling in need of a stimulant of some kind, she\
heated up her cocoa, and drank it off then and there. Now we are faced\
with a new problem. We know the cocoa contained no strychnine. The\
coffee was never drunk. Yet the strychnine must have been administered\
between seven and nine o\'92clock that evening. What third medium was\
there\'97a medium so suitable for disguising the taste of strychnine that\
it is extraordinary no one has thought of it?\'94 Poirot looked round the\
room, and then answered himself impressively. \'93Her medicine!\'94\
\
\'93Do you mean that the murderer introduced the strychnine into her\
tonic?\'94 I cried.\
\
\'93There was no need to introduce it. It was already there\'97in the\
mixture. The strychnine that killed Mrs. Inglethorp was the identical\
strychnine prescribed by Dr. Wilkins. To make that clear to you, I will\
read you an extract from a book on dispensing which I found in the\
Dispensary of the Red Cross Hospital at Tadminster:\
\
\'93\'91The following prescription has become famous in text books:\
\
\
          Strychninae Sulph. . . . . . 1 gr.\
\
          Potass Bromide . . . . . . . 3vi\
\
          Aqua ad. . . . . . . . . . . . . 3viii\
\
          Fiat Mistura\
\
\
_This solution deposits in a few hours the greater part of the\
strychnine salt as an insoluble bromide in transparent crystals. A lady\
